Interpreting the Division of Praise in al-Tawdih
Commentary on the division of praise

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the six probabilities for the author's division of praise (awwalan wa thaniyan) and explain the Hanafi distinction between hamd (tongue-only praise) and shukr (comprehensive gratitude).

Core Idea
The author's 'first' and 'second' is not redundant; it either multiplies praise or expands its vehicle from the tongue to all worship.

In the opening of al-Tawdih, the author divides praise into 'first' and 'second', prompting a rich commentary exploring six possible meanings. This passage not only unpacks those probabilities but also addresses a potential objection of redundancy, resolving it through the principles of ta'kid (emphasis) and ta'mim (generalization). Along the way, it clarifies the crucial Hanafi distinction between hamd (praise with the tongue alone) and shukr (gratitude with heart, tongue, and limbs), a distinction that underpins the interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's division
The author of al-Tawdih begins by praising Allah 'first' (awwalan) and 'second' (thaniyan). Understand that this is not a simple repetition but a deliberate rhetorical device. The commentator explores what 'first' and 'second' could mean.
The division is the starting point for all six probabilities.
2
Enumerate the six probabilities
List each probability: (1) praise for essence/attributes vs. blessings; (2) praise for four divine actions (creation, preservation in this world and the next); (3) praise in this world vs. the Hereafter; (4) repetition/multiplication of praise; (5) praise for success in composing al-Tanqih vs. al-Tawdih; (6) praise at beginning vs. end of composition.
Each probability has Quranic support or logical basis.
3
Analyze the objection of redundancy
The objection: if all praise is covered in the first mention, why mention 'secondly'? Understand the two answers: (1) it is for multiplication (emphasis); (2) the first praise was general in object but specific in vehicle (tongue); the second generalizes the vehicle to all actions (heart, limbs, wealth).
The second answer relies on the distinction between hamd and shukr.
4
Trace the Hanafi distinction between hamd and shukr
From the passage: hamd is only with the tongue; shukr is with heart, tongue, and limbs. Favors require shukr, but the commentator uses this to show that praise can be extended beyond the tongue via metaphor when the phrase 'reins of praise' is used.
This distinction is fundamental in Hanafi usul for understanding worship.

Misk al-Malih – Commentary on al-Tawdih and al-Talwih.
First probability: 'First' refers to praise of Allah, of His essence free from defect and His great attributes, regardless of whether He is the Bestower, because in praise, it is not necessary that the one praised has bestowed favor, as in the definition of praise: 'whether for a blessing or otherwise.' 'Second' refers to praise of Allah for His great and abundant favors reaching His servants, especially for the favor of success in composing the book. All praise and gratitude to Allah.
Second probability: The favors of Allah, though countless and innumerable, return to four matters: (1) first creation (worldly), (2) first preservation (worldly), (3) second creation (otherworldly), (4) second preservation (otherworldly). So 'first' means praise for first creation and preservation, and 'second' means praise for second creation and preservation. Because in the Holy Quran, praise is for these matters. In Surat al-Fatiha, praise is for all matters: 'Lord of the worlds' indicates praise for first creation, since the nurturing of the worlds occurs after their creation; 'the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful' indicates first preservation, as both imply bestowal of great favors upon which the survival of humanity in this world depends; 'Master of the Day of Judgment' indicates second creation; and 'You alone we worship' indicates second preservation, because the fruit of worship and righteous deeds will be obtained in the Hereafter, resulting in attaining Paradise with eternity. Surat al-An'am indicates first creation: 'Praise be to Allah who created the heavens... He it is who created you from clay...' Surat al-Kahf indicates first preservation: 'Praise be to Allah who sent down to His servant the Book,' because the survival of humanity in this world depends on the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the word of Allah. Surat Saba indicates second creation: 'And those who disbelieve say, 'The Hour will not come to us...'' proving the resurrection of bodies and refuting deniers. Surat al-Mala'ika (Surat Fatir) indicates second preservation: 'Who makes the angels messengers...' the meeting of angels with the people of Paradise and their welcome indicates preservation in the Hereafter.
Third probability: The observation of His saying. Third probability: 'First' means praise in this world, and 'second' means praise in the Hereafter, as Allah says: 'Praise is in the first and the last.' The point is that Allah is deserving of praise in this world due to His perfect essence known by rational proofs, and due to the gifts that reach His servants; and in the Hereafter, Allah is deserving of praise due to the great favor of witnessing His majesty, and praise will be for such favors that no eye has seen, no ear has heard, and no heart has conceived. The verse 'And the last of their call will be 'Praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds'' indicates this praise in the Hereafter.
Fourth probability: 'First' and 'second' mean repetition, multiplication, and succession of praise, i.e., time after time.
Fifth probability: 'First' means praise for success in composing al-Tanqih, and 'second' means praise for success in composing al-Tawdih.
Sixth probability: 'First' means praise at the beginning of composition, and 'second' means praise upon completion and conclusion.

Misk al-Malih, p.54, Commentary on al-Tawdih and al-Talwih.
If you say: Praise has already been mentioned for Allah's majesty and blessings in the two abodes of perishment and permanence, so what is the meaning of the author's statement 'And the reins of praise to Him, secondly' (i.e., turning, as an adjunct to 'praising' and the construction is that 'secondly' is attached to 'praising')? I say: Its meaning is intending His glorification and the intention of drawing near to Him in everything suitable for that from words, actions, and spending wealth, indicating the types of acts of worship. For the favors of Allah require gratitude with the heart, tongue, and limbs, and praise is only with the tongue.
Translation: So if you say: Praise has already been mentioned for Allah's majesty and blessings in the abode of perishment and the abode of permanence, so what is the purpose of the author's statement 'And the reins of praise to Him, secondly'? (i.e., 'turning' explains the meaning of 'secondly', i.e., one who turns) attached to 'praising' – he explained the construction that 'secondly' is attached to 'praising'. I say: Its purpose is intending glorification of Allah and the intention of attaining nearness to Him in everything that has the capacity for that from words, states, and spending wealth, indicating the types of acts of worship. Because the favors of Allah necessitate gratitude with the heart, tongue, and limbs, and praise is only with the tongue.
Question: The origin of this question is the third reason. The summary of the question is: Since 'first' and 'second' refer to praise in this world and the Hereafter, and praise upon His perfection, majesty, and bestowal of blessings, then from all aspects, praise is established and attained for Allah. After that, mentioning 'and the reins of praise to Him, secondly' would be redundant, because no individual of praise remains for which the reins of praise are turned again to Allah, so this phrase would be useless.
Answer (1): The author's purpose is multiplication of praise, i.e., time after time, so this phrase becomes an emphasis for what preceded.
Answer (2): It is conceded that in the preceding sentence praise is general, but that generality is only with respect to the objects (related things), because blessings, essence, and attributes are all objects of praise. With respect to the vehicle (the means), praise is specific to the tongue, as clarified in the definition: 'praise is with the tongue.' However, the favors of Allah are so numerous that they require that praise of Allah not be specific only to the tongue, but also general in terms of the vehicle – tongue, heart, limbs, words, actions, and spending wealth. In short, whatever with which gratitude can be expressed, praise can be offered. For this reason, the second sentence was mentioned so that the vehicle becomes general, and a generalization after specification occurs with respect to the vehicle.
Question: Just as praise is specific to the tongue, praise (thana) is also specific to the tongue, so generalization after specification would not be achieved.
Answer: Although the real meaning of thana is specific to the tongue, here a metaphorical meaning is intended: absolute glorification and seeking nearness; glorification with the tongue is not intended. This is of the type: mentioning the restricted and intending the unrestricted.
Question: Such a metaphor could also occur in praise (hamd), that praise is taken as absolute praise, not restricted to the tongue. Why was the metaphor not taken there?
Answer: Actually, in 'And the reins of praise to Him' a metaphor in the form of simile and allusion is intended. Because the one who intends glorification and the intention of nearness to Allah necessarily...
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the author's opening statement regarding praise (al-hamd) and its division into 'first' and 'second'. The commentator explores six probabilities for the meaning of 'first' and 'second'. The first probability distinguishes between praise for Allah's essence/attributes versus praise for His blessings. The second probability maps praise onto four categories of divine action: creation and preservation in this world and the next, supported by Quranic references. The third probability interprets 'first' and 'second' as praise in this world and the Hereafter. The fourth takes them as repetition. The fifth relates to the composition of two works, and the sixth to the beginning and end of composition. Following these probabilities, a question arises about redundancy: if all praise is already covered, why mention 'secondly'? The commentator provides two answers: first, it is for multiplication (emphasis). Second, the earlier praise was general in terms of objects but specific in vehicle (tongue); the later phrase generalizes the vehicle to include all forms of worship, not just tongue. A further objection is that 'thana' (praise) is also specific to tongue, but the commentator replies that here 'thana' is used metaphorically for absolute glorification. Another objection asks why the same metaphor is not applied to 'hamd', and the answer is that in 'thana' a metaphor is used via simile and allusion because the context of turning the reins of praise implies comprehensive glorification beyond the tongue.

Key Terms
الحمد (al-hamd): Praise that is only with the tongue; it does not require the praised to have bestowed a favor.
الشكر (al-shukr): Gratitude that is with the heart, tongue, and limbs; obligatory for divine favors.
اولا و ثانیا (awwalan wa thaniyan): Used by the author to distinguish between two aspects of praise: first and second, with multiple interpretations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between hamd (praise) and shukr (gratitude)?
A) Hamd is with the tongue only; shukr is with heart, tongue, and limbs.
B) Hamd is for blessings; shukr is for essence.
C) Hamd is obligatory; shukr is recommended.
D) They are synonyms.

Key Takeaway
The division of praise into 'first' and 'second' serves to either multiply praise or expand its vehicle, resolving apparent redundancy through the Hanafi distinction between hamd and shukr.

Hanafi Defense of Combining Haqiqah and Majaz in Textual Analysis
Defense of rhetorical construction against grammatical objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on combining literal and metaphorical meanings (al-jam' bayn al-haqiqah wa al-majaz) in a single utterance and apply the Hanafi strategies for resolving apparent contradictions in textual interpretation.

Core Idea
Hanafis permit combining literal and metaphorical meanings through ellipsis, general metaphor, or assuming future as present.

In this lesson, we examine a classic passage from the sharh of Al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih where the commentator defends the author's use of 'hamidan' in two senses—literal praise in this world and metaphorical intention for the hereafter. The objections raised test the limits of combining haqiqah and majaz, and the Hanafi response demonstrates a flexible yet principled approach to textual interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
The objector claims that using 'hamidan' both literally (praise in this world) and metaphorically (intention of praise for the hereafter) violates the rule against combining haqiqah and majaz in a single utterance.
This is a common rhetorical objection in usul al-fiqh.
2
Locate the Hanafi Responses
The commentator provides five distinct answers: (1) ellipsis (mahdhuf), (2) general permissibility of combining, (3) it is actually a general metaphor (umum al-majaz), (4) praise can be for future blessings, and (5) assuming the non-existent as existent.
Each response addresses a different aspect of the objection.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Principle
Note that the Hanafi school often allows jam' bayn al-haqiqah wa al-majaz when necessary for coherence, especially in oblique constructions. The principle of 'amm al-majaz' (general metaphor) is cited as a specific Hanafi justification.
This flexibility arises from the Hanafi emphasis on maqasid (purposes) over strict literalism.
4
Apply to the Text
See how each response resolves the temporal contiguity issue between the beginning of the book and the praise of the hereafter. For example, by treating the second 'hamidan' as an implied word with metaphorical meaning, the literal sense is confined to the first occurrence.
The temporal gap between this world and the hereafter is bridged by intention or assumption.
5
Evaluate the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference is that Hanafis can maintain rhetorical elegance while others may require stricter literal consistency. This affects how opening phrases of books and khutbas are interpreted.
Understanding this dispute helps in critically evaluating different scholarly approaches to similar texts.

Misk al-Maleeh: Unity and contemporaneity of beginning and time of praise and blessing are established, therefore making it a state (hal) is correct. If you say: According to the third aspect, he is 'praising secondly' in the sense of intending praise and resolving upon it to be simultaneous with the agent; then it would necessitate combining literal and metaphorical meaning. I say: It is made of the type of ellipsis, i.e., 'and praising secondly' in the sense of 'resolving upon praise,' so combining literal and metaphorical does not occur. Translation: So if you say that according to the third aspect he will be 'praising secondly' meaning 'intending praise' and 'resolving upon praise' so that it becomes simultaneous with the agent, and then it necessitates combining literal and metaphorical. I say that it is made from the category of the deleted, i.e., 'praising secondly' meaning 'resolving upon praise,' so combining literal and metaphorical is not necessary. In short, an answer to a question, but this question and answer depend on another question that the commentator did not mention. The question is: Previously you presented the form of the contiguity of states with time of the original act of beginning, that 'beginning' means the customary thing up to the purposes of the book, and the limit of prayer also occurs in that time, so contiguity with the agent is realized. This interpretation can be useful in 'praising firstly' but not in 'secondly' because according to the third interpretation 'secondly' means praise in the hereafter, so praise will occur in the hereafter time, while the beginning of the book is in this world; contiguity of the state with the agent in terms of time is impossible because the times of this world and the hereafter are far apart. The answer given to this question is: In this case, when praise is related to 'firstly,' its literal meaning is intended; when related to 'secondly,' the metaphorical meaning of 'intent and resolve upon praise' is intended. The purpose is: When I begin this book, I am intending and resolving upon the praise of the hereafter, so the time of intention and resolve is united with the time of beginning the book because at the time of beginning the book, his intention and resolve concerning the hereafter is present, so contiguity is established. Then there is an objection to this answer, which the commentator Taftazani mentions and answers. The objection: In the form of this answer, combining literal and metaphorical is necessary because when the word 'praise' is related to 'firstly,' literal meaning is intended; when related to 'secondly,' it becomes in the meaning of 'intent' and metaphorical meaning is intended, and that is invalid. Answer (1): The summary of the commentator's answer is that combining literal and metaphorical in a single word is not necessary here; rather, before 'secondly' there is an implied 'praising' from which metaphorical meaning (intent) is taken. The phrase would be: 'praising God firstly and praising secondly' — the first 'praising' is literal, the second 'praising' metaphorical. Answer (2): Combining literal and metaphorical is permissible according to some imams, and this speech is based on that. Answer (3): This is not combining literal and metaphorical, rather it is a general metaphor, and that is permissible according to the Hanafis, so only metaphorical meaning is intended, which is 'resolve upon praise' whether in this world or the hereafter, so no problem. Answer (4): It is not necessary for praise for a blessing that the time of praise and the blessing be one; rather, it is also permissible to praise in the present for a future blessing in the hereafter, so the purpose is: I am praising now at the time of beginning the book for the blessings of the hereafter, so contiguity of time is agreed, no problem (mentioned by Allamah Hali). Answer (5): Sometimes a non-existent thing is assumed as existent due to certainty of its occurrence; here too, the author assumes the hereafter praise, which has not yet occurred, as existent and praises it at the time of beginning the book, so no problem. His statement: 'And upon the best of His messengers, sending blessings.' Since the greatest of blessings reaching the servant is the religion of Islam, and through it one attains eternal bliss in the abode of peace, and that is through the mediation of the Prophet, peace be upon him, supplication came as a follower of praise of God, so the author made prayer follow the praise. Translation: And when among the blessings reaching the servant the greatest blessing was the religion of Islam, and through it one attains eternal bliss in the abode of peace, and this religion of Islam is obtained through the mediation of the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, so prayer upon the Prophet becomes a follower of praise of God Almighty, so the author made prayer a sequel to praise. In short, answer to an implied question. The question: Why was prayer upon the Prophet mentioned after praise? Praise of God was mentioned because He is the true Benefactor; His blessings were thanked. Prayer has no reason to be mentioned. Answer (1): Just like praise, prayer upon the Prophet is necessary because the Prophet is also a great benefactor and bestower of favor for us, because among all blessings the great blessing is the religion of Islam, through which attainment and eternity in paradise are achieved, and the religion of Islam reached us through the Prophet; therefore he is the greatest benefactor, and the rule is: thanking the benefactor is obligatory by reason, and on this basis mentioning prayer upon the Prophet was necessary. Answer (2): The commentator's answer is based on the Prophet's being a benefactor, but some scholars answered that the master of both worlds deserves prayer by virtue of his perfect essence, just as God's essence deserves praise by virtue of His perfect essence, regardless of whether He is a benefactor, because after God, the Prophet is the greatest and most perfect, as a poet's verse: 'From your luminous face, the moon was illuminated' — 'After God, you are great, the story is short' — so he deserves prayer by virtue of that, whether he is a benefactor or not. Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the introductory phrasing of Al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih. It addresses objections to the grammatical and rhetorical structure of the author's opening words: 'al-hamdu lillah... wa salatu 'ala rasulih.' The discussion centers on two main issues: (1) The grammatical state (hal) of 'hamidan' (praising) and its temporal contiguity with the agent, and (2) the justification for mentioning salawat (blessings) after hamd. 

The commentator begins by establishing that the beginning of the book and the time of praise and prayer are united; therefore making 'hamidan' a hal (circumstantial accusative) is correct. He then presents a hypothetical objection: if the third interpretation of 'hamidan thalithan' is taken as 'intending praise' to achieve temporal contiguity, then it would combine literal and metaphorical meanings (since 'hamdan' is literal praise but the intended meaning is metaphorical intent). The response is that this is avoided by positing an implied word (mahdhuf) — i.e., 'wa hamidan thalithan' with 'hamidan' in its metaphorical sense. 

A deeper objection follows: the first occurrence of hamd (awwalan) refers to literal praise offered at the beginning of the book, while the second (thalithan) refers to praise in the hereafter. Temporal contiguity is impossible because the times are different. The commentator answers that when hamd is related to 'awwalan' it is literal; when to 'thalithan' it is metaphorical (intention of praise), and intention is contemporary with the beginning of the book. 

This answer itself is objected to as combining literal and metaphorical. The commentator gives five responses: 
(1) The conjunction is not on the same word — the second 'hamidan' is implicit and purely metaphorical. 
(2) Some scholars allow combining literal and metaphorical. 
(3) It is actually a general metaphor (majaz mursal) which is permissible for Hanafis. 
(4) Praise can be for future blessings; thus the author praises now for future blessings of the hereafter. 
(5) A non-existent thing can be assumed as existent due to certainty; the author treats the future praise of the hereafter as existent.

Then the passage shifts to justifying the inclusion of salawat after hamd. The commentator explains that the greatest blessing (Islam) comes through the Prophet, so supplication for him follows praise of God. He answers a presumed objection: Why mention salawat after hamd? Answers: (1) The Prophet is the greatest benefactor, and thanking the benefactor is obligatory by reason. (2) Some scholars say the Prophet deserves blessings by his perfect essence, regardless of being a benefactor, just as God deserves praise by His perfect essence. (3) The text is cut off but likely continues with another answer.

This is a classic exercise in usul textual analysis: defending the author's rhetorical choices against grammatical and rational objections. The commentator demonstrates multiple interpretive strategies, reflecting the Hanafi method of preferring rational coherence (talil) and showing flexibility in interpretation to preserve the intended meaning.

Key Terms
جمع بین الحقیقة والمجاز (jam' bayn al-haqiqah wa al-majaz): Using a single word or phrase in both its literal and metaphorical senses within the same context.
محذوف (mahdhuf): An omitted word that is understood from the context; ellipsis.
عموم مجاز (umum al-majaz): A type of metaphor where the entire expression is metaphorical, not just one word.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is one answer to the objection that combining literal and metaphorical meanings is necessary in the phrase 'hamidan awwalan wa thalithan'?
A) The second 'hamidan' is implied (mahdhuf) and purely metaphorical, so no literal meaning is intended there.
B) The author used the word in a single sense only, so no combination occurs.
C) The objection is invalid because the words are not being used simultaneously.
D) The commentator says it is permissible regardless of the objection.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school employs multiple strategies—ellipsis, general metaphor, assumption of existence—to defend textual coherence when apparent contradictions arise from combining literal and figurative meanings.

6 min
Parsing 'Lam Tablugh' and the Omitted Pronoun in Usul al-Fiqh
Grammatical analysis of a relative clause with omitted pronoun

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the grammatical issue of a missing pronoun (aa'id mahdhuf) in a relative clause and explain how the Hanafi commentator resolves it using the principle of placing the explicit in place of the implicit (wad' al-zahir mawdi' al-mudmar).

Core Idea
An omitted pronoun in a relative clause is permissible if the clause itself serves as the link.

This passage examines a grammatical objection to the phrase 'lam tablugh sifat tadiqatin' in a usul text. At first glance, the relative clause seems to lack a pronoun linking it to its antecedent, which would normally render the construction invalid. The commentator defends the phrase by presenting two possible interpretations: either a deleted pronoun or the explicit clause itself acting as the link. Understanding this resolution is crucial for correctly parsing usul texts and avoiding superficial objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammatical problem
Recognize that the relative clause 'lam tablugh...' describes a preceding noun but has no visible pronoun (aa'id) referring back to that noun. This violates the typical requirement for a link between a clause and its antecedent.
The problem is not lexical but structural.
2
Understand the commentator's first solution
The first solution posits an omitted pronoun: the clause is actually 'lam yablughuha' (they did not reach it), where 'ha' refers back to the details. This is a common phenomenon in Arabic grammar where the pronoun is dropped due to context.
The omitted pronoun is called 'aa'id mahdhuf'.
3
Understand the commentator's second solution
The second solution interprets the explicit phrase 'fursan hadha al-ilm' (the knights of this science) as itself serving as the link. This is called 'placing the explicit in place of the implicit' (wad' al-zahir mawdi' al-mudmar). The entire clause then acts as a link by repeating the antecedent.
This is a rhetorical device to emphasize the subject.
4
Recognize the principle
The commentator uses the principle that linguistic context (qarina) can compensate for an omitted grammatical element. In usul al-fiqh, such principles are essential for interpreting primary texts correctly.
This is a form of bayan (clarification) in usul.
5
Apply the analysis
Apply this understanding to similar constructions in other usul texts. When encountering a relative clause without an explicit pronoun, consider whether an omitted pronoun or the clause itself can serve as the link, guided by context and the usul principle of wad' al-zahir mawdi' al-mudmar.
This prevents misinterpretation of the author's intent.

قوله لم تبلغ صفة تدقيقات والعائد محذوف اى لم يبلغها فرسان علم الاصول الى هذه الغاية من الزمان او المراد لم يصل فرسان هذا العلم الى تلك الغاية من التدقيق فيكون من وضع الظاهر موضع المضمر وتعدية البلوغ بالى لجعله بمعنى الوصول والانتهاء. ترجمہ الم مبلغ یہ تدقیقات کی صفت ہے اور عائد محذوف ہے یعنی (لم يبلغہا) نہیں پہنچے ان تدقیقات تک اصول فقہ کے شہسوار اس زمانہ کی انتہا تک یا مراد یہ ہے کہ نہیں پہنچے اس علم کے شہوار اس تدقیق کی انتہا تک پس یہ ہوگا اہم ظاہر کو میر کی جگہ رکھنے سے ۔ اور بلوغ کوالی کیساتھ متعدی کرنا اسکو وصول اور انتہاء کے معنی میں کرنے کی وجہ سے ہے۔ غرض بیان ترکیب کر لم يبلغ فرسان هذا العلم إلى هذا الامد جمادیل کر تدقیقات کی صفت ہے۔ والعائد محذوف الخ ۔ غرض جواب سوال مقدر۔ سوال یہ ہوتا ہے کہ جب جملہ ماقبل کیلئے صفت واقع ہو تو اس میں کسی رابط کا ہونا ضروری ہے پھر رابطہ کی دو صورتیں ہیں (1) کبھی رابطہ ضمیر ہوگی (۲) کبھی موصوف بعینہ جملہ صنعتیہ میں مذکور ہوگا جو کہ رابطہ ہوگا یہاں رابطہ کی دو صورتوں میں سے کوئی بھی نہیں ہے۔ جواب ! شارح جواب دے رہے ہیں کہ مقام ہذا میں دونوں صورتیں ہو سکتی ہیں۔ اول صورت! میں رابط ضمیر محذوف ہوگی عبارت ہوگی۔ لم يبلغها فرسان هذا العلم الى هذه الغالية ، اور اس غایت سے غایت زمانی
His statement: 'Lam tablugh sifat tadiqatin wa al-aa'idu mahdhuf' i.e., 'the knights of the science of usul did not reach these details up to this limit of time', or the meaning is 'the knights of this science did not reach that limit of precision', thus it is placing the explicit in place of the implicit, and making 'bulugh' transitive with 'ila' to give it the meaning of 'wusul' and 'intiha'. The purpose is to explain the structure: 'lam yablugh farsan hadha al-ilm ila hadha al-amad' and 'tadiqat' is the attribute. The omitted pronoun... Then an objection: when a clause is an attribute, it needs a link. There are two forms: (1) a pronoun link, (2) the described noun itself mentioned in the clause. Here neither is apparent. The commentator answers: in this place both are possible. First form: the pronoun link is omitted, the phrase would be 'lam yablughuha farsan hadha al-ilm...'
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage deals with the grammatical parsing of the phrase 'lam tablugh sifat tadiqatin' as an attributive clause. The commentator explains that the omitted pronoun (al-aa'id) can be either a hidden pronoun meaning 'lam yablughuha' or the explicit phrase 'farsan hadha al-ilm' itself can serve as the link (by placing the explicit in place of the implicit). He then entertains a hypothetical objection: a relative clause describing a preceding phrase normally requires a visible link; here none seems to exist. The commentator's answer is that both types of link are actually present: either a deleted pronoun or the entire clause itself acting as the link. The text cuts off while explaining the first form.

Key Terms
عائد محذوف (aa'id mahdhuf): An omitted pronoun that links a relative clause to its antecedent.
وضع الظاهر موضع المضمر (wad' al-zahir mawdi' al-mudmar): Placing the explicit in place of the implicit; a rhetorical device where the subject noun is repeated or mentioned explicitly in the clause to serve as the link.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What grammatical issue is being resolved in this passage?
A) The meaning of 'tadiqat'
B) The missing link (aa'id) in the relative clause
C) The conjugation of 'balagha'
D) The definition of 'farsan'

Key Takeaway
In Arabic grammatical analysis for usul al-fiqh, a missing pronoun in a relative clause can be explained by either an omitted pronoun or the clause itself acting as the link, following the principle of wad' al-zahir mawdi' al-mudmar.

The Deliberate Use of Demonstrative Pronouns in Al-Tawdih's Preface
Explaining the use of demonstrative pronoun and particle 'lammā'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi commentator justifies the use of 'hādhā' over a pronoun, and analyze the causal relationship established by 'lammā' in a classical usul text.

Core Idea
Every word in a usul text is deliberate; even a demonstrative pronoun reveals the author's intent.

In the preface of Al-Tawdih, the author writes 'I named this book...' using the demonstrative pronoun 'hādhā' instead of a simple pronoun. This passage from the commentary explores why. The analysis reveals how scholars combine grammar, rhetoric, and logic to understand the author's deeper meaning. The discussion also addresses how the particle 'lammā' creates a causal link between the completion of the commentary and the naming of the book. Understanding such analysis sharpens your ability to interpret dense usul texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and the issue
The phrase is 'sammaytu hādhā al-kitāb lammā...' (I named this book because...). The issue: why use 'hādhā' instead of a pronoun like 'huwa'?
This is a common inquiry in commentary literature.
2
Understand the principle of tamayyuz
The commentator explains that the demonstrative pronoun gives 'kāmil al-tamyīz' (complete distinction) compared to a pronoun.
Pronouns only refer back; demonstratives point directly.
3
Analyze the causal link with lammā
Lammā normally establishes that the second clause is the result of the first. The objection is that completion of a commentary is not an obvious cause for naming it Al-Tawdih.
The first clause (condition) is the completion; the second (result) is the naming.
4
Examine the answers to the objection
Multiple answers are given: the pronoun 'itmāmihi' refers to a described commentary; only 'sammaytu' is the result; causality is based on general rule not universal; or 'sammaytu' is coordinate not the answer.
Each answer uses a different principle: taḍmīn, syntactical analysis, etc.
5
Note the manuscript variant
Some manuscripts have a wāw before 'sammaytu', making it coordinate with another verb, which changes the syntax of the answer.
This shows how textual variants affect the interpretation.

مسک الملیح
فرح الصرب الطويح)
مراد ہو گی مطلب ہوگا کہ اس زمانہ تک شہسوار علم اصول میں سے کوئی بھی ان تدقیقات تک نہیں پہنچ سکا۔ دوسری صورت ! میں الامد سے غایت تدقیقی مراد ہوگی تو گویا موصوف تدقیقات خود جملہ صنعتیہ میں مذکور ہوگا تو یہ من قبيل وضع المظهر موضع المضمر ہوگا جیسے الحاقہ ما الحاقہ کیونکہ مرجع کے مذکور ہونے کی وجہ سے موقع ضمیر کا تھا تو عبارت اس طرح ہوگی لم یبلغ فرسان هذ العلم الی هذه الغالبية من التدقیق تو موصوف تدقیق خود جملہ صفتیہ میں آگیا تو ربط حاصل ہو گیا۔ وتعدیۃ البلوغ ۔ جواب سوال ! بلوغ فعل متعدی بنفسہ ہوتا ہے اس کوالی کے ساتھ متعدی کرنا درست نہیں۔ جواب ! یہ دیا کہ صنعت تضمین کو اختیار کیا لم يبلغ بمعنی لم یصل اور لم ینتہ ہے۔
قوله سميت هذ الكتاب جواب لما وضع الاشارة موضع الضمير لكمال العناية بمتييزه فان قلت لما . لثبوت الثاني لثبوت الاول فيقتضى سببية ماذكر بعد لما لتسمية الكتاب بالتوضيح فما وجهه قلت وجهه ان الضمير فى اتمامه للشرح المذكور الموصوف بانه شرح لمشكلات التنقيح وفتح لمغلقاته اتمام مثل هذا الشرح مع اشتماله على الامور المذكورة يصلح سببا لتسميت بالتوضيح في حل غوامض التنقيح.
ترجمه ! سميت هذا الكتاب لما کا جواب ہے منصف نے اسم اشارہ کو ضمیر ک جگہ رکھ دیا بوجہ کمال اہتمام کے اسکو جدا کرنے کیسا تھ (دوسری کتب سے ) پس اگر تو کہے کہ لما ثبوت ثانی کیلئے ہے بوجہ ثبوت اول کے پس یہ تقاضا کر یگا اُس چیز کے سبب بنے کا جو لما کے بعد ذکر کی گئی ہے کتاب کے نام رکھنے کیلئے تو ضیح کیساتھ پس اسکی کیا توجیہ ہے میں کہتا ہوں کہ اسکی توجیہ یہ ہے کہ ضمیر اتمامہ میں شرح مذکور کے لئے جو کہ موصوف ہے بایں صفت کہ وہ تنقیح کے مشکلات کیلئے شرح ہے اور اس کے مغلقات کو کھولنے والی ہے اور اس جیسی شرح کو کمل کرنا ساتھ مشمل ہونے اسکے امور مذکورہ پر صلاحیت رکھتی ہے سبب بنے کی توضیح فی حل غوامض التنقیح نام رکھنے کے ساتھ ۔
جواب لما سے بیان ترکیب ہے اسمیت جواب لما ہے۔ فائدہ ایہاں دو نسخے ہیں (۱) ایک نسخہ میں واؤ نہیں ہے اس نسخے کے مطابق ترکیب درست ہے سمیت جواب لما ہے (۲) نسخہ ثانیہ میں واؤ ہے دریں صورت و سمیت جعلتہ عراضتہ پر معطوف ہوگا اور جعلیہ عراضہ جواب لما ہوگا نہ کہ وسمیت۔ (۲) دوسرا جواب یہ دیا گیا ہے کہ واؤ زائدہ ہے عاطفہ نہیں ہے فلا اشکال وضع اسم الاشارہ الخ۔ جواب سوال ! سوال یہ ہوتا ہے کہ ہذا کتاب کا مشار الیہ کتاب ہے جو ماقبل میں مذکور ہو چکی ہے تو یہ موقع ضمیر کا تھا سمیہ کہنا چاہیے تھا اسم اشارہ ذکر کرنے میں کیا حکمت ہے۔ جواب ! (۱) حکمت یہ ہے کہ گو ضمیر کے ذکر سے مرجع کو تمیز حاصل ہو جاتا ہے لیکن کمال تمیز حاصل نہیں ہوتا جب کہ اسم اشارہ کو ذکر کرنے سے مشار الیہ میں کمال تمیز حاصل ہو جاتا ہے گویا مشار الیہ محسوس و مبصر ہوتا ہے یہاں مقصود کمال تمیز کا حصول تھا اسی بناء پر اسم اشارہ ذکر کیا۔ جواب (۲) اسم اشارہ کو اس لیے ذکر کیا کہ مرجع بہت بعید ہو گیا تھا تو اسم اشارہ کا ذکر مناسب تھا ۔ فان قلت حاصل اشکال یہ ہے کہ لما موضوع ہے برائے ثبوت ثانی بسبب مثبوت اول اول جمله شرط ثانی جزاء ہوگا۔ ضابطہ مسلمہ ہے کہ شرط جزاء کیلئے سبب بنتی ہے جز مسبب بنتی ہے مثلا ان ضربت ضربی ضابطہ کے مطابق لما تیسرا اتمامه الخ شرط وسبب بنے گا اور سمیت خذ الكتاب بالتوضيح في حل غوامض الصفح جزاء اور مسبب بنے گا حالانکہ اتمام شرح الح توضیح نام رکھنے کا سبب نہیں ہو سکتا کیونکہ ان میں کوئی ربط نہیں ہے۔ جواب ! (۱) اتمامہ کی ضمیر کا مرجع شرح ہے لیکن مطلقا نہیں بلکہ وہ شرح جو موصوف ہے بشرح مشکلات وفتح مغلقات اور دیگر اوصاف کے ساتھ اور شرح موصوف بهذه الصفات المذکورہ کا اتمام صلاحیت رکھتا ہے کہ وہ سبب بنے توضیح في حل غوامض افلیج نام رکھنے کیلئے تا کہ اسم مطابق مسمی ہو جائے لیکن شارح تفتازانی کا جواب قابل اشکال ہے کیونکہ ضمیر کا مرجع فقط ذات ہوتی ہے ذوات مع الصفات مرجع نہیں ہوتا اسی بناء پر اللہ تعالی نے اولئک علی صدی میں اولئک اسم اشارہ ذکر کیا ھم ضمیر کو ذکر نہ کیا کیونکہ اس صورت میں ھم ضمیر کا مرجع ذوات متقین بنتے جبکہ ذات باری تعالی متقین موصوفین بهذه الصفات کو مرجع بنانا چاہتے تھے تو اسم اشارہ ذکر کیا ۔ جواب ! (۲) جواب وجزاء فقط سمیت ہے تو اتمام شرح تسمیہ هذا الکتاب کا سبب بن سکتا ہے یقینا۔ جواب ! (۳) سبیت و معیت بنا قانون اکثری ہے کلی نہیں ہے جیسے ۔ ولما توجہ تلقاء مدین قال عسی ربی ان بعد ینی میں توجہ الی مدین اس قول کا سبب نہیں ہے۔ جواب! (۴) لما کا جواب جعلتہ عراضہ ہے نہ کہ سمیتہ بلکہ معطوف ہے تو اصل جواب کا مسبب بننا ضروری ہے معطوف کا مسبب بننا ضروری نہیں ہے کیونکہ جو امور معطوف علیہ میں ملحوظ ہوتے ہیں ضروری نہیں ہے کہ معطوف میں بھی ملحوظ ہوں جیسے رب شاہ و شک تھا رب کا مدخول معرفہ نہیں ہوسکتا لیکن معطوف علی مدخول رب معرفہ ہو سکتا ہے۔
تمت خطبة التوضيح بعونه تعالى وبفضله )
Misk al-Maleeh (the sweet musk) […] [Explanation of the difficult points of al-Tawdih]. It would mean that no knight of the science of usul had reached these fine points until that time. In another form, al-ghāyat al-tadqīq is intended from al-āmad, so the described (tadqīq) itself would be mentioned in the adjectival phrase, and this would be of the type 'placing the explicit term in the place of the implicit pronoun', like 'al-ḥāqqatu mā al-ḥāqqah' because due to the mention of the antecedent, the position was for a pronoun. So the expression would be: 'lam yablugh fursān hādhā al-ʿilm ilā hādhi al-ghāyati min al-tadqīq' and thus the described (tadqīq) comes in the adjectival phrase itself, so connection is obtained. And the transitive of 'bulūgh': answer to a question! 'Bulūgh' is a transitive verb by itself; making it transitive with 'ilā' is not correct. Answer! It was said that the device of 'taḍmīn' was adopted: 'lam yablugh' in the sense of 'lam yaṣil' and 'lam yantahī'.

His saying 'sammaytu hādhā al-kitāb' is the answer to 'lammā'. He placed the demonstrative pronoun in the place of the pronoun due to great care for its distinction. If you say: 'lammā' is for the establishment of the second due to the establishment of the first; it requires causality for what is mentioned after 'lammā' for the naming of the book as 'al-Tawdīḥ', so what is its justification? I say: its justification is that the pronoun in 'itmāmihi' refers to the mentioned commentary described as a commentary on the problems of al-Tanqīḥ and opener of its difficulties. The completion of such a commentary, along with its inclusion of the mentioned matters, is fit to be a cause for naming it 'al-Tawdīḥ fī ḥall ghawāmiḍ al-Tanqīḥ'.

Translation: 'sammaytu hādhā al-kitāb' is the answer to 'lammā'. The author placed the demonstrative pronoun in place of the pronoun due to great care for its distinction (from other books). So if you say that 'lammā' establishes the second due to the establishment of the first, then it requires that what is mentioned after 'lammā' be a cause for naming the book as 'al-Tawdīḥ' – so what is its justification? I say: its justification is that the pronoun in 'itmāmihi' refers to the mentioned commentary which is described as a commentary on the problems of al-Tanqīḥ and opener of its difficulties. The completion of such a commentary, along with its inclusion of the mentioned matters, has the capacity to be a cause for naming it 'al-Tawdīḥ fī ḥall ghawāmiḍ al-Tanqīḥ'.

Explanation of the syntax of the answer to 'lammā': 'sammaytu' is the answer to 'lammā'. Benefit: There are two manuscripts here: (1) In one manuscript, there is no 'wāw'; according to this manuscript, the syntax is correct: 'sammaytu' is the answer to 'lammā'. (2) In the second manuscript, there is a 'wāw'; in that case, 'wa sammaytu' is coordinated with 'jaʿaltuhu ʿarāḍatan' and 'jaʿaltuhu ʿarāḍatan' is the answer to 'lammā', not 'wa sammaytu'. (2) A second answer is given: the 'wāw' is redundant, not conjunctive, so no problem. 'waḍaʿa ism al-ishārat…'

Answer to a question: The question is: the referent of 'hādhā al-kitāb' is the book mentioned previously, so this was a place for a pronoun; it should have been 'sammaytu' (with a pronoun). What is the wisdom in mentioning the demonstrative pronoun? Answer: (1) The wisdom is that although mentioning a pronoun gives distinction to its antecedent, it does not give complete distinction; whereas mentioning the demonstrative pronoun gives complete distinction to the referent, as if the referent is perceptible and visible. Here complete distinction was intended, so the demonstrative pronoun was mentioned. (2) The demonstrative pronoun was mentioned because the antecedent had become very distant, so mentioning the demonstrative pronoun was appropriate.

'fa-in qulta': The gist of the objection is that 'lammā' is used for establishing the second due to the establishment of the first; the first clause is the condition, the second is the result. It is a established rule that the condition is a cause for the result, and the result is an effect. For example, 'in ḍarabta ḍarībī' – according to the rule, 'lammā' with 'itmāmihi…' becomes the condition and cause, and 'sammaytu hādhā al-kitāb bi-al-Tawḍīḥ fī ḥall ghawāmiḍ…' becomes the result and effect, even though the completion of the commentary cannot be a cause for naming it 'al-Tawḍīḥ' because there is no connection between them. Answer: (1) The pronoun in 'itmāmihi' refers to the commentary, but not absolutely; rather, it is the commentary described with 'sharḥ mushkilāt wa fatḥ mughlaqāt' and other attributes. The completion of a commentary described with these mentioned attributes has the capacity to be a cause for naming it 'al-Tawḍīḥ fī ḥall ghawāmiḍ al-Tanqīḥ' so that the name matches the named. But the answer of the commentator al-Taftazānī is open to objection because the antecedent of a pronoun is only the essence; essences with attributes are not antecedents. For this reason, Allah Almighty said 'ulā'ika ʿalā hudā' – He used the demonstrative pronoun 'ulā'ika' instead of the pronoun 'hum', because in that case 'hum' would refer to the essences of the muttaqīn, while He intended to refer to the essences of the muttaqīn described with these attributes, so He used the demonstrative pronoun. Answer: (2) The answer and result is only 'sammaytu', so the completion of the commentary can certainly be a cause for naming this book. Answer: (3) Causality and association are based on the general rule, not universal. Example: 'wa lammā tawajjaha tilqā'a madyana qāla ʿasā rabbī an yahdīyanī sawā'a al-sabīl' – the turning towards Madyan is not a cause for this saying. Answer: (4) The answer to 'lammā' is 'jaʿaltuhu ʿarāḍatan' not 'sammaytuhu', rather 'sammaytuhu' is coordinate. So it is necessary that the original answer be an effect; it is not necessary that the coordinate be an effect, because matters considered in the coordinated-upon are not necessarily considered in the coordinate. Example: 'rabbu shā'a wa-shiqā' – 'rabbu' cannot have a definite noun as its object, but the coordinate to 'rabbu' can have a definite noun as its object.

Completed the preface of al-Tawḍīḥ by His help and bounty.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the author's preface, specifically analyzing the phrase 'sammaytu hādhā al-kitāb li-mā...' (I named this book because...). The commentator (probably Miskullāh?) explains why the author used the demonstrative pronoun 'hādhā' instead of a simple pronoun like 'huwa'. The reason given is 'kāmil al-ʿināya bi-tamyīzihi' – complete care for its distinction from other books. The passage then addresses grammatical objections: first, the particle 'lammā' normally requires that what follows (the completion of the commentary) be the cause of the result (naming the book). The objection is that the completion of the commentary is not an obvious cause for the name. Several answers are provided: 1) The pronoun in 'itmāmihi' refers to the commentary described with attributes, so its completion is a suitable cause. 2) Only 'sammaytu' is the result, not 'sammaytu hādhā al-kitāb'. 3) Causality is not always strict, and an example from the Quran is given. 4) Possibly 'sammaytu' is not the answer but a coordinate, so the causal link is not required. There is also discussion about manuscript variants with or without 'wāw'. This is a typical commentary style where the commentator explores every possible nuance of the text, including grammar, rhetoric, and logic. The key takeaway for students of usul is that even in the preface, the author's choice of words is deliberate and carries meaning, and scholars debated the precise reasons behind it.

Key Terms
اسم الإشارة (ism al-ishārah): Demonstrative pronoun, e.g., هٰذا, used for pointing to something specific.
لما (lammā): A particle meaning 'when' or 'because', used to establish a causal relationship between two clauses.
تضمین (taḍmīn): Giving a verb the meaning of another verb, here 'lam yablugh' taking the meaning of 'lam yaṣil'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the author use 'هذا' instead of a pronoun in 'سميت هذا الكتاب'?
A) For special distinction of the book
B) Because the book was far away
C) Because of the particle 'لما'
D) No particular reason

Key Takeaway
The author's use of 'hādhā' instead of a pronoun is a deliberate rhetorical choice to emphasize the distinctiveness of the book, and the causal link with 'lammā' is justified by the nature of the commentary described.

Distinguishing Grammatical Categories: Plural vs Generic Noun
Defining grammatical categories and resolving the dispute over 'kalim'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between plural, collective noun, and generic noun in Arabic, and identify the Hanafi position on the word 'kalim' as a generic noun, supporting it with its five proofs.

Core Idea
Kalim is a generic noun (jins), not a plural, proven by adjective, pattern, diminutive, tamyiz, and pronoun — five decisive proofs.

This lesson begins with a foundational grammatical taxonomy of singular, plural, collective nouns, and generic nouns — tools essential for precise legal analysis. These categories determine how words govern adjectives, pronouns, and even rulings in fiqh. The lesson then applies this taxonomy to a famous dispute: is the Qur'anic word 'kalim' a plural or a generic noun? The Hanafi school, following the majority, insists it is a generic noun, with profound implications for how we interpret divine speech.

Guided Path
1
Define the four grammatical categories
Distinguish singular, plural, collective noun, and generic noun by their defining features: indication of number, existence of singular, pronoun agreement, diminutive formation.
2
Identify the dispute about 'kalim'
Recognize that the majority (including Hanafis) say it is a generic noun (jins) while al-Mutarrizi and Sahib al-Lubab say it is a plural.
3
Analyze the opposing proof
The opponent argues that 'kalim' is always used for more than two, so it must be a plural.
Their dalil is purely based on usage.
4
Apply the Hanafi five proofs
Work through each of the five linguistic proofs from the text: adjective agreement, plural pattern, diminutive form, use as tamyiz, and pronoun reference.
Each proof independently shows 'kalim' cannot be a plural.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The classification affects adjective agreement, pronoun choice, and diminutive form when 'kalim' appears in legal texts.
For example, in 'al-kalim al-tayyib', the adjective remains masculine singular if 'kalim' is a generic noun.

Preamble: (1) Singular: indicates one, e.g., rajul. (2) Dual: indicates two, e.g., rajulan. (3) Plural: indicates more than two, and its singular exists from its root, and the pronoun referring to it is masculine singular. (4) Collective noun: applies to more than two, but its singular does not exist from its root, and the pronoun referring to it is feminine singular, e.g., rakab, raht. Difference between plural and collective noun: They agree that both apply to more than two and take a feminine singular pronoun. Differences: (1) Plural is plural in form and meaning, while collective noun is plural only in meaning. (2) Plural has a singular from its word; collective noun does not. (3) Plural is divided into plural of paucity and plural of multiplicity; collective noun is not divided. (4) Diminutive of collective noun is formed on its own pattern; plural takes its singular's diminutive. Genus: applies to few and many, singular and more than one, without distinction: if with ta, it means single unit; without ta, it means more than one, e.g., tamrah (single) and tamr (genus). Name of genus: applies to few and many with no distinguishing ta, same word for singular and plural, e.g., ma'. Difference between collective noun and genus: Both are singular in form; but genus can take either masculine or feminine pronoun, while collective noun takes only feminine. After this preamble, the purpose: There is disagreement about the word 'kalim'. The majority say it is a generic noun; while al-Mutarrizi and Sahib al-Lubab say it is a plural. Their proof: the word is used for more than two; if it were a genus, it would sometimes be used for one. The majority reply: usage is not a proof because it changes; the original coinage (wad') is the proof, and kalim can apply to both one and more than one, so it is a genus. Proofs of the majority: (1) In 'al-kalim al-tayyib', if it were plural, the adjective should be 'al-tayyibah'. (2) 'Kalim' is not on the pattern of any known plural. (3) If plural, its diminutive would be 'kulayyim', not 'kulaym'. (4) It is used as a specificative (tamyiz) with numbers, which only occurs with singular nouns. (5) The pronoun referring to it is masculine singular. (Second point?) If it were plural, it would either be sound or broken; but it is not sound (no waw-nun) and broken plurals do not preserve the singular form. Therefore, the majority view is preferred. Sharh Taftazani also supports the majority. He states that the relation of kalim to kalimah is like tamr to tamrah: tamr is a genus, tamrah its singular, with ta as the differentiator. Similarly, kalim is a genus, kalimah its singular. Kalim is singular in form but collective in meaning, i.e., it applies to many. So it has two aspects: singular in form, collective in meaning. Hence it can take a masculine singular adjective when considering the form.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This lengthy passage first provides a detailed grammatical preamble defining singular, dual, plural, collective noun, genus, and name of genus, with their differences. It then applies this to the dispute over 'kalim'. The majority hold that 'kalim' is a generic noun (jins), while al-Mutarrizi and Sahib al-Lubab argue it is a plural. The author presents five strong proofs for the majority position, including the adjective form, the plural patterns, the diminutive, its use as a specificative, and the pronoun reference. Taftazani agrees, explaining that 'kalim' is like 'tamr' – a generic noun with a singular 'kalimah', and it is singular in form but collective in meaning, allowing masculine singular adjectives.

Key Terms
جمع (jam'): Plural: indicates more than two, has a singular from its root, takes feminine singular pronoun, has regular diminutive from singular.
اسم جمع (ism jam'): Collective noun: applies to more than two, no singular from its root, takes feminine singular pronoun, diminutive on its own pattern.
جنس (jins): Generic noun: applies to both singular and plural, distinguished by the presence or absence of ta (feminine marker).
كلم (kalim): Word: subject of dispute; majority says it is a generic noun meaning 'speech/words', with singular 'kalimah'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the five proofs that 'kalim' is a generic noun?
A) The adjective 'tayyib' is masculine singular.
B) 'Kalim' is not on a known plural pattern.
C) 'Kalim' is used as a tamyiz with numbers.
D) 'Kalim' takes a feminine plural pronoun.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies 'kalim' as a generic noun (jins), not a plural, based on five decisive linguistic proofs, with important implications for interpreting divine speech.

Definition and Conditions of Nama
Lexical definition of nama with two conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions of nama (growth) as defined in Hanafi usul and distinguish it from saman and daram.

Core Idea
Nama requires increase in all three dimensions naturally – not just fatness or disease.

In Hanafi usul, the term nama carries a precise legal definition that affects rulings like zakat on livestock. The text provides a lexical and terminological clarification, establishing two conditions to distinguish genuine growth from mere increase. Understanding this distinction is foundational for applying the concept in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the lexical meaning
The text defines nama as increase and elevation (زيادة والارتفاع). Note the verb form and its pattern (from ضرب or نصر).
The lexical analysis helps establish the root meaning before imposing legal conditions.
2
Extract the first condition
The increase must occur in all dimensions: length, width, and depth. This excludes saman (fatness), which only increases in width.
Saman is increase in girth without length; thus it is not nama.
3
Extract the second condition
The increase must be natural and proportional, in accordance with the body's nature. This excludes daram (dropsy), which is unnatural and disease-related.
Daram is swelling due to illness, not natural growth.
4
Apply the conditions
Both conditions must be satisfied for something to be considered nama in a legal context. If either is missing, the increase is not legally recognized as growth.
This has implications for zakat on livestock: only natural growth counts toward the nisab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنماء الزيادة والارتفاع فرض نماء کی تحقیق لغوی کا بیان ہے انماء کا لغوی معنی زیادتی اور بلندی از باب ضرب ہو تو ناقص یائی از باب نصر ہو تو ناقص وادی ہو گا نماء میں دو شرطیں ہیں شرط اول یہ ہے کہ زیادتی تمام اقطار و اطراف جسم میں ہو یعنی طولا وعرضا و عمقا اس سے سمن ( موٹا پا ) خارج ہو گیا کیونکہ اس میں طولا زیادتی و بڑھوتری نہیں ہوتی دوسری شرط یہ ہے کہ وہ زیادتی تناسب طبعی اور مقتضی طبیعت و فطرت کے موافق ہو اس سے درم خارج ہو گیا کیونکہ وہ خلاف طبع اور بوجہ مرض ہوتا ہے
And 'al-nama' means increase and elevation. This is a lexical clarification of nama. The lexical meaning of 'inma'' is increase and height. From the pattern of daraba (ضرب) it would be defective ya' (ناقص یائی), from the pattern of nasara (نصر) it would be defective waw (ناقص وادی). Nama has two conditions: First condition is that the increase occurs in all dimensions and directions of the body, i.e., length, width, and depth. This excludes fatness (saman), because in it there is no increase in length. Second condition is that this increase is in accordance with natural proportion and what is required by nature and innate disposition. This excludes dropsy (daram), because it is contrary to nature and occurs due to disease.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage defines the term nama (growth) linguistically and specifies two conditions for it: (1) increase in all three dimensions (length, width, depth), distinguishing it from mere fatness; (2) natural proportional increase, distinguishing it from disease-related swelling like dropsy. This is important in Hanafi fiqh for issues like the growth of property (as in zakat on livestock or agricultural produce) or the growth of a child/slave. The lexical derivation is also noted: the verb is from the defective pattern.

Key Terms
نماء (nama’): Increase and elevation in size, specifically in all dimensions and naturally.
سمن (saman): Fatness; increase in width without increase in length.
درم (daram): Dropsy; unhealthy swelling due to disease.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Saman (fatness)
B) Daram (dropsy)
C) Natural growth in length, width, and depth
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
Nama is legally defined as dimensional and natural growth, excluding fatness and disease.

The Linguistic Definition of 'Asl' and the Crucial Qualification 'Min Haythu'
Explaining the lexical definition of 'asl' with a perspectival qualification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi lexical definition of 'asl', explain the purpose of the qualification 'min haythu innahu yubna 'alayhi', and apply it to resolve the apparent conflict that the four proofs (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma', Qiyas) are themselves based on other sciences.

Core Idea
A thing's being an 'asl' depends on perspective; the four proofs are roots for fiqh even if branches for kalam.

This lesson unpacks the precise lexical definition of 'asl' (origin/root) as used in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The commentator Taftazani introduces a crucial qualification that saves the definition from a serious objection. Understanding this subtlety is foundational for correctly applying the terms 'asl' and 'far'' in legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection against the matn's definition
The matn defined 'asl' simply as 'ma yubna 'alayhi shay' (that upon which something is built). But the four proofs (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma', Qiyas) are themselves built upon tawhid and kalam, so they would be branches, not roots. Why then are they called usul al-fiqh?
This is the objection Taftazani addresses.
2
Identify Taftazani's addition
Taftazani adds the qualification 'min haythu innahu yubna 'alayhi' (from the perspective that it is built upon it). This is not in the original definition and is the key innovation.
The qualification restricts the definition to a specific perspective.
3
Understand how the qualification resolves the objection
Something can be an asl in one respect and a far' in another. The four proofs are asl for fiqh (because fiqh is built on them) but far' relative to kalam (because they are based on kalam). The qualification makes this perspectival flexibility explicit.
Without the qualification, the definition would falsely imply absolute origin.
4
Apply the principle to the father-son-grandfather analogy
The passage gives the example: a father is an asl for his son (the son is part of him) but a far' relative to his own father. This illustrates the relational nature of asl and far'.
Similarly, the four proofs are asl for fiqh but far' for kalam.

فالاصول جمع اصل، اس میں شارح نے اصول کی تحقیق بیان کی ہے اصول جمع ہے اس کا مفرد اصل ہے نیز سوال کا جواب بھی ہو سکتا ہے جو ماتن پر وارد ہوتا ہے سوال ! یہ ہے کہ تعریف اضافی میں بقول ماتن مضاف کی تعریف کرنی چائیے اور مضاف اصول جمع ہے نہ کہ مفرد تو مصنف کو اصول ( جمع ) کی تعریف کرنی چاہیے تھی نہ کہ اصل (مفرد کی حالانکہ مصنف نے اصل کی تعریف کی ہے یہ تو مضاف کی تعریف نہ ہوئی تو شارح نے جواب دیا کہ اصول اصل کی جمع ہے مفرد کی تعریف و توضیح سے جمع کی تعریف و توضیح بھی ہو جاتی ہے تعریف خواہ اصل (مفرد ) کی ہو یا اصول (جمع کی ہو ) دونوں صورتوں میں تعریف المضاف ہی ہوگی - وهو في اللغة ما يبتنى عليه شئى من حيث انه يبتنى عليه اس عبارت میں شارح تفتازانی اصل کا لغوی معنی بیان کر رہے ہیں کہ اصل وہ چیز ہے جس پر کوئی دوسری چیز مبنی ہو اول شئی کو بائیں حیثیت کہ اس پر دوسری شئی کی بناء ہے اصل کہتے ہیں۔ فائدہ ! علامہ صدر الشریعہ نے اصل کا لغوی معنی ما یقینی علیہ شئی ذکر کیا ہے یہی مختار قاضی عضد الدین علامہ محلی ہے جبکہ دیگر علماء نے اور معانی بیان کیے ہیں (۲) علامہ رازی نے اصل کا لغوی معنی المحتاج الیہ ذکر کیا ہے (۳) علامہ آمدی نے اصل کا معنی بیان کیا ہے مایستند الیہ تحق الشئی (۴) مامنہ اشتی قاله صاحب الحاصل (۵) منشاء الشى وبهذا القيد خرج ادلة الفقه غرض شارح تفتازانی جواب سوال مقدر ۔ سوال ! یہ ہوتا ہے کہ مصنف نے تو اصل کی تعریف یہ کی ہے ما یعنی علیہ غیرہ، آپ نے اس کے ساتھ من حيث انه يبتنى علیہ کی قید کا اضافہ کیوں کیا اس میں کیا نکتہ ہے۔ جواب اشارح جواب دے رہے ہیں کہ اضافہ کرنے کی حکمت ایک اشکال کو رفع کرنا ہے، اشکال یہ ہوتا ہے کہ یہاں اصول فقہ سے مراد و لائل اربعہ میں کتاب ، سنت، اجماع ، وقیاس لیکن اصل کی تعریف ان پر صادق نہیں آتی کیونکہ اصل کی تعریف یہ ہے کہ اس پر غیر کی بناء ہو یہاں تو خود ادلہ اربعد بنی ہیں علم التوحید وعلم الکلام پر تو یہ اصول تو نہ ہوئے بلکہ یہ تو فروع ہو گئے لھذا ان کو اصول الفقہ کہنا درست نہیں ہے شارح تفتازانی فرماتے ہیں کہ جب ہم نے اصل کی تعریف میں من حيث انه يبتنى کی قید کا اضافہ کیا تو یہ اشکال رفع ہو گیا وہ اس طرح کہ اصل کی تعریف میں قید حیثیت ملحوظ ہے کہ اصل وہ ہے جس پر غیر کی بناء ہو اس حیثیت سے کہ اس پر کوئی اور شئی بنی ہے تو تعریف الاصل میں اس قید کا لحاظ کرنا ضروری ہے لھذا اصل کیلئے یہ ضروری نہیں ہے کہ وہ من کل الوجوہ اصل ہو بلکہ کسی خاص حیثیت سے اس کا اصل ہونا اور مبنی علیہ ہونا کافی ہے مثلاً اب اصل ہے ابن کیلئے بائیں حیثیت کہ ابن اس کا جز ہے نہ کہ مطلقا کیونکہ باعتبار جداب فرع ہے اسی طرح اصول الفقہ بمعنی دلائل الفقہ اصل ہیں بائیں حیثیت کہ فقہ ان پر مبنی ہے نہ کہ اس حیثیت سے
So usul is the plural of asl. In this, the commentator explains the verification of usul: usul is plural, its singular is asl. This also serves as an answer to a question that may be raised against the matn. The question is: In the definition of the idafi construction, according to the matn, the mudaf should be defined, and the mudaf here is usul (plural) not singular. So the author should have defined usul (the plural) not asl (the singular), whereas the author defined asl. So this would not be the definition of the mudaf. The commentator answers: usul is the plural of asl; defining and clarifying the singular also defines and clarifies the plural. Whether the definition is of asl (singular) or usul (plural), in both cases it is a definition of the mudaf. And [the definition of asl] in language is: that upon which something is built, from the perspective that it is built upon it. In this phrase, the commentator Taftazani is stating the lexical meaning of asl: asl is the thing upon which another thing is built, considering the perspective that another thing is built upon it. Benefit: Sadr al-Shariah mentioned the lexical meaning of asl as 'that upon which something is based' – this is the chosen opinion of Qadi 'Adud al-Din and 'Allama al-Mahalli. Other scholars have stated other meanings: (2) Fakhr al-Razi said it means 'that which is needed by something else'; (3) Amidi said 'that to which the realization of the thing is attributed'; (4) 'that from which something originates' as stated by Sahib al-Hasil; (5) 'the root/origin of the thing'. By these restrictions, the proofs of fiqh are excluded. The intent of the commentator Taftazani is to answer an anticipated question. The question is: The author defined asl as 'that upon which another is built'; why did you add the qualification 'from the perspective that it is built upon it'? What is the point? The commentator answers: The reason for adding it is to remove an objection. The objection is that by usul al-fiqh is meant the four proofs: Book, Sunnah, Ijma', and Qiyas. But the definition of asl does not apply to them because the definition is that upon which another is built, whereas these four proofs themselves are built upon the science of tawhid and kalam. So they would be branches (furu'), not roots. Therefore, calling them usul al-fiqh would be incorrect. The commentator Taftazani says: When we added the qualification 'min haythu innahu yubna 'alayhi' (from the perspective that it is built upon it) to the definition of asl, this objection is removed. That is, the definition of asl includes a perspective condition: asl is that upon which another is built from the perspective that it is built upon it. Thus, for asl, it is not necessary that it be an origin from all perspectives; rather, being an origin and a basis in a specific perspective is sufficient. For example, a father is an origin for his son from the perspective that the son is part of him, not absolutely, because from another perspective (as a grandfather) the father is a branch. Similarly, usul al-fiqh meaning the proofs of fiqh are asl from the perspective that fiqh is built upon them, not from that perspective.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the lexical definition of 'asl' (origin). The commentator first addresses an objection: why define the singular 'asl' when the term in usul al-fiqh is plural 'usul'? He clarifies that defining the singular suffices for the plural. Then he presents the lexical definition: 'asl is that upon which something is built, with the perspective that it is built upon it'. This definition is chosen from among several given by earlier scholars (Sadr al-Shariah, Razi, Amidi, etc.). The key innovation is the addition of the qualification 'min haythu innahu yubna 'alayhi' (from the perspective that it is built upon it). This is not a mere embellishment; it solves a crucial problem: if 'asl' meant simply 'that upon which something is built', then the four proofs (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma', Qiyas) would not qualify because they themselves are built upon the sciences of tawhid and kalam. But with the perspective qualification, it becomes clear that something can be a root in one respect and a branch in another. For example, a father is a root for his son (as a source of his existence) but a branch relative to his own father. So the four proofs are roots specifically for fiqh, even if they are branches in other contexts. This demonstrates the Hanafi concern for precise definition to avoid category errors in usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
اصل (asl): That upon which something is built, from the perspective that it is built upon it.
من حيث انه يبتنى عليه (min haythu innahu yubna 'alayhi): A qualification meaning 'from the perspective that it is built upon'. It restricts the definition to consider only the aspect making something a source.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Taftazani add the qualification 'min haythu innahu yubna 'alayhi' to the definition of 'asl'?
A) To emphasize the literal meaning
B) To exclude the four proofs from the definition
C) To solve the objection that the four proofs themselves are built on other sciences
D) To follow the majority of scholars

Key Takeaway
The qualification 'min haythu innahu yubna 'alayhi' is essential to make the definition of 'asl' perspectival, allowing something to be a root in one domain and a branch in another.

Rational Entailment: Definition vs Example
Clarifying that 'tartib al-hukm 'ala dalilihi' is an instance, not the definition of 'ibna 'aqli

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the definition of rational entailment ('ibna 'aqli) and its instances, and apply the correct comprehensive definition to identify new cases.

Core Idea
The definition of rational entailment is the ordering of one thing upon another by reason, not just one of its instances.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, precision in definitions is essential. The commentator here corrects a common misunderstanding: the phrase 'ordering of a ruling upon its proof' is not the definition of rational entailment, but only one example. This lesson unpacks why that distinction matters for the discipline's conceptual framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The base text had mentioned 'tartib al-hukm ala dalilihi' (ordering of a ruling upon its proof). Some might take this as the definition of rational entailment. First, identify that claim clearly.
This step isolates the common error.
2
Recognize the error
The commentator shows that if this were the definition, it would exclude many other forms of rational entailment, such as effect upon cause or metaphor upon reality. Understand the objection: the definition becomes too narrow.
This step highlights the flaw of non-comprehensiveness (ghayr jami').
3
State the correct definition
The comprehensive definition is 'ordering of one thing upon another by reason' (tartib al-shay' ala al-shay' bi i'tibar al-'aql). Memorize this definition and note that it includes all the examples given.
This is the core of the lesson.
4
Apply to other cases
Now, given a new relational statement (e.g., 'the derivative entails its root'), test whether it falls under rational entailment by checking if it fits the general definition.
Practice ensures conceptual mastery.

مثال کیوں نہیں ذکر کی تو اس کا جواب دیا جا سکتا ہے کہ ماہیت مرکبہ کی خاص کر مثال اس لیے بیان کی کیونکہ وہ مقام اور مقصود کے مناسب تھی کیونکہ مقصود لفظ اصل کی ماہیت کو سمجھانا ہے اور اصل ماہیت اعتبار یہ بھی ہے اور مرکبہ بھی لہذا مصنف نے اسی مناسبت سے مثال بھی ماہیت مرکبہ کی بیان کی (ولا ضرر فیہ ) یہ جواب تسلیمی ہے ۔ علی ان الحق سے، جواب (۲) ہے کہ ہم تسلیم ہی نہیں کرتے کہ ماہیت اعتبار یہ کی دو قسمیں ہیں بلکہ حق بات یہ ہے ماہیت اعتباریہ کی صرف ایک قسم ہے وہ ہے ماہیت مرکبہ اور ماہیات اعتبار یہ بسطہ کو ماہیات نہیں کہا جاتا بلکہ ان کو امور اعتبار یہ کہا جاتا ہے اور ان پر ماہیت کا لفظ بولا ہی نہیں جاتا متفکر ایھا الطالب فانه دقیق یہ جواب عدم تسلیمی ہے اذا تمهد هذا فنقول ما يتعقله الواضع الخ شارج تفتازانی کہتے ہیں اس تمہید کے بعد کہ ماہیت کی دو قسمیں ہیں (۱) ماہیت حقیقیہ (۲) اعتبار یہ ہم اپنے مقصد کو بیان کرتے ہیں کہ تعریف کی دو قسمیں ہیں ۔ ( تعریف حقیقی ) (۲) تعریف
It is not an interpretation or definition; rather it is a mention of one form among its several forms, one part among its parts, and an example. The definition of rational entailment ('ibna 'aqli) is something else: it is the ordering of one thing upon another by reason. Then it has multiple parts, e.g.: (1) ordering of effects upon causes, (2) entailment of metaphor upon reality, (3) entailment of particular rulings upon universal [principles], (4) entailment of actions upon sources, (5) entailment of a ruling upon its proof. So the author did not mention the definition of rational entailment from 'tartib al-hukm 'ala dalilihi'; rather he mentioned one part of its parts that was intended and meant here as an example. This is because in usul al-fiqh, 'usul' means proof; therefore, the entailment of a derivative upon its root enters into rational entailment. Question: If 'tartib al-hukm 'ala dalilihi' is made the definition of rational entailment, what flaw would follow? Answer: The commentator is removing this doubt: if 'tartib al-hukm 'ala dalilihi' is made the definition, then the definition of rational entailment would not be comprehensive, for entailment of metaphor upon reality, entailment of actions upon sources, entailment of effects upon causes, etc. — all these parts would be excluded from rational entailment, while they are all rational entailment. Therefore we do not make 'tartib al-hukm' a definition but rather an example. The comprehensive definition is: the ordering of one thing upon another by reason. (And Allah knows best what is correct.)

His statement: 'And know that the definition is either real...' The quiddity either has realization and existence independently of mental consideration, or not. The first is [real quiddity]. The author had mentioned two types of definition: real and verbal. Before explaining them, commentator Taftazani presents a preamble in which he explains the types of quiddity, because definition is only of quiddity, so before discussing definition it is necessary to know the definition and types of quiddity. Therefore he said: Quiddity is of two types: (1) real quiddity, (2) considerational quiddity. For every quiddity is either such that its realization, existence, and being in actual reality (nafs al-amr) occur regardless of whether anyone supposes it or reason considers it — i.e., it is not dependent on the consideration of any considerer or the supposition of a supposer — or its existence and being are dependent on the consideration of a considerer and the supposition of a supposer. The first is called real quiddity; 'haqiqiyyah' means 'established in actual reality'. The second is called considerational quiddity. That is, considerational quiddity is that which depends on the consideration of reason, like when a coiner considers several matters, compounds them, and coins a word for them. Examples of real quiddity: human, stone, tree, horse, cow, earth, sky, sun, moon, stars, etc. — all these exist and are realized in actual reality. But it is to be noted that actual reality is divided; it includes both what exists externally and what exists in the mind, though the theologians do not affirm mental existence. Commentator Taftazani gave multiple examples of considerational quiddity: (1) 'asl (root): the coiner coined it for such a thing in which the description of its being a basis for something else is considered; (2) fiqh: the coiner coined it for specific issues; (3) the word 'jins' (genus): the coiner coined it for the universal that is used in answer to 'what is it?'; (4) 'naw' (species): the coiner coined it for the universal that is used in answer to 'what is it?' for multiple things agreeing in realities. All these (asl, fiqh, jins, naw') are considerational quiddities. 'And it is necessary that some parts need others...' In this expression, commentator Taftazani wants to explain the difference between real and considerational quiddity. The difference is that real quiddity is of two types: (1) simple real quiddity, (2) composite real quiddity. The mark of composite real quiddity is that its parts need each other; otherwise the single reality cannot be realized. For example, the parts of human are animal and rational: animal needs rational for being a species, and rational needs animal for being a differentia. If there were no need, then it would be like placing a stone next to a tree. As for considerational quiddity, its parts do not need each other; rather, the coiner considers several matters, gathers them, and coins a name for them without any part needing another. The summary of the difference: the parts of composite real quiddity are mutually connected and need each other, whereas the composite parts of considerational quiddity do not need each other. 'And the illustration with composites does not negate...' With this expression, commentator Taftazani wants to answer an objection. The objection is: just as real quiddity is sometimes simple and sometimes composite, so too considerational quiddity has two types: (1) simple, (2) composite. But the author gave all examples of composite considerational quiddity. The text: 'as when we compound a thing from multiple matters...' So it appears that according to the author considerational quiddity is only specific to the composite; it is not simple. Answer (1): giving examples of composite considerational quiddity does not negate simple considerational quiddity. At most, the author would be open to the objection: why did he not mention an example of the simple? The response is that he specifically gave the example of the composite because it was appropriate to the context and purpose. For the purpose is to explain the quiddity of the word 'asl', and 'asl' is both considerational quiddity and composite. Therefore the author gave the example of composite quiddity accordingly. (And there is no harm in it.) This is a concessive answer. Answer (2): we do not concede that considerational quiddity has two types; rather the truth is that considerational quiddity has only one type, and that is composite. Simple considerational quiddities are not called quiddities; rather they are called considerational matters, and the term 'quiddity' is not applied to them. (Reflect, O student, for it is subtle!) This is a non-concessive answer. 'When this preamble is established, we say: what the coiner conceives...' Commentator Taftazani says: after this preamble that quiddity is of two types—(1) real quiddity, (2) considerational—we present our purpose: that definition is of two types: (1) real definition, (2) [verbal/considerational definition].
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage from the Sharh al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih addresses two interconnected topics: the correct understanding of 'rational entailment' ('ibna 'aqli) and the classification of definitions into real (haqiqi) and conventional (i'tibari). The commentary follows the order of the base text (Al-Tawdih) and expands upon it. The author first clarifies a common misunderstanding: the phrase 'ordering of a ruling upon its proof' is not the definition of rational entailment but merely one of its instances. The comprehensive definition is 'the ordering of one thing upon another by reason,' which encompasses many other instances such as the entailment of effects upon causes, metaphor upon reality, etc. This precision prevents the definition from being too narrow. The commentator then moves to a preparatory discussion on quiddity (mahiyyah), since definitions are of quiddities. He distinguishes between real quiddity (which has existence in actual reality independent of mental consideration) and considerational quiddity (which depends on mental composition, like terms coined by a lexicographer). Real quiddity can be simple or composite, its parts necessarily interdependent. Considerational quiddity, however, is always composite according to the preferred position; its parts are assembled by the coiner without mutual need. The commentator responds to a potential objection that considerational quiddity could be simple, offering two answers: a concessive one (the author's examples suit the context) and a non-concessive one (simple considerational items are not called quiddities at all). The passage exemplifies the rigorous analytical method of Hanafi usul al-fiqh, where definitions and classifications are carefully delineated to avoid conceptual confusion.

Key Terms
ابتنا عقلی (ibna 'aqli): The ordering of one thing upon another by reason
ترتب الحکم علی دلیلہ (tartib al-hukm 'ala dalilihi): Ordering of a ruling upon its proof

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the correct definition of 'rational entailment' ('ibna 'aqli)?
A) The ordering of a ruling upon its proof.
B) The ordering of one thing upon another by reason.
C) The entailment of metaphor upon reality.
D) The ordering of effects upon causes.

Key Takeaway
Rational entailment is a broad concept; its definition must be comprehensive and not restricted to one instance.
