Real and Considerational Quiddity in Hanafi Usul
Classifying quiddity into real and considerational, with emphasis that considerational quiddity is composite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real quiddity (mahiyyah haqiqiyyah) and considerational quiddity (mahiyyah i'tibariyyah), and explain why Hanafi usul holds that considerational quiddity is always composite.

Core Idea
Considerational quiddity is always composite, because simple mental constructs are not called quiddities.

Before defining definitions, the commentator clarifies the nature of quiddity (mahiyyah). This lesson unpacks the two types: real quiddity, which exists independently of the mind, and considerational quiddity, which depends on mental composition. A key point of Hanafi usul is that considerational quiddities are always composite, unlike in the Mutakallimin school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of quiddity
The commentator states that every quiddity is either independent of mental consideration (real) or dependent on it (considerational). First, memorize these two categories.
This is the foundational taxonomy.
2
Analyze real quiddity
Real quiddity exists in actual reality (nafs al-amr). It includes both external and mental existents. Understand that its parts (if composite) are mutually interdependent.
Example: 'human' is a composite of animal and rational that need each other.
3
Analyze considerational quiddity
Considerational quiddity is created by the coiner (wadi') who assembles several aspects and coins a term. Its parts do not need each other. The Hanafi position insists it is always composite; simple mental constructs are called 'considerational matters' (umur i'tibariyyah), not quiddities.
Examples: 'asl' (root), 'fiqh', 'jins' (genus), 'naw'' (species).
4
Understand the objection and responses
An objection arises: why not allow simple considerational quiddities? The commentator gives two answers: a concessive one (the author's examples fit the context) and a non-concessive one (simple are not quiddities). Grasp the stronger second answer.
This step sharpens your understanding of the school's terminology.

مثال کیوں نہیں ذکر کی تو اس کا جواب دیا جا سکتا ہے کہ ماہیت مرکبہ کی خاص کر مثال اس لیے بیان کی کیونکہ وہ مقام اور مقصود کے مناسب تھی کیونکہ مقصود لفظ اصل کی ماہیت کو سمجھانا ہے اور اصل ماہیت اعتبار یہ بھی ہے اور مرکبہ بھی لہذا مصنف نے اسی مناسبت سے مثال بھی ماہیت مرکبہ کی بیان کی (ولا ضرر فیہ ) یہ جواب تسلیمی ہے ۔ علی ان الحق سے، جواب (۲) ہے کہ ہم تسلیم ہی نہیں کرتے کہ ماہیت اعتبار یہ کی دو قسمیں ہیں بلکہ حق بات یہ ہے ماہیت اعتباریہ کی صرف ایک قسم ہے وہ ہے ماہیت مرکبہ اور ماہیات اعتبار یہ بسطہ کو ماہیات نہیں کہا جاتا بلکہ ان کو امور اعتبار یہ کہا جاتا ہے اور ان پر ماہیت کا لفظ بولا ہی نہیں جاتا متفکر ایھا الطالب فانه دقیق یہ جواب عدم تسلیمی ہے اذا تمهد هذا فنقول ما يتعقله الواضع الخ شارج تفتازانی کہتے ہیں اس تمہید کے بعد کہ ماہیت کی دو قسمیں ہیں (۱) ماہیت حقیقیہ (۲) اعتبار یہ ہم اپنے مقصد کو بیان کرتے ہیں کہ تعریف کی دو قسمیں ہیں ۔ ( تعریف حقیقی ) (۲) تعریف
It is not an interpretation or definition; rather it is a mention of one form among its several forms, one part among its parts, and an example. The definition of rational entailment ('ibna 'aqli) is something else: it is the ordering of one thing upon another by reason. Then it has multiple parts, e.g.: (1) ordering of effects upon causes, (2) entailment of metaphor upon reality, (3) entailment of particular rulings upon universal [principles], (4) entailment of actions upon sources, (5) entailment of a ruling upon its proof. So the author did not mention the definition of rational entailment from 'tartib al-hukm 'ala dalilihi'; rather he mentioned one part of its parts that was intended and meant here as an example. This is because in usul al-fiqh, 'usul' means proof; therefore, the entailment of a derivative upon its root enters into rational entailment. Question: If 'tartib al-hukm 'ala dalilihi' is made the definition of rational entailment, what flaw would follow? Answer: The commentator is removing this doubt: if 'tartib al-hukm 'ala dalilihi' is made the definition, then the definition of rational entailment would not be comprehensive, for entailment of metaphor upon reality, entailment of actions upon sources, entailment of effects upon causes, etc. — all these parts would be excluded from rational entailment, while they are all rational entailment. Therefore we do not make 'tartib al-hukm' a definition but rather an example. The comprehensive definition is: the ordering of one thing upon another by reason. (And Allah knows best what is correct.)

His statement: 'And know that the definition is either real...' The quiddity either has realization and existence independently of mental consideration, or not. The first is [real quiddity]. The author had mentioned two types of definition: real and verbal. Before explaining them, commentator Taftazani presents a preamble in which he explains the types of quiddity, because definition is only of quiddity, so before discussing definition it is necessary to know the definition and types of quiddity. Therefore he said: Quiddity is of two types: (1) real quiddity, (2) considerational quiddity. For every quiddity is either such that its realization, existence, and being in actual reality (nafs al-amr) occur regardless of whether anyone supposes it or reason considers it — i.e., it is not dependent on the consideration of any considerer or the supposition of a supposer — or its existence and being are dependent on the consideration of a considerer and the supposition of a supposer. The first is called real quiddity; 'haqiqiyyah' means 'established in actual reality'. The second is called considerational quiddity. That is, considerational quiddity is that which depends on the consideration of reason, like when a coiner considers several matters, compounds them, and coins a word for them. Examples of real quiddity: human, stone, tree, horse, cow, earth, sky, sun, moon, stars, etc. — all these exist and are realized in actual reality. But it is to be noted that actual reality is divided; it includes both what exists externally and what exists in the mind, though the theologians do not affirm mental existence. Commentator Taftazani gave multiple examples of considerational quiddity: (1) 'asl (root): the coiner coined it for such a thing in which the description of its being a basis for something else is considered; (2) fiqh: the coiner coined it for specific issues; (3) the word 'jins' (genus): the coiner coined it for the universal that is used in answer to 'what is it?'; (4) 'naw' (species): the coiner coined it for the universal that is used in answer to 'what is it?' for multiple things agreeing in realities. All these (asl, fiqh, jins, naw') are considerational quiddities. 'And it is necessary that some parts need others...' In this expression, commentator Taftazani wants to explain the difference between real and considerational quiddity. The difference is that real quiddity is of two types: (1) simple real quiddity, (2) composite real quiddity. The mark of composite real quiddity is that its parts need each other; otherwise the single reality cannot be realized. For example, the parts of human are animal and rational: animal needs rational for being a species, and rational needs animal for being a differentia. If there were no need, then it would be like placing a stone next to a tree. As for considerational quiddity, its parts do not need each other; rather, the coiner considers several matters, gathers them, and coins a name for them without any part needing another. The summary of the difference: the parts of composite real quiddity are mutually connected and need each other, whereas the composite parts of considerational quiddity do not need each other. 'And the illustration with composites does not negate...' With this expression, commentator Taftazani wants to answer an objection. The objection is: just as real quiddity is sometimes simple and sometimes composite, so too considerational quiddity has two types: (1) simple, (2) composite. But the author gave all examples of composite considerational quiddity. The text: 'as when we compound a thing from multiple matters...' So it appears that according to the author considerational quiddity is only specific to the composite; it is not simple. Answer (1): giving examples of composite considerational quiddity does not negate simple considerational quiddity. At most, the author would be open to the objection: why did he not mention an example of the simple? The response is that he specifically gave the example of the composite because it was appropriate to the context and purpose. For the purpose is to explain the quiddity of the word 'asl', and 'asl' is both considerational quiddity and composite. Therefore the author gave the example of composite quiddity accordingly. (And there is no harm in it.) This is a concessive answer. Answer (2): we do not concede that considerational quiddity has two types; rather the truth is that considerational quiddity has only one type, and that is composite. Simple considerational quiddities are not called quiddities; rather they are called considerational matters, and the term 'quiddity' is not applied to them. (Reflect, O student, for it is subtle!) This is a non-concessive answer. 'When this preamble is established, we say: what the coiner conceives...' Commentator Taftazani says: after this preamble that quiddity is of two types—(1) real quiddity, (2) considerational—we present our purpose: that definition is of two types: (1) real definition, (2) [verbal/considerational definition].
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage from the Sharh al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih addresses two interconnected topics: the correct understanding of 'rational entailment' ('ibna 'aqli) and the classification of definitions into real (haqiqi) and conventional (i'tibari). The commentary follows the order of the base text (Al-Tawdih) and expands upon it. The author first clarifies a common misunderstanding: the phrase 'ordering of a ruling upon its proof' is not the definition of rational entailment but merely one of its instances. The comprehensive definition is 'the ordering of one thing upon another by reason,' which encompasses many other instances such as the entailment of effects upon causes, metaphor upon reality, etc. This precision prevents the definition from being too narrow. The commentator then moves to a preparatory discussion on quiddity (mahiyyah), since definitions are of quiddities. He distinguishes between real quiddity (which has existence in actual reality independent of mental consideration) and considerational quiddity (which depends on mental composition, like terms coined by a lexicographer). Real quiddity can be simple or composite, its parts necessarily interdependent. Considerational quiddity, however, is always composite according to the preferred position; its parts are assembled by the coiner without mutual need. The commentator responds to a potential objection that considerational quiddity could be simple, offering two answers: a concessive one (the author's examples suit the context) and a non-concessive one (simple considerational items are not called quiddities at all). The passage exemplifies the rigorous analytical method of Hanafi usul al-fiqh, where definitions and classifications are carefully delineated to avoid conceptual confusion.

Key Terms
ماہیت حقیقیہ (mahiyyah haqiqiyyah): Quiddity that has realization in actual reality (nafs al-amr) independent of mental consideration
ماہیت اعتباریہ (mahiyyah i'tibariyyah): Quiddity that depends on mental consideration, such as coined terms (mawdu') with no external existence
نفس الامر (nafs al-amr): Actual reality; includes both external and mental existence (according to Hanafi/Maturidi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the consequence of saying that considerational quiddity can be simple?
A) It would be correct and fully accepted.
B) It would be rejected because all quiddities must be composite.
C) It would be a valid alternative view.
D) The commentator does not address this.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, considerational quiddity is always composite; simple mental constructs are not quiddities.

intermediate
8 min
Real vs. Conventional Quiddity in Hanafi Usul
Differentiating real and conventional quiddities and their composites

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish real quiddity from conventional quiddity and explain why conventional quiddity is always composite according to Taftazani.

Core Idea
Conventional quiddities are always composite; their parts need not be interdependent.

In Hanafi usul, definitions are either real or conventional. Real quiddities like 'human' have interdependent parts (genus and differentia). Conventional quiddities like 'fiqh' are constructs assembled by the jurist. This lesson explores Taftazani's distinction and his defense of why only composite conventional quiddities exist.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of quiddity
From the passage, recognize that real quiddity (ḥaqīqiyya) and conventional quiddity (iʿtibāriyya) are two categories. Real quiddity can be simple or composite; conventional quiddity is only composite.
Conventional quiddity's composite nature is central.
2
Understand the sign of composite real quiddity
Real quiddity parts are interdependent: e.g., 'animal' needs 'rational' to form 'human'. This mutual dependence is the marker.
Without interdependence, the composite fails to be a single reality.
3
Understand conventional quiddity's independence
Conventional quiddity parts are independent; the positor collects several considerations and assigns a label without requiring any part to need another.
Example: 'aṣl' combines Quran, Sunnah, etc., as independent sources.
4
Analyze the objection and Taftazani's responses
The objection: why only composite examples for conventional quiddity? Taftazani gives two answers: (1) concessional – examples suit the purpose; (2) non-concessional – simple conventional notions are not quiddities at all.
The non-concessional answer is aligned with the definition.
5
Distinguish between the two answers
The concessional answer admits possibility of simple conventional quiddities but says it's irrelevant; the non-concessional denies them entirely. Understand which is adopted.
Taftazani favors the non-concessional view: conventional quiddity is always composite.

ماھو پر بولی جاتی ہے۔ یہ سب ( اصل ، فقہ، جنس، نوع ) ماہیات اعتبار یہ ہیں ۔ ولا بد فيها من احتياج بعض الاجزاء ال عبارت میں شارح تفتازانی ماہیت حقیقیہ و ماہیت اعتباریہ میں فرق بیان کرنا چاہتے ہیں فرق یہ The sign of composite real quiddity is that its parts are dependent on each other; otherwise a single reality cannot be verified. For example, the parts of human are 'animal' and 'rational'. So 'animal' in being a genus needs 'rational', and 'rational' in being a differentia needs 'animal'. If there were no dependency, then its example would be like placing a stone next to a tree. But the author gave all examples of composite conventional quiddity. See the expression: 'As when we compose a thing from several matters etc.' From this it is understood that according to the author, conventional quiddity is specific to composite only, not simple. At most, the objection necessary against the author is: why did he not mention an example of the simple? To this it can be answered that he specifically gave the example of composite because it was suitable to the place and purpose, since the purpose was to explain the quiddity of the word 'asl', and 'asl' is both conventional and composite. Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage defines and contrasts real quiddity (māhiyya ḥaqīqiyya) and conventional quiddity (māhiyya iʿtibāriyya), key terms in Hanafi uṣūl. Real quiddity, like 'human', has interdependent parts: genus and differentia must be related. Conventional quiddity, like 'fiqh' or 'aṣl', is a construct: the jurist selects multiple considerations and assigns a label without requiring internal dependency. The commentator Taftazānī then addresses an objection: why did the author (likely Ṣadr al-Sharīʿa) only give examples of composite conventional quiddities, implying no simple conventional quiddity? Two answers are offered: firstly, it is simply a matter of relevance; secondly, on the correct view, conventional quiddity is always composite—simple conventional notions are not quiddities at all. This nuance is foundational for understanding how definitions work in uṣūl.

Key Terms
الماهية الحقيقية (al-māhiyya al-ḥaqīqiyya): A quiddity whose parts (if composite) are interdependent, representing an actual essence.
الماهية الاعتبارية (al-māhiyya al-iʿtibāriyya): A quiddity constructed by the positor from independent considerations, not reflecting a real essence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani's non-concessional answer, what is the correct classification of a simple conventional notion like 'whiteness'?
A) Simple conventional quiddity
B) Conventional matter (amr iʿtibārī), not a quiddity
C) Real quiddity
D) Conventional quiddity with one part

Key Takeaway
Conventional quiddities are always composite; their parts are independent and assembled by the positor.

Resolving Apparent Contradictions: Real and Nominal Definitions
Taftazani resolves an apparent contradiction about definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Taftazani's method of ta'wil and aspect-based distinction to resolve apparent contradictions between statements about definitions in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
One definition can be both real and nominal—depending on whether the question is about reality or lexical meaning.

This passage from Taftazani's commentary on Al-Tawdih addresses an apparent contradiction: Sadr al-Shariah's words suggest essential realities can only be defined really, while Taftazani earlier stated they can be both real and nominal. Using ta'wil and aspect-based reasoning, Taftazani shows how the contradiction dissolves. This lesson sharpens your ability to handle textual apparent conflicts in usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contradiction
The objector claims that Taftazani's statement (definitions of essential realities can be real or nominal) contradicts the author's statement (definitions of essential realities are only real, like contingent realities are only nominal). Recognize the source of tension: the author's comparison seems to imply exclusivity.
This is a common type of usul problem: two texts that appear to conflict.
2
Examine Taftazani's first response: ta'wil
Taftazani offers three interpretations (ta'wilat) of the author's words to harmonize them: (1) The author meant 'from the aspect of being essential realities, not from the aspect of being concepts for names'; (2) The author meant 'usually' not universally; (3) The author was giving an example, not implying exclusivity.
Ta'wil is a legitimate method to reconcile texts when the literal meaning leads to contradiction.
3
Examine the second response: tahqiq (investigation)
If one insists on the literal meaning without ta'wil, then the author's statement would be contrary to the truth. Taftazani asserts the correct position: essential realities have two aspects—as realities in themselves (real definition) and as meanings of names (nominal definition).
This is a stronger response: it does not reinterpret the author but declares the author's literal statement to be inaccurate.
4
Understand the two aspects of essential realities
Essential realities can be considered: (a) as quiddities established in themselves—then definition answers 'ma haqiqiyya' (real). (b) as concepts the positor had in mind—then definition answers 'ma sharhah' (nominal). The same thing can be defined both ways depending on the question.
This distinction is key: the definition's type follows the aspect under which the thing is considered.
5
Apply the principle of aspect-based distinction
Whenever you encounter an apparent contradiction regarding definitions, check whether the two statements are about different aspects (i'tibar) of the same thing. If so, they can both be true. Taftazani's method exemplifies this usul principle: al-ta'arud yuzalu bi ta'yin al-jihah.
This principle is broader than definitions; it applies to many usul conflicts.

و الحلوي

ماهيات الخ غرض شارح جواب سوال ہے۔ سوال ! یہ ہوتا ہے آپ کی عبارت اور مصنف کی عبارت میں تعارض ہے کیونکہ آپ کی عبارت سے ثابت ہوا کی ماہیات حقیقیہ کی تعریف کبھی حقیقی ہوتی ہے کبھی اسمی اور مصنف کی عبارت مشعر ہے کہ ماہیات حقیقیہ کی تعریف صرف حقیقی ہوگی جیسا کہ ماہیات اعتباریہ کی تعریف صرف اسمی ہوتی ہے مشعر ہونے کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ مصنف تعریف حقیقی کو ماہیات حقیقیہ کے مقابلہ میں لائے ہیں۔ اور تعریف اسمی کو ماہیات اعتباریہ کے مقابلہ میں اور ماہیات اعتباریہ کی تعریف صرف اسی ہی ہوتی ہے لہذا اس تقابل سے معلوم ہوا کہ ماہیت حقیقیہ کی تعریف بھی صرف حقیقی ہوگی اسی نہیں ہوگی حالانکہ آپ نے کہا کہ ماہیت حقیقیہ کی تعریف حقیقی بھی ہوتی ہے اور ابھی بھی تو آپ دونوں بزرگوں کے اقوال میں تعارض و تخالف ہے۔ قلت في العدول عن ظاهر العبارة سعة الخ شارج تفتازانی اس اشکال کے دو جواب دے رہے ہیں، جواب اول یہ ہے کہ اگر چہ ظاہر عبارت مصنف اسی طرف مشعر ہے کہ ماہیات حقیقیہ کی تعریف حقیقی ہی ہوتی ہے لیکن ظاہر سے عدول کیا جا سکتا ہے اور اس میں تاویل کی گنجائش موجود ہے تین قسم کی تاویلات ہوسکتی ہیں (۱) یوں کہا جائے کہ یہاں قید حیثیت معتبر ہے تقدیر عبارت یوں ہو گی تعریف الماہیات الحقیقیة من حيث انها ماہیات حقیقیہ لامن حیث اٹھا مفہومات و موضوعات للاسم لہذا تعارض ختم ہو گیا کیونکہ مصنف کی عبارت کا بھی یہی مطلب ہے کہ ماہیات محذوف ہے مراد مصنف یہ ہے کہ اکثر تعریف حقیقی ہوتی ہے (لا علی سبیل الاطراد والکلیہ اور کبھی تعریف اسمی بھی ہوتی ہے (۳) یا یوں تاویل کی جائے ه كتعريف الماهيات الحقيقية سے مقصود مصنف تعریف حقیقی کی مثال سب سے پہلے ما شارحہ کے ساتھ کسی اسم کی تفسیر اور اس کا معنی و مفہوم معلوم کیا جاتا ہے پھر ھل بسیطہ کے ذریعہ اس اسم کے مسمی و معنی او مفہوم کے وجود کو طلب کیا جاتا ہے پھر ما حقیقیہ کے ذریعہ اس مسمی کی حقیقت و ماہیت طلب کی جاتی ہے یہ تفصیل تو اس صورت میں ہے کہ ماہیات حقیقیہ کو اسماء کے سمی ہونے کی حیثیت سے لیا جائے (۲) دوسری صورت یہ ہے کہ ماہیات حقیقیہ کو اس حیثیت سے لیا جائے کہ وہ اسم کا معنی اور مفہوم ہیں اور واضع کا متعقل ہیں جس وقت وہ ان کو وضع کر رہا تھا تو اس حیثیت سے جو ماہیت حقیقیہ کی تعریف ہوگی وہ اسمی ہی ہوگی کیونکہ یہ ما شارجہ کے جواب میں واقع ہوگی جو مفہوم اسم یا متعقل واضع کو طلب کرنے کیلئے استعمال ہوتا ہے تو اس تحقیق سے یہی بات ثابت ہوئی کہ ماہیات حقیقیہ کی تعریف کبھی حقیقی ہوتی ہے اور کبھی اسمی ہوتی ہے اگر حقیقت کے بارے میں سوال ہوا تو جواب میں تعریف حقیقی آئے گی اگر مفہوم و معنی لغوی کے بارے میں سوال ہوا تو جواب میں تعریف اسمی آئے گی- وهذا التعريف قد يكون نفس حقيقة ذالك الشئى الخ یہاں سے شارح تفتازانی یہ بیان فرمارہے ہیں کہ کبھی تعریف حقیقی و اسمی متحد ہو جاتی ہیں اور تعریف اسمی کبھی کبھی اس شئی کی نفس حقیققت اور عین حقیقت ہوتی ہے بایں طور کہ واضع کا متعقل متصور نفس حقیقت و ماہیت ہوتی ہے اور کبھی نفس حقیقت نہیں ہوتی بلکہ مفہوم و معنی ہوتا ہے مثلاً اگر متعقل واضع نفس حقیقت انسان ہو تو اس کا ذکر بایں حیثیت کہ یہ سٹی ومفہوم موضوع لہ ہے تو یہ تعریف اسمی ہوگی اور بائیں حیثیت کہ یہ حقیقت انسانی ہے تو یہ تعریف حقیقی ہے اسی بناء پر محققین نے تصریح کی ہے کہ ایک ہی چیز تعریف اسمی بھی ہو سکتی ہے اور تعریف حقیقی بھی دونوں ایک لفظ میں جمع ہوسکتی ہیں مثلا ایک چیز کی تعریف اس کے وجود کے علم و تصدیق سے قبل کی جائے تو وہ تعریف اسمی ہوگی اور یہی تعریف اسمی بعد العلم بوجود الشئی تعریف حقیقی بن جائے گی مثلاً علم ہندسہ کے مبادی میں مثلث کی تعریف کی جائے ھوشکل محیط بہ ثلث اضلاع (ای ثلاث خطوط ) تو یہ تعریف آسی ہوگی اور پھر جب اس کو مثلث کا وجود بالدلیل معلوم ہو گیا تو اب یہی تعریف اسمی بعینہ تعریف حقیقی بن جائے گی۔ فوائد متفرقہ ! (۱) فائدہ بحث تعریف حقیقی واسی میں چند امور کا لحاظ کرنا ضروری ہے اول بات یہ ہے کہ سب سے پہلے واضع قبل الوضع موضوع لہ کا تصور و تعقل فی الذہن کرتا ہے (یعنی اس کے معنی و مفہوم کو ذہن میں لے آتا ہے اُس کو مفہومات ثانیہ کہا جاتا ہے تا کہ اُس کے مقابلہ میں کوئی اسم وضع کرے ) (۲) پھر ایک اسم کو اس کے مقابلہ میں وضع کرتا ہے (۳) مسمی الاسم کو مفہوم اسم اور موضوع بھی کہا جاتا ہے (۴) ایک حقیقت و ماہیت مسمی الاسم ہوتی ہے پس تعریف مسمی الاسم من انه موضوع لہ و مفہوم له مع قطع النظر عن الماهية الحقیقیہ تعریف آئی ہے تو اسمیں مفہوم لغوی و معنی لغوی کو بیان کیا جاتا ہے اس کیلئے کتب لغت وضع کیے گئے ہیں یہی وجہ ہے کہ کتب لغت میں تعریفات انمی بیان کی جاتی ہیں اور اگر مسمی الاسم کی حقیقت کی تعریف کی جائے تو وہ تعریف حقیقی ہوتی ہے۔ فائدہ ! (۲) نضع بازا کھا اسما اس میں بحث ہے کہ تعریف حقیقی کیلئے وضع ضروری ہے یا نہ جمہور کے نزدیک تعریف حقیقی
Misk al-Malih. ... [Introduction] The purpose of the commentator is to answer the question. The question: There is contradiction between your statement and the author's statement. Because your statement proved that the definition of essential realities is sometimes real and sometimes nominal, while the author's statement indicates that the definition of essential realities is only real, just as the definition of contingent realities is only nominal. The indication is that the author compared real definition with essential realities, and nominal definition with contingent realities, and the definition of contingent realities is only nominal. Therefore, from this comparison it is known that the definition of essential reality will also be only real, not nominal, whereas you said that the definition of essential reality is both real and nominal. So there is contradiction and opposition between the statements of you both. I said: In deviating from the apparent meaning of the text there is scope. The commentator Taftazani gives two answers to this objection. The first answer: Although the apparent wording of the author indicates that the definition of essential realities is only real, but deviation from the apparent is possible, and there is room for interpretation. Three possible interpretations: (1) It can be said that here the condition of aspect is considered; the implied phrase would be: definition of essential realities from the aspect that they are essential realities, not from the aspect that they are concepts and subjects for a name. Thus the contradiction is removed. (2) Or it can be said that the word 'usually' is omitted; the author's intent is that most definitions are real, not universal and unrestricted, and sometimes a nominal definition occurs. (3) Or interpret that the author's intent in 'like the definition of essential realities' is to give an example of real definition; it does not prove exclusivity. The second answer: If no interpretation is made and the apparent is left as is, then the author's statement would not be according to investigation (tahqiq); it would be contrary to investigation. The correct position is that definition of essential realities is sometimes real and sometimes nominal because essential realities have two considerations and two aspects: (1) Sometimes essential realities are considered as they are realities of the named (musamma) and quiddities established in themselves. In this case, their definition is called real definition. (2) The second case is that essential realities are taken as meaning and concept of the name and what the positor (wadi') had in mind when he posited the name. In this aspect, the definition will be nominal. So from this investigation it is established that definition of essential realities is sometimes real and sometimes nominal. If the question is about reality, the answer is a real definition; if about lexical meaning, the answer is a nominal definition. And this definition may be the very reality of that thing. From here, the commentator Taftazani explains that sometimes real and nominal definitions become unified. A nominal definition is sometimes the very reality of that thing, as when the positor's conception is the reality itself, and sometimes it is not the reality but the meaning. For example, if the positor's conception is the reality of man, then mentioning it as concept and meaning posited for the name is a nominal definition, and as human reality is a real definition. Thus the scholars have stated that one thing can be both a nominal definition and a real definition, both can be combined in one expression. For example, a definition given before knowledge of the thing's existence is nominal, and after knowledge it becomes real. Example: in geometry, defining triangle as 'a figure enclosed by three sides' is nominal; when its existence is proven, the same definition becomes real. Miscellaneous benefits: (1) Benefit: In discussing real and nominal definitions, several matters must be considered: First, the positor before positing conceives the object of positing in the mind (i.e., brings its meaning to mind, called secondary concepts) in order to posit a name for it. (2) Then he posits a name for it. (3) The named (musamma) is also called the meaning of the name and the object. (4) There is a reality and quiddity of the named. So the definition of the named from the aspect that it is object and meaning, disregarding the essential reality, is nominal. In it, lexical meaning is explained; for this, lexicons are compiled. If the definition is of the reality of the named, it is real. (2) Benefit: [cut off] ... about whether a positing is necessary for real definition. According to the majority, real definition...
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from the commentary (sharh) of Taftazani on Al-Tawdih. It addresses an objection about an apparent contradiction between the commentator's statement and the original author (Sadr al-Shariah's) statement regarding definitions of essential realities (mahiyat haqiqiyya). The objector claims that Taftazani said definitions of essential realities can be both real (haqiqi) and nominal (ismi), whereas the matn author's words indicate that essential realities can only be defined really, just as contingent realities are only defined nominally. Taftazani first responds by offering three interpretations (ta'wilat) of the matn author's words to reconcile them: either the author meant definitions from the aspect of being essential realities (not as concepts for names), or he meant 'usually' rather than universally, or he was simply giving an example without implying exclusivity. Then Taftazani gives a second, stronger answer: if one insists on the literal meaning, then the matn author's statement is incorrect (khilaf al-tahqiq). The correct position is that essential realities have two aspects: as realities in themselves (yielding real definitions) and as meanings and concepts of names (yielding nominal definitions). He further explains that real and nominal definitions can coincide, and a single definition can be nominal before knowledge of existence and real after. The passage then lists miscellaneous benefits, emphasizing the role of the positor and the distinction between lexical and real definitions. Throughout, Taftazani demonstrates the Hanafi usul method of resolving apparent contradictions through ta'wil and distinguishing aspects.

Key Terms
تعریف حقیقی (ta'rif haqiqi): Definition that explains the reality/quiddity of a thing, given in response to a question about what the thing is in itself (ما حقیقیہ).
تعریف آسمی (ta'rif ismi): Definition that explains the lexical meaning or concept of a name, given in response to a question about what the word means (ما شارحہ).
ماہیات حقیقیہ (mahiyat haqiqiyya): Essential realities: quiddities that exist in themselves, such as the reality of humanity.
ماہیات اعتباریہ (mahiyat i'tibariyya): Contingent realities: quiddities that are mentally considered and have no external existence, like genus and species.
ما شارحہ (ma sharhah): A question word used to ask for the explanation of a word's meaning.
هل بسیطہ (hal basitah): A question asking about the existence of something (e.g., 'Does it exist?').
ما حقیقیہ (ma haqiqiyya): A question asking for the reality/quiddity of something (e.g., 'What is it?').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani, the definition of an essential reality becomes nominal when:
A) It is given in response to a question about the thing's reality (ما حقیقیہ).
B) It is given in response to a question about the name's meaning (ما شارحہ).
C) It is given after the existence of the thing is known.
D) It is given by the positor before positing the name.

Key Takeaway
Apparent contradictions in usul texts can be resolved by distinguishing aspects (i'tibar) and allowing for ta'wil.

Tard and Aks: The Two Pillars of a Sound Definition
Explaining the conditions of tard and aks in legal definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conditions of tard and aks to determine whether a definition is valid in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
A valid definition must exactly match its defined term in extension: no more, no less.

In this lesson, we explore two critical conditions of definition (hadd) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: tard and aks. Tard ensures that wherever the definition applies, the defined applies, preventing over-inclusiveness. Aks ensures that wherever the defined applies, the definition applies, preventing under-inclusiveness. Together, they guarantee a definition is both preventive (man') and comprehensive (jam').

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and its defined
Take the proposed definition (hadd) and the term it is supposed to define (mahdud).
2
Apply the tard condition
Check if every instance where the definition is true is also an instance of the defined. If not, the definition is not tardy (invalid due to over-inclusion).
E.g., defining 'man' as 'animal' fails tard because it includes horses.
3
Apply the aks condition
Check if every instance where the defined is true is also an instance of the definition. If not, the definition is not aksy (invalid due to under-inclusion).
E.g., defining 'man' as 'actually writing animal' fails aks because it excludes men who do not write.
4
Evaluate the definition
A valid definition must satisfy both tard and aks simultaneously. If either fails, the definition is invalid.
Remember: tard = exclusion of non-instances; aks = inclusion of all instances.

يجب في التعريف المساواة بين الحد والمحدود (۳) يجب ان لا يكون الحد اعّم من المحدود ولا اخص منه (۴) يجب في التعريف المنع والجمع طرد کا اصطلاحی معنی شارح نے یہ بیان کیا ۔ صدق المحدود على ما صدق عليه الحد مطردا كلیا یعنی جہاں جہاں حد کمی آتی ہے وہاں المحدود کا سچا آنا ای کلما صدق علیہ الحد سے اطرادکلی کی تفسیر و تشریح کی ہے کہ جہاں جہاں حد صادق آئے محدود بھی صادق آئے بعض حضرات نے طرد کی تعریف کی ہے کلما وجد الحد وجد المحدود - شارح تفتازانی فرماتے ہیں کہ اس تعریف کا حاصل بھی وہی ہے جو ہم نے بیان کیا ہے فبالا طراد سے خلاصہ بیان کر رہے ہیں که اطراد کی وجہ سے تعریف مانع عن دخول الغیر ہو جاتی ہے۔ کیونکہ طرد کا معنی ہے جہاں بھی تعریف صادق آ رہی ہے وہاں معرف بھی صادق آئے اگر کسی مقام پر حد تو صادق آ رہی ہے لیکن محدود و معرف صادق نہیں آرہا تو کہا جائے گا یہ تعریف مطرد نہیں کہ غیر افراد حد میں داخل ہو گئے تو تعریف مانع عن دخول الغیر نہیں ہوگی جیسے انسان کی تعریف کی جائے حیوان ماش کے ساتھ تو یہ تعریف مطرد و مانع نہیں کیونکہ حمار، وفرس، و بقر پر تعریف تو صادق آ رہی ہے لیکن معرف محدود (انسان) صادق نہیں آرہا۔ اما العكس فاخذ بعضهم الخ عکس کا اصطلاحی معنی بیان کر رہے ہیں عکس کے معنی میں دو قول ہیں، پہلا معنی العکس پر الف لام عوض عن المضاف الیہ ہے اور الکس کا معنی ہے عکس الطرد یعنی جو طرد کی تعریف کی گئی ہے اُس کا عکس پھر عکس کا ایک معنی عرفی ہے ایک منطقی ہے عکس عرفی کی تعریف یہ ہے جعل المحمول مـوضـوعــا والموضوع محمولا مع بقاء الكمية اى الكلية والجزئية) ومع بقاء الكيفية اى الايجاب والسلب ( عکس عرفی میں کمیت بھی تبدیل نہیں ہوتی کیفیت بھی تبدیل نہیں ہوتی اور عکس منطقی و اصطلاحی کی تعریف ہے جعل المحمول موضوعا والموضوع معمولاً مع بقاء الكيفية فقط لهذا عکس عرفی مو جبہ کلیہ کا موجبہ کلیہ آئے گا اور عکس منطقی موجبہ کلیہ کا موجبہ جزئیہ آئے گا کما یقال سے عکس عرفی کی دو مثالیں بیان کیں ایک وجود کی دوسری عدم وجود کی مثال اول کل انسان ضاحک و با العکس اس عکس سے عکس عرفی مراد ہے وہ یہاں ثابت ہے یعنی کل ضاحک انسان تو اصل کی طرح عکس بھی موجبہ کلیہ ہے۔ عدم وجود کی مثال کل انسان حیوان و لائکس یہاں عکس عرفی کی نفی ہے نہ ر بکس منطقی کی کیونکہ عکس عرفی آئیگا کل حیوان انسان یہ جھوٹ ہے تو قضیہ کا ذبہ بن جائے گا البتہ اس کا عکس منطقی آسکتا ہے وہ ہے بعض الحیوان انسانیہ قضیہ صادقہ ہے کیونکہ بعض افراد حیوان کیلئے انسانیت کو ثابت کیا گیا ہے۔ اب سوال یہ ہے کہ یہاں کون سائکس، مراد ہے عرفی یا منطقی تو بعض حضرات نے یہاں عکس سے کس عرفی مراد لیا ہے یہی وجہ ہے مصنف نے عکس ( جوطرد کے مقابل ہے) کی یہ تعبیر کی ہے کلما صدق علیہ الحد و صدق علیہ الحد کیونکہ مرد یہ تھا کما صدق علیہ الحد ، صدق
علیہ المحدود تو موضوع کو محمول اور محمول کو موضوع بنا دیا گیا مقصد یہ ہوگا جہاں جہاں محدود صادق آ رہا ہے وہاں حد بھی صادق آئے اگر کسی جگہ محدود تو صادق آ رہا ہے لیکن حد صادق نہیں آ رہی تو کہا جائے گا ہے چنانچہ فرمایا کہ اس کی تعریف اسمی اس لیے ہے کہ یہ تعریف اصل کے مفہوم لغوی کو بیان کر رہی ہے کہ لفظ اصل ایک ماہیت اعتبار یہ مرکبہ کیلئے وضع کیا گیا ہے وہ مرکب اعتباری ہے ایک شئی جس میں ابتناء الخیر علیہ کی صفت کا اعتبار کیا گیا ہے ( کما ھو راوی المصنف ) یا ایک نئی جس میں
It is required in definition that there be equality between the definition (hadd) and the defined (mahdud). (3) The definition must not be more general than the defined nor more specific. (4) In definition, prevention (man') and inclusion (jam') are required. The commentator explained the technical meaning of tard: the defined is true for everything the definition is true for, universally. That is, wherever the definition applies, the defined also applies. Some defined tard as: whenever the definition is found, the defined is found. Taftazani says the outcome is the same. By tard, the definition becomes preventive of entry of others. Example: if man is defined as 'walking animal', it is not tardy because it applies to donkey, horse, cow, but not to man. As for aks: there are two meanings. One is 'urfi (conventional) conversion: making the predicate subject and subject predicate while preserving quantity and quality. The other is mantiqi (logical) conversion: only preserving quality. Then two examples: 'Every man is laughing' converts to 'Every laughing thing is man' (true in 'urfi). 'Every man is animal' does not convert 'urfi to 'Every animal is man' (false), but logically converts to 'Some animal is man'. Then a question which aks is intended. Some take aks as the opposite of tard: instead of 'whenever the definition is found, the defined is found', it is 'whenever the definition is absent, the defined is absent'. Both interpretations amount to the same: that the definition must be comprehensive for all individuals of the defined. Then the text says: 'And there is no doubt that the definition of asl is a nominal definition because it is an explanation...'
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage from a Hanafi usul commentary explains the conditions for a valid definition (ta'rif), focusing on the technical terms tard (coextensive inclusion) and aks (coextensive exclusion). The commentator first states the rule that a definition must be equal to the defined—not broader nor narrower—and must both include all instances of the defined (jam') and exclude everything else (man'). Tard means that wherever the definition (hadd) is true, the defined (mahdud) is also true. If the definition applies to something that is not the defined, it fails tard—like defining man as 'walking animal' which also applies to donkeys, horses, and cows. Aks is then explained in two senses: conventional logical conversion (preserving both quantity and quality) and logical conversion (preserving only quality). The commentator clarifies that the intended aks in definition is that wherever the defined is true, the definition is also true—the converse of tard. If a definition fails aks, it is not comprehensive; it omits some instances of the defined. For example, defining man as 'necessarily existing animal' fails aks because all men are necessarily existing animals, but the definition also applies to non-men? Actually the example given: defining man as 'actually writing animal' is not comprehensive because not all men write. The passage ends by connecting this to the definition of 'asl (source) in usul, which is a nominal definition explaining the lexical meaning. This entire discussion is rooted in the Hanafi logical tradition, using examples from everyday life to illustrate precise conditions for a sound definition.

Key Terms
طرد (ṭard): A condition that wherever the definition (hadd) is true, the defined (mahdūd) is also true; ensures the definition excludes non-instances.
عكس (aks): A condition that wherever the defined (mahdūd) is true, the definition (hadd) is also true; ensures the definition includes all instances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the condition of 'tard' require in a definition?
A) The definition must be more general than the defined.
B) Whenever the definition is true, the defined must also be true.
C) The definition must be more specific than the defined.
D) Whenever the defined is true, the definition must also be true.

Key Takeaway
A valid definition in Hanafi usul must satisfy both tard (the definition applies to every instance of the defined) and aks (every instance of the defined is included in the definition), ensuring perfect extensional equality.

Dispute on Ṭard in Definition of Aṣl
Critique of Razi's definition of aṣl and Taftazani's objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the requirement of ṭard (universality) in definitions by analyzing the Hanafi-Mutakallim dispute over Razi's definition of 'aṣl.

Core Idea
Ṭard is required for complete definitions but not for nominal ones—the purpose of definition dictates its form.

This passage presents a foundational debate in usul al-fiqh: must every definition be mutṭarid (universally applicable) and māni' (exclusive)? The Hanafi commentator critiques Imam Razi's definition of 'aṣl as 'that which is needed' because it applies to causes not called 'aṣl. Taftazani objects, arguing that nominal definitions need not be fully universal. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to evaluate definitions and their role in analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
State the Hanafi position that ṭard is required for all definitions and Taftazani's objection that it is only necessary for complete definitions.
The dispute hinges on the purpose of definition.
2
Examine the classification of causes
List the five types of causes (material, formal, efficient, final, condition) and note that 'aṣl refers only to material cause.
This shows why Razi's definition is non-universal.
3
Analyze Taftazani's first proof
Consider the lexicography argument: dictionaries use broader definitions without criticism, proving ṭard is not required for nominal definitions.
This challenges the absolute necessity of ṭard.
4
Analyze Taftazani's second proof
Distinguish between complete and incomplete definitions; only complete definitions require ṭard. Incomplete definitions suffice for partial distinction.
Taftazani uses this to defend Razi's definition as incomplete.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response
Identify the inconsistency in Taftazani's own earlier statement that lack of ṭard makes a definition defective, pointing out a contradiction.
The thamarah al-khilaf: Razi's definition is rejected by Hanafis but accepted by Mutakallimun.

محدود صادق نہیں آتا کیونکہ ان کو اصل نہیں کہا جاتا لہذا بعض (۱۳۴) مسک الملیح شرح العوضي العلمي besturdubooks.wordp افراد ایسے پائے گئے ہیں جن پر حد و تعریف صادق ہے لیکن محدود و معرف صادق نہیں اس کو عدم اطراد کہا جاتا ہے لہذا ثابت ہو گیا کہ اصل کی تعریف محتاج الیہ کے ساتھ کرنا غیر مطر د اور دخول غیر سے مانع نہیں ہے وههنا بحث من وجوه یہاں سے شارح تفتازانی مصنف پر چند اشکالات کر رہے ہیں۔ الاول ! : شارح تفتازانی کے اس اشکال کے متعدد جوابات دیے گئے ہیں۔ جواب ! (۱) آپ کا یہ اشکال آپ ہی کے قول کے خلاف ہے کیونکہ چند سطور قبل آپ ہی کے الفاظ مبارکہ گذر چکے ہیں وهذا الا ظل لہ فی بیان فساد التعریف از عدم الاطراد مفسدله
Misk al-Maleeh (133) Explanation of al-[?] He mentioned under necessity, so Taftazani's objection is wrong. So as a whole, defining 'asl as 'that which is needed' is not universal. From here the commentator is explaining the objection of the author against Sahib al-Husul (Razi). Summary: Imam Razi defined 'asl as 'that which is needed', but this definition is not universal (mutṭard) and not exclusive (manī'), because the meaning of universal and exclusive is that wherever the definition applies, the defined also applies. This meaning is not present in Razi's definition, because there are many individuals to whom the definition applies, e.g., (1) efficient cause, (2) formal cause, (3) final cause, (4) condition. All these are needed; the definition applies to them, but the defined—the word 'asl—does not apply to them, because the word 'asl is only used for material cause. So it is not found that wherever the definition applies, the defined also applies. Therefore Razi's definition is not universal because the thing that something needs has five types. The commentator is presenting his evidence for the enumeration. Summary: That thing to which another thing is in need for its existence and realization is either internal to the thing or external. If internal, then two possibilities: either the thing needs it for potential existence or actual existence. If potential, it is called material cause, e.g., wood for a bed. If actual, it is called formal cause, e.g., the form of a bed for the bed. If the needed thing is external to the thing, then two possibilities: either it is that from which the thing comes into existence (efficient cause), e.g., the carpenter for the bed, or it is that for the sake of which the thing is brought into existence (final cause), e.g., sitting on the bed. If the needed thing is beyond these four, it is called condition, e.g., the carpenter's tools, the wood's suitability for the bed. These are the five types. Some have expressed the enumeration in another way: the cause (needed) is either prior to the effect or posterior. If prior, then either internal to the effect (material cause) or external (efficient cause). If posterior, then either internal (formal cause) or external (final cause). These are the four, and the author added condition to them, making five. The purpose is that only one of these five types is the one to which the defined ('asl) applies linguistically, and that is the material cause, e.g., 'the origin of this bed is such wood'. Apart from that, the definition (needed) applies to the remaining four types because they are all types of needed, but the defined does not apply to them because they are not called 'asl. So there exist some individuals to which the definition applies but the defined does not. This is called lack of universality. So it is proven that defining 'asl as 'that which is needed' is not universal and does not exclude others. Here there is a discussion from several aspects. Here the commentator Taftazani is making several objections against the author. The first: The summary of the first objection is that you considered Razi's definition defective because it is not universal and exclusive. Your objection hinges on the point that universality is a condition in every definition, whether real or nominal. We do not accept this; rather we claim that universality is not necessary in every definition, especially in nominal definitions it is absolutely not necessary. So if Razi's definition is not universal, that is not problematic. The commentator is presenting two proofs for the non-necessity of universality. (1) First proof from the books of language: In dictionaries there are many words whose definition is given with words broader than the defined, e.g., 'al-sa'd' is 'a plant', 'al-dhi'b' is 'a wild beast', etc. When the definition is more general than the defined, it becomes non-universal because necessarily individuals enter the definition that are not in the defined. Since dictionaries define words with broader terms, and no one criticizes these definitions and they accept their validity, it proves that in nominal definitions universality is not required. So your general rule that universality is required in every definition is invalid. (2) Second proof: The scholars have stated that there are two types of definition: (1) incomplete definition, (2) complete definition. In complete definition, universality is a condition, but in incomplete definitions it is not. This is why it is permissible in incomplete definitions that they be broader than the defined; in that case they are not exclusive. The benefit of incomplete definitions is that sometimes the purpose is not to distinguish the defined from everything else, but only from certain specific things. For the validity of an incomplete definition, distinguishing from some others is sufficient. For example, here our purpose is to distinguish 'asl from a specific other (far'), so we should define it in a way that distinguishes it from far', even if the definition does not exclude others, like efficient cause, formal cause, etc. So we define 'asl as 'that which is needed' and far' as 'that which needs'; these two will be distinguished from each other, and that is our purpose, even if the definition is not exclusive and not universal; it applies to many individuals beyond the defined. So from the scholars' explicit statements it is proven that universality and exclusivity are not necessary for every definition; they are only necessary for complete definitions, not for incomplete definitions. Response: To this objection of Taftazani, several answers have been given. Answer (1): This objection of yours contradicts your own statement, because a few lines earlier your own blessed words have passed: 'And this is the most apparent for him in showing the defectiveness of the definition due to lack of universality, making it defective.'
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a dispute in usul al-fiqh concerning the requirement of ṭard (universality) in definitions, specifically regarding the definition of the term 'aṣl (source) as 'that which is needed' (al-muḥtāj ilayh). The author critiqued Imam Razi's definition for being non-universal (ghayr muṭṭarid) and non-exclusive (ghayr māni'), meaning it includes things that are not called 'aṣl, like efficient cause, formal cause, final cause, and condition, whereas 'aṣl is only used for material cause. The commentator then clarifies the five types of causes: material (dākhil bil-quwwa), formal (dākhil bil-fi'l), efficient (khārij minhu al-wujūd), final (khārij lahu al-wujūd), and condition (mā warā' al-arba'a). Taftazani objects that ṭard is not a condition for all definitions, especially nominal ones, and that incomplete definitions suffice for distinguishing between related terms like 'aṣl and far'. He provides two proofs: (1) lexicographers define words with broader terms without criticism, and (2) scholars distinguish between complete and incomplete definitions; only complete ones require ṭard. The response notes that Taftazani's own earlier statement affirms that lack of ṭard makes a definition defective, contradicting his objection. This exemplifies a key usul principle: the purpose of definition determines its form; in Hanafi usul, nominal definitions may be non-exclusive when the goal is partial distinction.

Key Terms
اطراد (iṭṭirād): The property of a definition that wherever the definition applies, the defined also applies; universal applicability.
محتاج اليه (muḥtāj ilayh): That which something needs for its existence; it can be internal or external, and is divided into five types: material, formal, efficient, final cause, and condition.
تعريف ناقص (ta'rīf nāqiṣ): An incomplete definition that distinguishes the defined from some other things but not from all; does not require ṭard.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason Razi's definition of 'aṣl is considered defective by the Hanafi commentator?
A) It defines 'aṣl by its opposite
B) It applies to efficient cause, which is not called 'aṣl
C) It is too narrow
D) It uses metaphorical language

Key Takeaway
The definition of 'aṣl as 'that which is needed' fails to be muttarid because it includes causes not labeled 'aṣl, but Taftazani defends it as a valid nominal definition that serves partial distinction.

Refining the Definition of Fiqh: The 'Nafs' and 'Ma'rifah' Analysis
Definitional analysis with objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the definition of fiqh by distinguishing the two interpretations of 'nafs' and by defending the inclusion of the restriction 'from evidence' in 'ma'rifah'.

Core Idea
Fiqh is not mere knowledge but evidence-based perception; the definition must exclude blind imitation.

This passage from the Sharh al-Talwih delves into the precision of the definition of fiqh. The author examines two interpretations of 'nafs' (self) in Imam Abu Hanifa's definition and responds to an objection that the phrase 'from evidence' is unwarranted. Understanding this refinement is crucial for grasping the Hanafi usul insistence on independent reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of mudaf and mudaf ilayh
The mudaf (modified) is 'al-nafs' and mudaf ilayh (modifier) is 'fiqh'. Two definitions are given for each: one acceptable (from Abu Hanifa) and one spurious (attributed to Shafi'is). The author remains silent on the former, indicating acceptance.
This sets up the definitional refinement.
2
Understand the two interpretations of 'nafs'
Taftazani explains that 'nafs' can mean either the body-soul composite or just the soul. This affects the scope of fiqh: if body-soul, then fiqh covers bodily actions; if soul only, then fiqh addresses the rational soul that receives divine address.
Both interpretations are valid and complement each other.
3
Analyze the objection regarding 'ma'rifah'
The objector claims that the phrase 'from evidence' in the definition of ma'rifah has no basis in language or terminology. This is a methodological critique that could undermine the definition's validity.
The objection forces a defense of the essential nature of inferential knowledge in fiqh.
4
Evaluate the three responses defending the 'from evidence' restriction
The author provides three responses: (1) Linguistic basis from Raghib al-Isfahani's definition implying inference. (2) Terminological consensus that faqih refers only to mujtahid. (3) Practical necessity: knowledge of all rulings in detail is impossible without inference.
These responses solidify the Hanafi usul position that fiqh is inherently inferential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله نقل للمضاف تعريفين مقبولا ومزيفا الخ غرض شارح بیان فائدہ! مصنف نے مضاف (اصل) کی دو تعریفیں ذکر کیں (۱) مقبول (۲) غیر مقبول و مزیف اس طرح مضاف الیہ (فقہ) کی بھی دو تعریفیں ذکر کیں ان میں سے ایک کے ضعف اور غیر مقبول ہونے کی تصریح کردی ہے وہ تعریف وہ ہے جو اصحاب شافعی کی طرف منسوب ہے اور پہلی تعریف بنو امام صاحب سے منقول ہے اس کے بارے میں سکوت اختیار کیا ہے پھر اپنی طرف سے تیسری تعریف بیان کی۔ فالاول معرفة النفس مالها وما عليها . ويجوز ان يريد بالنفس غرض شارح تفتازانی ! تعریف اول کے بعض الفاظ کی توضیح و تشریح چنانچہ لفظ انفس کی تشریح کرتے ہوئے کہا کہ اس میں دو احتمال ہیں (۱) اس سے مراد ذات عبد ہو یعنی جسد مع الروح تو معرفہ النفس بمعنی معرفۃ العبد ہوگا کیونکہ اکثر احکام اعمال جسدی و بدنی سے متعلق ہوتے ہیں اور اعمال جسد بغیر روح حاصل نہیں ہو سکتے لہذا النفس سے جسد مع الروح دونوں کا مجموعہ مراد ہو گا (۲) احتمال دوم یہ ہے کہ نفس سے فقط روح اور نفس انسانی مراد ہو کیونکہ اسی کی وجہ سے اعمال وجود میں آتے ہیں اور خطاب اس کو ہوتا ہے کیونکہ ادراک و شعور و صلاحیت علم و فہم اس روح میں ہے بدن صرف آلہ ہے جس کے واسطہ سے انسان سے اعمال کا صدور ہوتا ہے۔ قوله وفسر المعرفة . والقيد الخير ممالا دلالة له الخ غرض شارح تفتازانی اعتراض علی المصنف ا تقریر اشکال یہ ہے کہ مائن نے معرفہ کی تعریف کی ہے ادراک الجزئیات عن دلیل اس میں جو آخری قید عن دلیل ذکر کی ہے یہ اپنی طرف سے ذکر کی ہے اس کا ثبوت نہ لغت میں ہے نہ اصطلاح میں اس پر کوئی دلیل موجود نہیں۔ جواب: اس اشکال کے متعدد جوابات دیے گئے ہیں (۱) یہ قید لخت ثابت ہے کیونکہ امام اللغة امام راغب اصفہانی نے معرفہ کی تعریف یوں کی ہے المعرفة والعرفان ادراک شنى بتفکر و تدبر لاثره بتفکر و تدبر سے استدلال اور ادراک عن دلیل ہی مراد ہے فلہذا یہ کہنا کہ یہ قید ثابت نہیں ہے غلط ہے (۲) فقہ علوم استدلالیہ میں سے ہے یہی وجہ ہے کہ فقیہ مجتہد کو کہا جاتا ہے مقلد کو فقیہ نہیں کہا جاتا تو اصطلاح میں بھی فقہ معرفۃ الاحکام عن دلیل کا نام ہے تو ولا اصطلاحا کہنا بھی غلط ہے (۳) مالها وما عليها سے مراد جمع احکام علی سبیل الاستغراق ہیں اور معرفہ جمیع احکام بالتفصیل بالفعل محال ہے لہذا ضروری ہے اس کے اندر استنباط واستدلال کی قید لگائی جائے۔
His statement: 'He transmitted for the mudaf two definitions, one acceptable and one spurious, etc.' The purpose of the commentator is to explain a benefit. The author mentioned two definitions for the mudaf (the original): (1) acceptable, (2) unacceptable and spurious. Similarly, for the mudaf ilayh (fiqh), he mentioned two definitions, and he specified that one of them is weak and unacceptable. That definition is the one attributed to the Shafi'is. The first definition is transmitted from Imam Abu Hanifa; regarding it, he remained silent. Then he himself presented a third definition. So the first definition is: 'Ma'rifah al-nafs ma laha wa ma alayha' (knowledge of the self: what is for it and what is against it). It is possible that by 'al-nafs' he intends... The purpose of the commentator Taftazani is to explain and clarify some words of the first definition. Thus, while explaining the word 'al-anfus', he said: There are two probabilities: (1) By it is meant the essence of the servant, i.e., the body with the soul, so 'ma'rifah al-nafs' would mean 'ma'rifah al-'abd' (knowledge of the servant) because most rulings pertain to bodily actions, and bodily actions cannot occur without the soul. Therefore, by 'al-nafs' is meant the combination of body and soul. (2) The second probability is that by 'nafs' is meant only the soul and the human self, because through it actions come into existence and the address (of revelation) is directed to it, since perception, awareness, and capacity for knowledge and understanding are in the soul. The body is merely an instrument through which actions emanate from the human being. His statement: 'And he explained al-ma'rifah... and the last restriction has no proof, etc.' The purpose of the commentator Taftazani is to object to the author. The explanation of the objection is that the author defined 'ma'rifah' as 'perception of particulars from evidence'. The last restriction 'from evidence' he mentioned on his own. Its proof is found neither in language nor in terminology; there is no evidence for it. Response: Several answers were given to this objection: (1) This restriction is firmly established because the linguist Imam Raghib al-Isfahani defined 'ma'rifah' and 'irfan' as 'perception of a thing through reflection and contemplation'. Through reflection and contemplation, what is meant is inference and perception from evidence. Therefore, saying that this restriction is not established is incorrect. (2) Fiqh is among the inferential sciences; this is why a mujtahid is called a faqih, while a muqallid is not called a faqih. So terminologically, fiqh is knowledge of rulings from evidence. Saying it is not established terminologically is also wrong. (3) 'Ma laha wa ma alayha' refers to the totality of rulings comprehensively, and knowing all rulings in detail actually is impossible; therefore, it is necessary to include the restriction of inference and deduction.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary (sharh) on Al-Tawdih, specifically explaining Taftazani's comments. The author notes that the original text (Tawdih) provided two definitions for the mudaf (the modified word, i.e., 'al-nafs' or the self) and for the mudaf ilayh (the modifier, i.e., 'fiqh'). One definition is acceptable (from Abu Hanifa) and one is spurious (attributed to Shafi'is). Then Taftazani clarifies the term 'al-nafs' by offering two interpretations: either the combination of body and soul, or just the soul as the locus of actions and address. This has implications for the definition of fiqh. Then Taftazani raises an objection: the author's definition of 'ma'rifah' as 'perception of particulars from evidence' includes the phrase 'from evidence' which is not found in linguistic or terminological definitions. The passage then provides three responses defending this restriction: (1) Raghib al-Isfahani's definition implies inference, (2) fiqh is an inferential science by consensus, (3) without inference, knowing all rulings is impossible. This shows the rigorous methodological approach of Hanafi usul, emphasizing that fiqh is not mere knowledge but knowledge derived through evidence.

Key Terms
مضاف (mudaf): The first part of a genitive construction; here refers to 'al-nafs' (the self) in the definition.
مضاف الیہ (mudaf ilayh): The second part of the genitive; here refers to 'fiqh' as the modified term.
نفس (nafs): The self; interpreted as either body+soul or soul alone in the definition of fiqh.
معرفة (ma'rifah): Knowledge or perception; in fiqh definition, it is qualified as 'perception from evidence'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the responses defending the 'from evidence' restriction in the definition of ma'rifah?
A) Linguistic basis from Raghib al-Isfahani's definition implying inference.
B) Terminological consensus that faqih refers only to mujtahid.
C) Practical necessity because knowing all rulings in detail is impossible without inference.
D) The restriction is found in the Quran and Sunnah explicitly.

Key Takeaway
The definition of fiqh must include 'from evidence' to exclude blind following and preserve the inferential nature of the discipline.

Resolving the Circularity Objection Between Sharia and Iman
Response to an objection about circular dependency between sharia and iman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three aspects of iman (essence, obligation, knowledge) and apply the Hanafi condition for circularity (same direction of dependence) to refute claims of dawr between sharia and iman.

Core Idea
No circularity: sharia depends on essence of iman; obligation of iman depends on sharia – different directions break the circle.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection that his view on the relationship between sharia and iman leads to circular dependency. The objector argues that if sharia depends on iman and the obligation of iman depends on sharia, then a vicious circle occurs. The author clarifies that sharia depends on the essence of iman (nafs al-iman) not on its obligation, while the obligation of iman depends on sharia. Since the direction of dependence differs, no circularity occurs. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the Hanafi epistemological foundation of sharia.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three aspects of iman
The text lists nafs al-iman (essence), wujub al-iman (obligation), and ilm wujub al-iman (knowledge of obligation). Categorizing these is key.
2
Locate the objector's claim of circularity
The objector claims that if sharia depends on iman and the obligation of iman depends on sharia, that would be circular. Note the assumption that the same 'iman' is involved in both dependencies.
3
Apply the Hanafi distinction
The Hanafi author distinguishes between essence and obligation. Sharia depends on essence, not obligation; obligation depends on sharia. Thus there is no mutual dependence in the same respect.
4
Analyze the condition for circularity
The text states that for circularity, the direction of dependence must be identical: A depends on B and B depends on A in the same aspect. Here the aspects differ (essence vs obligation), so no circularity.
5
Evaluate the pronoun resolution by the later commentator
The later response reinterprets pronouns to show that examples of things not dependent on sharia refer to essences, not obligations, reconciling the author's text.

اس اشکال کا یہ جواب دیا گیا ہے کہ در اصل تفتازانی کو غلط فہمی ہو گئی وہ سمجھ بیٹھا ہے کہ وجوب ایمان وجوب تصدیق ما لا يتوقف على الشرع کی مثال ہیں حالانکہ یہ ما توقف على الشرع کی مثال ہیں البتہ آگے ونحوهما کی هما ضمیر نفس ایمان نفس تصدیق کی طرف راجع ہے اور لما لايتوقف على الشرع نحوهما کا بیان ہے اور لتوقف الشرع علیہ کی ضمیر بھی نفس ایمان کی طرف راجع ہے تو وجوب ایمان وجوب تصدیق شریعت پر موقوف ہیں یہ توقف کی مثال ہیں البتہ نفس ایمان نفس تصدیق شرع پر موقوف نہیں ہے توقف الشرع علیہ ای علی نفس الایمان (هذا الجواب لائج و ظاهر عبارۃ المصنف) فيلزم الدور فاندفع اشكال الشارح قوله ثم الشرعى اما نظرى
As for the circularity (dawr) due to the sharia's dependence on them (iman and tasdiq), an objector says: We do not accept circularity; there is no circularity here, because here on one side is iman in Allah and tasdiq of the Prophet, and on the other side is sharia. Then on the side of iman and tasdiq there are three possibilities: (1) the essence of iman in Allah and the essence of tasdiq of the Prophet, (2) the obligation of iman and the obligation of tasdiq of the Prophet, (3) knowledge of the obligation of iman and knowledge of the obligation of tasdiq of the Prophet. Now the author (Sadr al-Shariah) had said that the obligation of iman and the obligation of tasdiq of the Prophet are not dependent on sharia, because sharia itself is dependent on the obligation of iman and the obligation of tasdiq of the Prophet. If the obligation of iman were dependent on sharia, then circularity would occur. We (the objector) say: Your statement that sharia is dependent on the obligation of iman and the obligation of tasdiq of the Prophet is incorrect. Rather, the establishment of sharia—whether by sharia is meant the address of Allah or the law of the Prophet (peace be upon him)—is dependent on the essence of iman in Allah and His attributes, and on the essence of tasdiq of the prophethood of the Prophet (peace be upon him) through miraculous evidence. The essence of iman is one thing, and the obligation of iman and the obligation of tasdiq of the Prophet are another. From sharia's dependence on the essence of iman and the essence of tasdiq of the Prophet, it does not necessarily follow that sharia is also dependent on the obligation of iman and the obligation of tasdiq of the Prophet or on knowledge of the obligation of iman and knowledge of the obligation of tasdiq of the Prophet, because we have already stated that these are separate. In summary: Sharia is dependent on the essence of iman in Allah, so that which sharia depends on is the essence of iman and the essence of tasdiq. Then we say: The essence of iman and the essence of tasdiq of the Prophet are not dependent on sharia so that circularity would occur. Rather, the obligation of iman and the obligation of tasdiq of the Prophet are dependent on sharia, as is the doctrine of the scholars that the obligation of iman is not established without a revealed (sam'i) and legal (shar'i) proof. Therefore, when the essence of iman and the essence of tasdiq are not dependent on sharia, circularity does not occur, because for circularity it is a condition that the direction of dependence be the same. For example, if (a) is dependent on (b) and then (b) is dependent on (a), then circularity occurs. If the direction of dependence differs, circularity does not occur. If (a) is dependent on (b) and then (b) is dependent on (c), that is not called circularity. Here too the direction of dependence differs, because that which sharia depends on is the essence of iman, not the obligation of iman, and that which the obligation of iman depends on is sharia. Therefore, circularity does not occur. Circularity would occur only if that which sharia depends on were the obligation of iman and that which the obligation of iman depends on were sharia, but this is not the case. So the author's proof that circularity is necessary is false. Response: This objection was answered thus: In reality, Taftazani misunderstood. He thought that the obligation of iman and the obligation of tasdiq are examples of what is not dependent on sharia, whereas they are examples of what is dependent on sharia. However, the pronoun 'huma' in 'wa nahwihima' refers back to the essence of iman and the essence of tasdiq, and that is the description of 'ma la yatawaqqafu 'ala al-shar' wa nahwihima'. The pronoun in 'li tawaqqufi al-shar'i 'alayhi' also refers to the essence of iman. So the obligation of iman and the obligation of tasdiq are dependent on sharia—these are examples of dependence. However, the essence of iman and the essence of tasdiq are not dependent on sharia; sharia's dependence is upon them, i.e., upon the essence of iman. This response is clear and apparent from the author's expression. So circularity is necessary? No, it is refuted. The commentator's objection is repelled. Then his statement: 'Then the shar'i is either nazari...'
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a dispute about circularity (dawr) between sharia and iman. The objector argues that the author's claim of necessary circularity is false because the dependence is not mutual in the same respect. Sharia depends on the essence of iman (nafs al-iman), not on its obligation (wujub al-iman). Meanwhile, the obligation of iman depends on sharia. Since the directions differ, there is no vicious circle. The text clarifies three distinct aspects: essence, obligation, and knowledge of obligation. The response from the Hanafi side (attributed to Taftazani's misunderstanding) reinterprets the pronouns in the author's text to show that the examples of what is not dependent on sharia refer to the essence, not the obligation. Thus, the author's statement is consistent. The passage exemplifies how Hanafi usul handles foundational epistemological issues.

Key Terms
دور (dawr): Circular dependency where A depends on B and B depends on A in the same respect, leading to an infinite regress
نفس الإيمان (nafs al-iman): The essence of faith, as opposed to its obligation or knowledge thereof
وجوب الإيمان (wujub al-iman): The legal obligation of faith, which is established by revealed proof

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the condition for circularity (dawr) according to the passage?
A) Any mutual dependence
B) Dependence in the same respect
C) Dependence on different aspects
D) Dependence on knowledge

Key Takeaway
Circularity between sharia and iman is avoided by distinguishing the essence of iman (which sharia depends on) from its obligation (which depends on sharia).

Essential Accident: Direct, Equal, or via a More General Part
Defining mawdu', 'ard, 'ard dhati with six-fold classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify the three types of essential accident ('ard dhati) and distinguish them from accidental accidents ('ard gharib) according to the later Hanafi-logician classification.

Core Idea
An essential accident arises from a thing's essence, directly or through an equal or a more general part; all else is accidental.

In Usul al-Fiqh, understanding what qualifies as an 'essential accident' is crucial because the subject of a science is defined by the accidents studied. This passage, from a commentary on Al-Tawdih, defines the subject (mawdu') of a science, the accident ('ard), and then meticulously classifies essential accidents into three types based on how they relate to the essence. The classification follows the later logicians, broadening the essential category.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three key terms
The passage defines mawdu' (subject of a science), 'ard (accident), and 'ard dhati (essential accident). Understand each definition: mawdu' is the thing whose essential accidents are studied; 'ard is an external predicated state; 'ard dhati arises from the essence.
The definition of 'ard is absolute, covering both essential and accidental.
2
Analyze the three types of 'ard dhati
For each type, note the relationship: Type 1 – directly from essence (e.g., laughter for man). Type 2 – through an equal intermediary (e.g., laughter through wonder, both equal). Type 3 – through a more general part (e.g., movement through animal, which is more general and part of man).
The intermediary in type 3 is part of the essence (animal is a part of man's definition).
3
Map the six-fold classification of all accidents
The passage derives six possibilities based on the cause: essence, part, or external (equal, general, specific, opposite). First three are essential, last three accidental. Memorize this grid.
Later logicians treat 'more general' as essential if it is part of the essence; earlier logicians considered it accidental.
4
Note the later vs earlier classification difference
This passage follows later logicians (mutakhkhirun). Earlier logicians (mutaqaddimun) placed the 'more general' under accidental. This affects which attributes are considered essential to the subject of a science.
In usul al-fiqh, the subject is often the evidences (adillah), and essential accidents include properties like being qat'i or zanni.

Source Text

والمراد بالبحث عن الاعراض ذاتیہ سوال یہ ہوا کہ موضوع کی تعریف میں باحث فید من عوارضہ الذاتیہ ہے اس بحث سے کیا مراد ہے تو شارح اس کی وضاحت کرتے ہوئے کہتے ہیں کہ عوارض ذاتیہ سے بحث کرنے کا مقصد یہ ہے اصول فقہ میں جو مسائل ذکر کیے جاتے ہیں عوارض ذاتیہ کو ان مسائل کا محمول بنایا جائے گا سوال یہ ہوا کہ یہ عوارض ذاتیہ تو بہنیں گے مسئلہ کا معمول یہ بتائیے کہ مسئلہ کا موضوع کون بنے گا تو شارح اس کا جواب دے رہے ہیں کہ موضوع مسئلہ کے بارے میں چار صورتیں ہیں (1) کبھی مسئلہ کا موضوع خود موضوع علم ہو گا جیسے الكتاب يثبت الحکم قطعا یہاں اثبات حکم عوارض ذاتیہ میں سے ہے یہ معمول ہے الکتاب پر جو قضیہ و مسئلہ کا موضوع ہے اور یہی
His statement: 'So the subject of this science' – the intended meaning of subject of a science. The commentator Taftazani is defining the subject: the subject of a science is the thing whose essential accidents are investigated in that science. For example, the subject of medicine is the human body, because in it the essential accidents of the human body are investigated. And 'by accident here' – in this phrase the definition of accident is given. The definition of absolute accident, whether essential accident or accidental accident, is that it is something external to the essence of a thing and predicated upon that thing. So an accident necessarily has two things: first, it is external to the essence of the thing; second, it is predicated upon that thing. In simple words, accident is called a state of something. And with essential accident – here the definition of essential accident is explained: essential accident is that which affects a thing due to its essence, i.e., the thing's own essence is the origin, and it affects the thing essentially without intermediary. For example, laughter affects man without intermediary – this is the first type of essential accident. Or essential accident is that which affects a thing with an intermediary, and then there is a relation of equality between the intermediary and the subject. For example, laughter affects man through wonder, and wonder and man have a relation of equality – it is said 'every man is wondrous' and 'every wondrous thing is a man'. This is the second type. Or essential accident is that which affects a thing with an intermediary, and the intermediary and the subject have a relation of generality and specificity: the intermediary is more general than the subject but enters into its essence, being a part of it. For example, movement affects man through animal; animal is more general than man and is a part of him. This is the third type. Benefit: There are two types of accidents: essential accidents and accidental accidents. Then each has three types. Reason for enumeration: when something affects another thing, it is either because of its essence (1), or because of a part (2), or because of an external cause. Then if due to an external cause, that external cause is either equal to the thing (3), or more general than it (4), or more specific than it (5), or opposite to it (6). The first three are essential accidents, and the last three are accidental accidents. (1) Example of first type: wonder affects man essentially. (2) Example of second: movement affects man because he is an animal, and animal is also man. (3) Example of third: laughter affects man through wonder, wonder is equal to man. (4) Example of fourth: movement affects white through body, and body is general relative to white. (5) Example of fifth: laughter affects animal through man, man is specific to animal general. (6) Example of sixth: heat affects water through fire, fire and water are opposite. Note: The division of accidents by Taftazani includes the more general as essential accident, which is according to the later logicians; earlier logicians include it in accidental accidents. As mentioned in the enumeration. And the meaning of research into essential accidents is: a question arose: in the definition of subject, it is 'research into its essential accidents', what is meant by this research? The commentator clarifies: the purpose is that in usul al-fiqh, the essential accidents are made the predicates of the issues that are mentioned. Then a question: these essential accidents will be the predicates; what will be the subject of the issue? The commentator answers: regarding the subject of an issue, there are four forms: (1) sometimes the subject of the issue is the subject of the science itself, e.g., 'the Book establishes the ruling definitively' – here the establishment of ruling is from the essential accidents, and it is the predicate, while 'the Book' is the subject of the issue, and it is also the subject of the science.
