Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion. It defines three key terms: the subject (mawdu') of a science, accident ('ard), and essential accident ('ard dhati). The subject of a science is the entity whose essential accidents are studied. An accident is an attribute external to the essence but predicated on the thing. Essential accidents are those that arise from the essence, either directly (first type) or through an intermediary that is equal (second type) or more general but part of the essence (third type). The passage then classifies all accidents into six types: three essential and three accidental, based on the cause. This classification is according to later logicians, while earlier logicians considered the more general as accidental. Finally, it explains that in usul al-fiqh, the essential accidents serve as predicates of the issues, and the subject of an issue can be the subject of the science itself, among other forms.

Key Terms
موضوع العلم (mawdu' al-ilm): The thing whose essential accidents are investigated in that science.
العرض (al-'ard): Something external to the essence of a thing and predicated upon it, i.e., a state of something.
العرض الذاتی (al-'ard al-dhati): That which affects a thing due to its essence, directly or through an intermediary that is equal or more general but part of the essence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani's classification, which of the following is an example of the third type of essential accident (through a more general part)?
A) Laughter affecting man because of wonder.
B) Movement affecting man because of animal.
C) Heat affecting water because of fire.
D) Laughter affecting man directly.

Key Takeaway
An essential accident is not only a direct attribute but can also arise through an equal or a more general part of the essence; this classification is foundational for defining any science's subject.

Three Categories of Essential Attributes in Usul al-Fiqh
Parsing the three categories of essential attributes for evidence and rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify the three categories of essential attributes (awāriḍ dhātiyya) for both evidence and rulings, and determine which categories are relevant to Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Essential attributes are divided into three: those that are the subject matter, those involved in its establishment, and those irrelevant to usul.

This lesson introduces a foundational classification in Hanafi usul: the tripartite division of essential attributes (awāriḍ dhātiyya) that apply to both legal evidence (adilla) and rulings (aḥkām). Understanding this division is crucial for knowing which attributes are properly studied in usul al-fiqh and which are not. The passage uses examples and an analogy to a carpenter to clarify the boundaries of the discipline.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject matter
Recognize that the subject matter (mabḥūth ʿanhā) of usul al-fiqh is evidence and rulings. The attributes under discussion are those that inhere in these two.
2
Learn the three categories
Memorize the three categories: (1) attributes that are themselves the subject matter (predicates in universal propositions), (2) attributes that have involvement in the attachment/establishment of the subject matter, and (3) attributes that are neither.
The third category is excluded from usul.
3
Parse examples for evidence
From the passage, identify examples of each category for evidence. First category: 'establishment' (e.g., 'al-kitāb muthbit al-ḥukm'). Second: 'general', 'shared', 'solitary report'. Third: 'triliteral', 'inflected', etc.
4
Parse examples for rulings
Similarly, for rulings: first category: 'establishment of ruling'. Second: 'related to worship', 'related to mature person'. Third: 'intuitive/theoretical', 'substance/accident'.
5
Apply the carpenter analogy
Understand that just as a carpenter only considers wood properties relevant to carpentry (hardness, straightness), the usuli only considers essential attributes that are either the subject matter or involved in its establishment, ignoring irrelevant linguistic or accidental attributes.
This reinforces the exclusion of the third category.

کو مسکو عنہا کے لحوق اور ثبوت میں کوئی دخل ہو پھر کلا القسمین کی مراد کو واضح کیا کہ کلا القسمین سے مراد ادلہ کے عوارض ذاتی اور احکام کے عوارض ذاتیہ ہیں وزرا نکتہ کا بڑا مکان ہے ان عوارض ذاتیہ کی مثال بیان کی مثل کسی دلیل کا عادت ہونا قدیم ہونا، بسیرا ہونا کے مرتکب ہوٹل ریل کا جملہ اسمیہ جملہ فعلیہ ہو یا اس کے مفردات کا ثلاثی زیا گیا دیتا معرب بنی ہونا۔ الی غیر کا ایک مثلا ہوتا، را ہونا، منصرف غیر منصرف ہوتا، مرفوع ، منصوب مجرور ہونا، یہ سب ایسے عوارض ذاتیہ ہیں جن کو موت آوث مننا معینی اثبات اور ثبوت میں کوئی دخل نہیں ہے لہذا اصول فقہ میں ان کی بحث نہیں ہوگی وهذا كلما ان النجار نے ایک نظر بیان کر کے سمجھایا کو اس کی نظیر ایسی ہے جیسے پنجاد لکڑی میں انہی چیزوں پر نظر رکھتا ہے جو اس کی صناعت سے متعلق ہیں مثلا اکثریتی کا ٹھوس اور سخت ہوتا ، اس کا نرم اور پتلا ہونا ہوتا ، اس کا لیٹر بھا سیدھا ہوں لیکن وہ ان چیزوں کو مد نظر نہیں رکھتا جو اس کے تین اعصا سے ہوتا متعلق نہیں ہیں مثلا لکڑی کا ممکن ہونا، حادث ہونا ہر کب ہوتا سیاہ ہوتا ۔ اس طرح اس فن میں برگی اصولی انہی عوارض کے لیتا ہے جو اس فن سے متعلق ہیں کہ ان عوارضی کو جن کا اس فن سے کوئی تعلق نہیں ہے ۔
It is that they have considerable involvement in the attachment and establishment of the original subject matter. For example, the evidence being general, shared, a report, etc. All these are essential attributes of evidence, but they themselves are not the subject matter; rather, they have involvement in the subject matter. For example, if the evidence is general, for which ruling will it be positive? If it is shared, for which ruling will it be useful? etc. (3) (3) The third category of essential attributes: those essential attributes that are neither themselves the subject matter nor are they involved in the subject matter. For example, the evidence being eternal, temporal, etc. Meaning, the evidence being triliteral, quadriliteral, inflected, based, declinable, indeclinable, the parts of the evidence being simple, compound, etc. These are also essential attributes of evidence, but they are neither the subject matter of this discipline nor are they involved in the subject matter. So the first category: The author, detailing the essential attributes, says that among these categories, the first category occurs as predicates in universal propositions. For example, 'The Book establishes the ruling conclusively.' Here, 'establishment' is the predicate of a universal proposition, and this is the first category of essential attributes. And the second category of essential attributes: (1) Sometimes it is a description and condition of the subject of the universal proposition, such as 'The report narrated by one person gives predominant conjecture.' This is the second category of essential attributes, and it occurs as a description for the subject (report). And (2) sometimes this category itself becomes the subject of the universal proposition, e.g., 'The general necessitates the ruling conclusively.' Here, 'general' itself is the subject of the proposition, and this is the second category of essential attributes. And (3) sometimes this second category becomes the predicate of the proposition, e.g., 'The indefinite remains general.' This is from the second category of essential attributes, and here it occurs as the predicate. Then the author says that just as the essential attributes of evidence have three categories, similarly the essential attributes of rulings also have these three categories: (1) Those essential attributes that are themselves the subject matter, i.e., the discussion concerning them is the primary purpose, such as the establishment of a ruling from evidence. This establishment is an essential attribute of the ruling and is the primary purpose of the investigation. (2) The second category: those essential attributes that have involvement in the attachment of the subject matter. For example, the ruling being related to the action of a mature person, to the action of a child, etc. Meaning, the ruling being related to the action of an insane person or the action of a slave, etc. All these have involvement in the subject matter, i.e., the establishment of the ruling, because variance in them causes variance in the ruling. (3) The third category: those essential attributes that are neither themselves the subject matter nor do they have any involvement in the attachment and establishment of the subject matter. For example, the ruling being intuitive, theoretical, affirmative, negative, substance, accident, etc. The first category of essential attributes of rulings occurs as predicates in universal propositions of the discipline, e.g., 'Definitiveness is established by the Book.' The establishment is the first category of essential attributes of rulings, and it occurs as the predicate in this universal proposition. And the second category sometimes occurs as the description and condition of the subject of the universal proposition, like 'The ruling that pertains to worship is established by the solitary report that pertains to worship.' This is the second category of essential attributes of rulings, and it occurs as the description. And sometimes these essential attributes themselves become the subject of the universal proposition, like 'The punishment is not established by analogy.' The punishment is the third category of essential attributes of rulings, and it becomes the subject in this universal proposition. And sometimes these essential attributes become the predicate of the universal proposition, like 'Zakat is from worship.' 'Worship' is from the second category of essential attributes, and here it is the predicate. And what comes from the third category: the two categories are detailed. Then the author says that regarding the first two categories, some attributes become predicates, some become subjects, some become both subject and predicate, and some become descriptions and restrictions of the subject. Now concerning the third category of essential attributes, he says that the third category of essential attributes, whether pertaining to evidence or rulings, is separate from the principles of this discipline; it has no relation to usul al-fiqh. In usul al-fiqh, only the first two categories are discussed: those essential attributes that are themselves the subject matter or that have involvement in the subject matter. Those essential attributes that are neither themselves the subject matter nor have any involvement in the subject matter are outside the issues and discussions of usul al-fiqh. And the third: the purpose of the commentator is to clarify that by 'third' is meant those essential attributes that are both themselves the subject matter and have involvement in the attachment and establishment of the subject matter. Then he clarified that by 'both categories' are meant the essential attributes of evidence and the essential attributes of rulings. This is an important point. He gave examples of these essential attributes: a evidence being 'aadat', 'qadeem', 'baseera', being a nominal sentence, verbal sentence, or its parts being triliteral, quadriliteral, inflected, based, etc., being inflected, nominative, accusative, genitive. All these are essential attributes that have no involvement in the establishment and proof of the ruling; therefore, they are not discussed in usul al-fiqh. And this is like the carpenter: he focuses only on those things that pertain to his craft, like the wood being solid and hard, or soft and thin, or straight, but he does not consider things that do not pertain to his craft, like the wood's possibility, temporalness, whiteness, blackness. Similarly, in this discipline, the usuli considers only those attributes that are related to the discipline and disregards those that have no relation.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the three categories of essential attributes (عوارض ذاتیہ) as they apply to both evidence (ادلہ) and rulings (احکام) in usul al-fiqh. The author, following the classification in the Tawdih, first explains the categories for evidence. The first category consists of attributes that appear as predicates in universal propositions (قضیہ کلیہ), e.g., 'the Book is conclusive in establishing rulings.' The second category can appear in three ways: as a description of the subject (e.g., 'a solitary report gives predominant conjecture'), as the subject itself (e.g., 'the general is conclusive'), or as the predicate (e.g., 'the indefinite is general'). The same three-fold division is then applied to rulings: the first category is attributes like 'establishment' as a predicate; the second includes attributes like 'being related to worship' which can be a description, subject, or predicate; the third category includes attributes like 'the punishment' as subject. Importantly, the third category of essential attributes—those that are neither the subject matter itself nor involved in its establishment—is excluded from usul al-fiqh. Examples include whether a word is triliteral, inflected, etc. The author uses the analogy of a carpenter who only considers qualities of wood relevant to his craft, ignoring unrelated ones. Thus, the usuli scholar should only concern himself with the first two categories of essential attributes.

Key Terms
عوارض ذاتیہ (awāriḍ dhātiyya): Essential attributes that inhere in the subject matter (evidence or ruling) and are either themselves the subject of investigation or affect the attachment of the ruling.
مبحوث عنہا (mabḥūth ʿanhā): The subject matter of the discipline; in usul al-fiqh, it is the evidence (dalīl) and the ruling (ḥukm).
قضیہ کلیہ (qaḍiyya kulliyya): A universal proposition that forms the basic unit of discussion; its subject and predicate are examined in terms of essential attributes.
عرض ذاتی (ʿaraḍ dhātī): An essential attribute inherent to the subject; in this context, it is the focus of classification into three categories.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of the first category of essential attributes of evidence?
A) The evidence being general (عام)
B) The evidence being triliteral (ثلاثی)
C) The evidence establishing a ruling (اثبات الحکم)
D) The evidence being inflected (معرب)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, only essential attributes that are either the subject matter (first category) or involved in its establishment (second category) are relevant; third-category attributes like linguistic properties are excluded from discussion.

Can a Single Science Have Multiple Subjects?
Opening discussion on multiple subjects for one science

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether multiple subject matters can exist for one science and evaluate the evidence from the example of medicine.

Core Idea
One science may have multiple subjects — the example of medicine proves cohesiveness does not require singularity.

This passage from Al-Tawdih opens a new section on the philosophy of science within usul al-fiqh. The author addresses a fundamental question: can a single discipline have more than one subject matter? He aligns with the early scholars (mutaqaddimun) who affirm this possibility, contrary to the majority. We'll examine their reasoning and its implications for understanding the structure of usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
Recognize the specific question being debated: whether a single science (ʿilm) can have multiple subject matters (mawḍūʿāt).
This is a meta-discussion about the nature of sciences, not a specific fiqh ruling.
2
State the Author's Position and Evidence
The author asserts that multiple subjects are permissible, citing the early scholars. Their proof is the science of medicine: it is one science yet has many subjects (body, diseases, treatments).
The passage cuts off, but the argument is inductive: a known example establishes permissibility.
3
Analyze the Reasoning (Taʿlīl)
Examine how the example of medicine functions as a proof. It shows that a science’s unity does not depend on a single subject but on its coherent principles and methods.
This reasoning challenges the assumption that subject, object, and method must all be singular.
4
Consider Significance for Usul al-Fiqh
Reflect on what this means for usul al-fiqh: its subject matter can include multiple sources (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas) and related concepts, while remaining a unified science.
This prepares the student to view usul as a multifaceted but coherent discipline.

Source Text

متن توضیح که واعـلـم اني لـمـا وقعت في مباحث الموضوع والمسائل اردت ان اسمعك بعض مباحثها التي لا يستغنی الخ مصنف فرماتے ہیں کہ جب میں موضوع اور مسائل کے مباحث میں پڑ گیا ہوں تو میرا ارادہ یہ ہے کہ موضوع کے متعلق چند مباحث بیان کر دوں اگر چہ ان مباحث کا اس فن کے ساتھ کوئی تعلق نہیں ہے لیکن محصل اور طالب علم ان مباحث سے مستغنی نہیں ہو سکتا اس لیے افادہ محصل کیلئے بیان کر رہا ہوں مصنف نے تین مباحث ذکر کی ہیں اور تینوں جمہور علماء کے اقوال کے خلاف ہیں ( الجمث الاول) یہ ہے کہ علم واحد کیلئے متعدد موضوع ہو سکتے ہیں یا نہ مصنف فرماتے ہیں کہ علماء متقین نے یہ فرمایا. ہے کہ ایک علم کیلئے اشیاء متعددہ موضوع بن سکتی ہیں یہ جائز ہے وہ دلیل یہ بیان کرتے ہیں علم طب ایک علم ہے اس کے موضوع
Text of Tawdih: and know that when I entered into the discussions of the subject (mawdu') and the issues (masa'il), I intended to make you hear some of those discussions that are indispensable. The author says: When I became engaged in the discussions of the subject and issues, my intention is to present a few discussions concerning the subject, even though these discussions have no connection to this discipline, but the student and researcher cannot do without them, so I present them for the benefit of the student. The author mentions three issues and they are all contrary to the statements of the majority of scholars. The first issue is whether multiple subjects can exist for a single science or not. The author says that the early scholars have stated that multiple things can be subjects for one science; it is permissible. They present as a proof the science of medicine: it is one science, but its subjects...
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage begins a new section from the text of Al-Tawdih. The author explains that he will discuss three issues regarding the subject-matter (mawdu') of a science, even though they are not strictly part of usul al-fiqh, because students need them. These three issues go against the majority. The first issue is whether one science can have multiple subjects. The early scholars (mutaqaddimin) said yes, citing medicine as an example—medicine is one science but has many subjects (e.g., the body, diseases, treatments). The passage cuts off, but the author's position is clear: it is permissible for a single science to have multiple subject matters.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawḍūʿ): The subject matter of a science; that which the science investigates and about which rulings are sought.
علم (ʿilm): A science or disciplined body of knowledge.
مباحث (mabāḥith): Discussions or topics of investigation within a science.
دلیل (dalīl): A proof or evidence, used in the passage as the example of medicine.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Medicine

Key Takeaway
The early Hanafi scholars affirmed that a single science can encompass multiple subject matters, as demonstrated by the science of medicine.

The Three Scenarios of Multiplicity of Subject in a Science
First investigation on tata'addud al-mawdu'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the Hanafi conditions under which a single science can have multiple subjects, distinguishing between the positions of Sadr al-Shari'ah and Taftazani.

Core Idea
A single science can have multiple subjects only when the subject is a relation and attributes arise from both sides.

This passage from Sharh al-Tawdih addresses a foundational question in the classification of sciences: can one science have more than one subject matter? The commentator Taftazani refines the author Sadr al-Shari'ah's two-scenario model into a more precise three-scenario framework, using examples from usul al-fiqh, logic, and fiqh. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for properly defining the subject and scope of any Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central question
The first investigation asks whether a single science (ʿilm wāḥid) can have multiple subject matters (mawḍūʿāt). Clarify that this is a meta-discussion about the structure of sciences.
2
Recognize the key criterion: idāfah
The decisive factor is whether the subject of inquiry (al-mabḥūth ʿanhu) is an idāfah (relation) between two things. If it is, we need to further examine from which side the relevant attributes (ʿawāriḍ) arise.
Idāfah is a relational concept; e.g., 'proofs in relation to rulings' is the subject of uṣūl al-fiqh.
3
Compare the two analytical models
Sadr al-Shariʿah proposes two scenarios: (1) subject is idāfah → multiplicity allowed; (2) subject is not idāfah → multiplicity not allowed. Taftazani argues for three: (1) idāfah + attributes from both sides → multiplicity allowed; (2) idāfah + attributes from one side → multiplicity not allowed; (3) no idāfah → multiplicity not allowed.
The difference hinges on whether the single condition (idāfah) is sufficient or a second condition (distribution of attributes) is needed.
4
Apply the models to concrete examples
For uṣūl al-fiqh (subject: proofs and rulings), attributes arise from both sides → multiplicity. For logic (subject: delivery of conception), attributes arise only from the delivered conception → single subject. For fiqh (subject: acts of mukallaf), no idāfah → single subject.
Taftazani's analysis makes logic's subject single, while Sadr al-Shariʿah's model would make it multiple.
5
Assess the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilāf)
The disagreement affects how we classify sciences. Under Taftazani's view, logic has a single subject (the conception being delivered), while under Sadr al-Shariʿah's view it would have two subjects (the delivering and the delivered conception). This influences the definition and scope of logic as a discipline.
Both views are within the Hanafi tradition; the later preferred view is Taftazani's more nuanced one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسک الملیح ۲۴۸ شرح التوضیح بلکہ تعدد موضوع اول صورت میں جائز ہے اور ثانی صورت ( ایصال تصور ابلی تصور ) میں تعدد موضوع جائز نہیں ہے اس صورت کو مائن نے ذکر نہیں کیا کیونکہ موضوع اگر چه اضافت شئی الی شئی ہے لیکن عوارض ناشی من احد المضافین ہیں ان دونوں کو اکٹھا بیان کرنے کی وجہ صرف یہ ہے کہ دونوں کا محبوث عنها اضافت دیگی الی شتی آخر ہے ( متفکر ایھا اللعيب ) شرح تلویح قوله واعلم هذه ثلاثه مباحث غرض شارح دوسری صورت یہ ہے کہ مبحوث عنہ اضافت تو ہو لیکن وہ احوال جن کو منجوٹ عنہ میں دخل ہے وہ مضافین سے پیدا نہ ہور ہے ہوں بلکہ ایک ہی مضاف سے پیدا ہور ہے ہوں تو اس صورت میں ایک ہی موضوع ہو گا تعدد موضوع جائز نہیں ہو گا جیسے منطق کا موضوع ایصال تصور الی تصور اس میں اضافت بین شیمین تو ہے ( ایصال الی تصور آخر ) لیکن وہ عوارض جن کو مبحوث عنہ میں دخل ہے وہ صرف ایک مضاف یعنی تصور موصل سے ناشی ہیں اور سب کا رجوع اسی طرف ہے دوسرے مضاف تصور موصل الیہ سے کوئی عارض ناشی نہیں ہے جیسا کہ مصنف نے تقریر کی لہذا موضوع بھی صرف تصور موصل ہی ہوگا یہ دوسری صورت ہے جس کو مصنف نے متن میں ذکر نہیں کیا مصنف نے صرف دو صورتیں بنائی ہیں (۳) اگر مبحوث عنہ بالکل اضافت ہی نہ ہو تو بھی تعدد موضوع جائز نہیں ہوگا بلکہ موضوع واجد ہوگا جیسے فقہ کا موضوع فعل المكلف وجوب حزمت وغیرہ یہ تیسری صورت ہے اس میں بھی تعدد موضوع جائز نہیں ہے وجہ یہ ہے اختلاف اور تعدد موضوع واجب کرتا ہے اختلاف مسائیل اور تعدد معلومات کو اور تعدد معلومات لازم کرتا ہے اختلاف اور تعد علم کو کیونکہ معلومات جب متعدد ہوں گے تو لاز ما علم بھی متعدد ہوگا اور معلومات سے مراد مسائل ہی ہیں تو نتیجہ یہ نکلے گا کہ اختلاف موضوع مستلزم ہے اختلاف علم کو جب موضوع متعدد ہوگا تو علم بھی متعد ہوگا حالانکہ علم و تو واحد فرض کیا گیا تھا تو ثابت ہوا کہ جب علم واحد ہو گا تو موضوع بھی واحد ہوگا موضوع متعدد نہیں ہو سکتا تثبت المدعی ۔ فائدہ ! شارح تفتازانی کی تقریر کے مطابق تعدد موضوع و عدم تعدد کے اعتبار سے یہاں میں صورتیں بنتی ہیں جب کہ مصنف کی تقریر کے مطابق دو شقیں بنتی ہیں (۱) اگر محجوث عند اضافت بین همچین ہے تو تعدد موضوع جائز ہے خواہ عوارض ناشی من کلا المضافین ہوں یا من احد المضافین تو مصنف کی تقریر کے مطابق منطق کا موضوع ایصال بھی تعدد موضوع کی مثال ہوگا جب کہ شارح کی تقریر کے مطابق یہ تعدد موضوع کی مثال نہیں ہے بلکہ عدم تعدد کی مثال ہے (۲) دوسری شق کہ مبحوث عند اضافت نہ ہو دونوں کی تقریر میں تین فرق ہیں (۱) مصنف کی تقریر دو شقوں پر مبنی ہے تفتازانی کی علی ثلاث شقوق (۲) مصنف کی تقریر کے مطابق منطق کا موضوع بھی متعدد ہوگا مثل موضوع اصول فقه بخلاف تقریر تفتازانی کیونکہ دریں صورت منطق کا موضوع واحد ہو گا متعدد نہیں ہو گا (۳) مصنف کی تقریر کے مطابق تعدد موضوع مشروط بشرط واحد ہو گا وهو ان يكون الموضوع اضافہ بین شیمین اور تفتازانی کی تقریر کے مطابق تعدد موضوع مشروط بشرطین ہوگا (۱) اضافت (۲) ان یکون العوارض بعضها ناشی عن احد المضافين وبعضها عن الآخر
Misk al-Malih, 248. Sharh al-Tawdih. Rather, multiplicity of subject is permissible in the first scenario, and in the second scenario (isal tasawwur ila tasawwur) multiplicity of subject is not permissible. The author did not mention this scenario because although the subject is an idafah of one thing to another, the attributes arise from one of the two related things. The reason for mentioning both together is that both have as their subject of inquiry an idafah of one thing to another. (Commentary of al-Talwih) His statement: 'Know that these are three investigations.' The purpose of Sharh al-Talwih is to clarify the text. First, the commentator mentions that the author's three investigations all relate to the subject. The first investigation is about whether a single science can have multiple subjects. The second investigation concerns the aspect (haythiyyah) mentioned in the subject. The third investigation is whether a single thing can be the subject of multiple sciences. Then he says that these three investigations are the author's own investigations, which contradict those of the majority of verified scholars. Then with 'one who looks is astonished by them', the commentator says that a person familiar with the discourse of the people will be astonished if he reflects on these investigations. (Comment: As usual, the commentator Taftazani, with 'astonishment', is mocking the author Sadr al-Shari'ah. But if a fair-minded observer looks into these investigations, he will obtain wonderful and exquisite subtleties; they are very precise investigations.) 'al-Awwal' is an exposition of the first investigation. The summary of the exposition is that it is not correct to allow multiplicity of subject absolutely. Rather, the investigation shows that there are three scenarios. In one scenario, multiplicity of subject is permissible; in two scenarios, it is not. The detail: The subject of inquiry in a science is either an idafah between two things or not. If it is an idafah between two things, it has two sub-scenarios. Either the attributes (awarid) that are involved in the subject of inquiry arise partly from one related thing and partly from the other, or this is not the case; rather, all attributes arise from a single related thing. If the subject of inquiry is an idafah between two things, and some attributes arise from one related thing and some from the other, then in this scenario the subject is multiple and both related things become subjects. For example, the subject of usul al-fiqh is the establishment of proofs for rulings. This is an idafah between two things: proofs inasmuch as they are related to rulings. The states (ahwal) that are involved in establishment partly arise from the proof, such as generality, commonality, etc., and partly arise from the ruling, such as punishment, worship, etc. Therefore, here the subject is multiple and both related things (proofs and rulings) are the subject of usul al-fiqh. This is the first scenario, and multiplicity of subject is permissible. (2) The second scenario is that the subject of inquiry is an idafah, but the states involved in the subject of inquiry do not arise from both related things; rather, they arise from a single related thing. In this scenario, there is one subject; multiplicity is not permissible. For example, the subject of logic is the delivery of a conception to a conception. This is an idafah between two things (delivery to another conception), but the attributes involved in the subject of inquiry arise only from one related thing, that is, the conception that is delivered, and all return to it. The other related thing, the conception to which delivery is made, gives rise to no attribute. So the subject is only the conception being delivered. This is the second scenario, which the author did not mention in the text; the author only considered two scenarios. (3) If the subject of inquiry is not an idafah at all, then multiplicity of subject is also not permissible; rather, the subject is single. For example, the subject of fiqh is the acts of a responsible person, obligation, prohibition, etc. This is the third scenario, where multiplicity of subject is not permissible. The reason: Difference and multiplicity of subject necessitate difference of problems and multiplicity of known things (ma'lumat), and multiplicity of known things necessitates difference and multiplicity of science, because when the known things are multiple, the science will also necessarily be multiple. By 'known things' are meant the problems. Thus the conclusion follows that difference of subject entails difference of science. When the subject is multiple, the science will also be multiple, whereas the science was assumed to be one. So it is established that when the science is one, the subject is also one; the subject cannot be multiple. This proves the claim. (Benefit: According to the commentator Taftazani's exposition, three scenarios arise with regard to multiplicity or non-multiplicity of subject, whereas according to the author's exposition, two divisions arise: (1) If the subject of inquiry is an idafah between two things, then multiplicity of subject is permissible, whether the attributes arise from both related things or from one of them. So according to the author's exposition, the subject of logic (isal) is also an example of multiplicity of subject, whereas according to the commentator's exposition, it is not an example of multiplicity but of non-multiplicity. (2) The second division: if the subject of inquiry is not an idafah. There are three differences between the two expositions: (1) The author's exposition is based on two divisions; Taftazani's on three. (2) According to the author's exposition, the subject of logic is also multiple like the subject of usul al-fiqh, unlike Taftazani's exposition because in that scenario the subject of logic is single, not multiple. (3) According to the author's exposition, multiplicity of subject is conditional on one condition: that the subject be an idafah between two things; according to Taftazani's exposition, multiplicity is conditional on two conditions: (1) idafah, and (2) that some attributes arise from one related thing and some from the other.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the first investigation in usul al-fiqh regarding whether a single science can have multiple subjects. The commentator Taftazani explains that the author Sadr al-Shari'ah originally presented two scenarios, but a more precise analysis yields three. The key criterion is whether the subject of inquiry involves a relation (idafah) between two things and how the attributes relevant to the science arise. For example, in usul al-fiqh, the subject is both proofs and rulings because some attributes come from proofs and some from rulings, so the subject is multiple. In logic, although the subject involves a relation (delivery of a conception), all attributes arise only from the conception being delivered, so the subject is single. In fiqh, there is no relation between two things; the subject is simply the acts of a responsible person, so it is single. The commentator then contrasts his own three-scenario analysis with the author's two-scenario approach, highlighting differences in the number of conditions and in the classification of logic. The discussion is purely rational and methodological, using examples from Islamic sciences to illustrate the principle. The authority of the positions is not directly indicated, but the commentator's view is presented as more refined.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawḍūʿ): The subject matter of a science; that which the science studies its accidental properties (ʿawāriḍ).
اضافت (iḍāfat): A relation or attribution between two things; relational notion in the subject of a science.
عارض (ʿāriḍ (pl. ʿawāriḍ)): An attribute or state belonging to the subject of a science; the properties studied.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani's exposition, under which condition is multiplicity of subject in a single science permissible?
A) When the subject involves an idafah between two things.
B) When the subject involves an idafah and attributes arise from both related things.
C) When the subject does not involve an idafah.
D) When the subject is one and the same thing.

Key Takeaway
A single science can have multiple subjects only when its subject is a relation (idafah) and the attributes studied arise from both sides of that relation.

Defining Natural Science and Ilm al-Sama' wa al-'Alam
Defining Ilm al-Sama' and natural science

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of Ilm al-Sama' wa al-'Alam and its subject matter, and identify how it relates to natural science (al-ilm al-tabi'i).

Core Idea
Natural science studies sensible bodies in terms of change; Ilm al-Sama' is a branch of it.

In this opening passage, the author defines two foundational sciences: Ilm al-Sama' wa al-'Alam (the science of the heavens and the world) and Ilm Tabi'i (natural science). Understanding these definitions is crucial for later discussions on the classification of knowledge, which in usul al-fiqh helps delineate the scope of different evidences. We will focus on the precise subject of each science and how they relate hierarchically.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sciences being defined
Find the two sciences named at the beginning: Ilm al-Sama' wa al-'Alam and Ilm Tabi'i. Note that the first is said to be a branch of the second.
The passage also mentions Ilm al-Hay'a (astronomy) for comparison.
2
Extract the definition of Ilm al-Sama' wa al-'Alam
Locate the sentence beginning 'And the science of the heavens and the world...' and write down its definition: it is the science through which knowledge of the states of bodies is obtained, including celestial bodies, four elements, their positions, and construction.
The definition comes from Sharh Taftazani.
3
Extract the definition of natural science (al-ilm al-tabi'i)
Find the sentence 'And the definition of natural science is...' and note that it discusses states of a body in terms of change (motion and rest).
Also note that its subject is 'the sensible body inasmuch as it is subject to change'.
4
Identify the subject of each science
For Ilm al-Sama', the subject is celestial bodies and the four elements. For natural science, the subject is sensible bodies subject to change.
Both involve sensible bodies, but natural science is broader.
5
Understand the relationship
The passage states that Ilm al-Sama' is a branch of natural science, meaning it is a more specific application.
This hierarchy will be used later in debates about the unity or plurality of sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسک العلیم دیا ہے (اجسام عالم سے بسائط مراد ہیں ) اسی طرح علم السماء و العالم کا موضوع بھی اجسام عالم کو قرار دیا ہے لیکن من حیث الطبخیہ تو علم حیت اور علم السماء دونوں کا موضوع اجسام عالم ہے اور ان کے ساتھ جو حیثیت ہے وہ بیانیہ ہے اعراض ذاتیہ موثہ کے بیان کیلئے ہے یہ جز و موضوع نہیں ہے کیونکہ اگر یہ اپنے موضوع کی جزء ہوتی تو مسحوث فی العلم نہ ہوتی حالانکہ یہ مبحوث فی العلم ہوتی ہے تو یہ حیثیت موضوع سے خارج رہے گی اور دونوں علموں کا موضوع فقط اجسام عالم ہوگا اور موضوع کے اعتبار سے ان میں کوئی تمایز اور اختلاف نہیں ہوگا البتہ معمولات کی وجہ سے اختلاف ہوگا علم ھیت میں ان کے اشکال سے بحث ہوگی اور علم السماء میں طبائع سے بحث ہوگی تو ثابت ہوا کہ ان دونوں میں علمین کا تمایز و تغار محمولات مسائل کی وجہ سے ہے اگر چہ موضوع واحد ہے تو دعوی ثانی وقوع في نفس الامر بھی ثابت ہو گیا۔ وعلم السماء والعالم علم یہاں سے شارخ تفتازانی علم السماء والعالم کی تعریف کر رہے ہیں کہ علم السماء والعالم وہ علم ہے جس کے ذریعہ سے احوال اجسام کی معرفت حاصل ہوتی ہے اجسام عالم سے ارکان عالم مراد ہیں اور ارکان عالم سے مراد سموات اور جو کچھ ان میں ہے اسی طرح اجسام سے مراد عناصر اربعہ ( پانی ہوا مٹی آگ ہیں یعنی ان کی طبائع حرکات اور ان کے مواضع کہ کونسا عصر کس کرہ میں ہے ان سے بحث ہوتی ہے نیز اس علم میں اجسام کی صنعت ( بناوٹ) اوران کو تہ بتہ بالترتیب رکھنے کی حکمت معلوم ہوتی ہے دھومن اتسابا تعلم الطبعی پھر علم السماء و العالم علم طبعی کی ایک قسم ہے اور علم طبعی کی تعریف یہ ہے کہ وہ ایک علم ہے جس میں احوال جسم سے بحث ہوتی ہے من حيث التغير ( یعنی من حیث الحرکت والسکون ) اور علم طبی کا موضوع الجسم الحسوس من حیث انہ معرض للتغير ہے الخ یعنی علم طبعی کا موضوع جسم محسوس ہے اس حیثیت سے کہ اس کو تغیر عارض ہوتا ہے یا ثبات حاصل ہوتا ہے اور علم طبعی میں جسم محسوس کئے احوال کی بحث اسی حیثیت سے ہوتی ہے شیخ ابو علی نے اسی طرح ذکر کیا ہے شارح نے ثم لا یخفی سے وضاحت کی ہے کہ یہ بات بھی مخفی نہ رہے کہ علم طبعی میں جو حیثیت ہے وہ خود محوث عنھا ہوتی ہے اور اس بات کی تصریح کی گئی ہے کہ یہ حیثیت قید عروض ہے
Misk al-Malih says: (By celestial bodies are meant simple bodies). Similarly, the subject of the science of the heavens and the world has also been made the celestial bodies. But from the natural perspective, both the science of hay'a and the science of the heavens have the celestial bodies as their subject, and what accompanies them (the aspect) is explanatory, for describing essential accidents. It is not part of the subject, because if it were part of its subject, it would not be discussed in the science, whereas it is discussed in the science. Thus this aspect is external to the subject, and both sciences have only the celestial bodies as subject, and there is no distinction or difference between them in terms of subject. However, there is difference due to the predicates (mahmulat): in the science of hay'a, the shapes are discussed, and in the science of the heavens, the natures are discussed. So it is established that the distinction and difference between these two sciences is due to the predicates of the problems, even though the subject is one. Then the second claim of occurrence in reality is also proven. And the science of the heavens and the world: from here, Sharh Taftazani begins defining the science of the heavens and the world: it is the science through which knowledge of the states of bodies is obtained; by celestial bodies are meant the pillars of the world, and by pillars of the world are meant the heavens and what is in them; similarly, by bodies are meant the four elements (water, air, earth, fire), i.e., their natures, motions, and positions — which element is in which sphere. This science also discusses the construction of bodies and the wisdom of arranging them in layers. Then the science of the heavens and the world is a branch of natural science. And the definition of natural science is that it is a science that discusses the states of a body in terms of change (i.e., in terms of motion and rest). And the subject of natural science is the sensible body inasmuch as it is subject to change, etc. That is, the subject of natural science is the sensible body from the aspect that change occurs to it or stability obtains. And in natural science, the discussion of the states of sensible bodies is from this aspect. Sheikh Abu Ali has mentioned similarly. The commentator (sharh) has clarified from 'thumma la yakhfa' that it should not remain hidden that in natural science, the aspect (haythiyya) is itself what is discussed, and it is specified that this aspect is a condition for accidental attributes.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is a definitional explanation of two physical sciences: Ilm al-Hay'a (astronomy) and Ilm al-Sama' wa al-'Alam (cosmology). The author first notes that both sciences share the same subject — celestial bodies — but are distinguished by their predicates: one discusses shapes, the other natures. Then he provides the definition of Ilm al-Sama' wa al-'Alam as the science of the states of bodies, including the four elements and their positions. He further states that this science is a branch of natural science (al-ilm al-tabi'i), which is defined as the science that studies the states of sensible bodies in terms of change (motion and rest). The commentator clarifies that the 'aspect' (haythiyya) under which the subject is considered is not a separate part of the subject but rather a condition for the accidental attributes discussed in the science. This sets the stage for the subsequent dispute about whether the distinction of sciences is based on subject matters or predicates.

Key Terms
علم السماء والعالم (ilm al-sama' wa al-alam): The science that studies the states of celestial bodies and the four elements
العلم الطبيعي (al-ilm al-tabi'i): The science that studies sensible bodies in terms of change
حيثية (haythiyya): The aspect or perspective under which the subject is considered in a science

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the subject of natural science according to the passage?
A) Celestial bodies
B) Sensible bodies subject to change
C) Abstract intelligibles
D) All bodies including divine attributes

Key Takeaway
Natural science studies sensible bodies under the aspect of change; Ilm al-Sama' is a branch focusing on celestial bodies and elements.

The Role of Haythiyya and Distinction of Sciences by Predicates
Explaining distinction of sciences and nature of haythiyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the aspect (haythiyya) is not part of the subject, and how two sciences can have the same subject yet differ by their predicates.

Core Idea
Two sciences can share one subject; their distinction lies in predicates, not the subject itself.

The passage makes a subtle but important point: Ilm al-Hay'a and Ilm al-Sama' both study celestial bodies, yet they are different sciences. How is that possible? The author explains that the distinguishing factor is not the subject but the predicates (mahmulat) — what is asserted about that subject. Additionally, the 'aspect' (haythiyya) is not a part of the subject but a guideline for which accidental attributes are discussed. This lesson unpacks that logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the claim of shared subject
The passage states that both Ilm al-Hay'a and Ilm al-Sama' have the same subject: celestial bodies.
This may seem to blur the boundary between sciences.
2
Understand the role of predicates (mahmulat)
The author says the difference between the two sciences is due to the predicates: one discusses shapes (ashkal), the other discusses natures (taba'i).
Predicates are the specific problems or inquiries.
3
Analyze the argument that haythiyya is not part of the subject
If the aspect (haythiyya) were part of the subject, it would not be discussed in the science (since the subject is presupposed). But it is discussed, so it is external to the subject.
This is a logical argument from the commentator.
4
Identify the function of haythiyya
Haythiyya is a condition (qayd) for accidental attributes (a'radh dhatiyya). It explains under what perspective the subject is studied.
It is not a component of the subject but a filter.
5
Apply to a new example
Consider two sciences studying the same human body: one studies anatomy (structure), the other physiology (function). They share the subject (human body) but differ in predicates.
This is a modern analogy; the classical example uses celestial bodies.

مسک العلیم دیا ہے (اجسام عالم سے بسائط مراد ہیں ) اسی طرح علم السماء و العالم کا موضوع بھی اجسام عالم کو قرار دیا ہے لیکن من حیث الطبخیہ تو علم حیت اور علم السماء دونوں کا موضوع اجسام عالم ہے اور ان کے ساتھ جو حیثیت ہے وہ بیانیہ ہے اعراض ذاتیہ موثہ کے بیان کیلئے ہے یہ جز و موضوع نہیں ہے کیونکہ اگر یہ اپنے موضوع کی جزء ہوتی تو مسحوث فی العلم نہ ہوتی حالانکہ یہ مبحوث فی العلم ہوتی ہے تو یہ حیثیت موضوع سے خارج رہے گی اور دونوں علموں کا موضوع فقط اجسام عالم ہوگا اور موضوع کے اعتبار سے ان میں کوئی تمایز اور اختلاف نہیں ہوگا البتہ معمولات کی وجہ سے اختلاف ہوگا علم ھیت میں ان کے اشکال سے بحث ہوگی اور علم السماء میں طبائع سے بحث ہوگی تو ثابت ہوا کہ ان دونوں میں علمین کا تمایز و تغار محمولات مسائل کی وجہ سے ہے اگر چہ موضوع واحد ہے تو دعوی ثانی وقوع في نفس الامر بھی ثابت ہو گیا۔ وعلم السماء والعالم علم یہاں سے شارخ تفتازانی علم السماء والعالم کی تعریف کر رہے ہیں کہ علم السماء والعالم وہ علم ہے جس کے ذریعہ سے احوال اجسام کی معرفت حاصل ہوتی ہے اجسام عالم سے ارکان عالم مراد ہیں اور ارکان عالم سے مراد سموات اور جو کچھ ان میں ہے اسی طرح اجسام سے مراد عناصر اربعہ ( پانی ہوا مٹی آگ ہیں یعنی ان کی طبائع حرکات اور ان کے مواضع کہ کونسا عصر کس کرہ میں ہے ان سے بحث ہوتی ہے نیز اس علم میں اجسام کی صنعت ( بناوٹ) اوران کو تہ بتہ بالترتیب رکھنے کی حکمت معلوم ہوتی ہے دھومن اتسابا تعلم الطبعی پھر علم السماء و العالم علم طبعی کی ایک قسم ہے اور علم طبعی کی تعریف یہ ہے کہ وہ ایک علم ہے جس میں احوال جسم سے بحث ہوتی ہے من حيث التغير ( یعنی من حیث الحرکت والسکون ) اور علم طبی کا موضوع الجسم الحسوس من حیث انہ معرض للتغير ہے الخ یعنی علم طبعی کا موضوع جسم محسوس ہے اس حیثیت سے کہ اس کو تغیر عارض ہوتا ہے یا ثبات حاصل ہوتا ہے اور علم طبعی میں جسم محسوس کئے احوال کی بحث اسی حیثیت سے ہوتی ہے شیخ ابو علی نے اسی طرح ذکر کیا ہے شارح نے ثم لا یخفی سے وضاحت کی ہے کہ یہ بات بھی مخفی نہ رہے کہ علم طبعی میں جو حیثیت ہے وہ خود محوث عنھا ہوتی ہے اور اس بات کی تصریح کی گئی ہے کہ یہ حیثیت قید عروض ہے
Misk al-Malih says: (By celestial bodies are meant simple bodies). Similarly, the subject of the science of the heavens and the world has also been made the celestial bodies. But from the natural perspective, both the science of hay'a and the science of the heavens have the celestial bodies as their subject, and what accompanies them (the aspect) is explanatory, for describing essential accidents. It is not part of the subject, because if it were part of its subject, it would not be discussed in the science, whereas it is discussed in the science. Thus this aspect is external to the subject, and both sciences have only the celestial bodies as subject, and there is no distinction or difference between them in terms of subject. However, there is difference due to the predicates (mahmulat): in the science of hay'a, the shapes are discussed, and in the science of the heavens, the natures are discussed. So it is established that the distinction and difference between these two sciences is due to the predicates of the problems, even though the subject is one. Then the second claim of occurrence in reality is also proven. And the science of the heavens and the world: from here, Sharh Taftazani begins defining the science of the heavens and the world: it is the science through which knowledge of the states of bodies is obtained; by celestial bodies are meant the pillars of the world, and by pillars of the world are meant the heavens and what is in them; similarly, by bodies are meant the four elements (water, air, earth, fire), i.e., their natures, motions, and positions — which element is in which sphere. This science also discusses the construction of bodies and the wisdom of arranging them in layers. Then the science of the heavens and the world is a branch of natural science. And the definition of natural science is that it is a science that discusses the states of a body in terms of change (i.e., in terms of motion and rest). And the subject of natural science is the sensible body inasmuch as it is subject to change, etc. That is, the subject of natural science is the sensible body from the aspect that change occurs to it or stability obtains. And in natural science, the discussion of the states of sensible bodies is from this aspect. Sheikh Abu Ali has mentioned similarly. The commentator (sharh) has clarified from 'thumma la yakhfa' that it should not remain hidden that in natural science, the aspect (haythiyya) is itself what is discussed, and it is specified that this aspect is a condition for accidental attributes.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is a definitional explanation of two physical sciences: Ilm al-Hay'a (astronomy) and Ilm al-Sama' wa al-'Alam (cosmology). The author first notes that both sciences share the same subject — celestial bodies — but are distinguished by their predicates: one discusses shapes, the other natures. Then he provides the definition of Ilm al-Sama' wa al-'Alam as the science of the states of bodies, including the four elements and their positions. He further states that this science is a branch of natural science (al-ilm al-tabi'i), which is defined as the science that studies the states of sensible bodies in terms of change (motion and rest). The commentator clarifies that the 'aspect' (haythiyya) under which the subject is considered is not a separate part of the subject but rather a condition for the accidental attributes discussed in the science. This sets the stage for the subsequent dispute about whether the distinction of sciences is based on subject matters or predicates.

Key Terms
علم السماء والعالم (ilm al-sama' wa al-alam): The science that studies the states of celestial bodies and the four elements
العلم الطبيعي (al-ilm al-tabi'i): The science that studies sensible bodies in terms of change
حيثية (haythiyya): The aspect or perspective under which the subject is considered in a science
محمولات (mahmulat): Predicates; the properties or problems discussed in a science

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the aspect (haythiyya) not considered part of the subject of a science?
A) Because the subject is always a sensible body
B) Because if it were part of the subject, it would not be discussed in the science
C) Because the aspect is only a name
D) Because sciences never share subjects

Key Takeaway
Sciences can share a subject; their distinction comes from predicates, and the aspect (haythiyya) is a condition, not a component of the subject.

Distinguishing Sciences: Subject vs Predicate
Presenting and refuting objections on subject vs predicate as basis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on distinguishing sciences by subject matters and evaluate Taftazani's objections using the Hanafi response.

Core Idea
Sciences are distinguished by their subjects, not their predicates.

This passage presents a critical dispute in usul al-fiqh between the Hanafi school and the Mutakallimin (represented by Taftazani) over the very basis of scientific classification. Understanding this debate clarifies how Hanafi scholars organize their science and why the subject matter (mawdu') is the linchpin of the entire discipline.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on distinguishing sciences
Identify that the author (Sadr al-Shariah) asserts that sciences are distinguished solely by their subject matters (mawdu'at), not by predicates (mahmulat).
This is the foundational claim.
2
Identify Taftazani's three objections
Extract Taftazani's critiques: (1) The division of aspect (haythiyya) is invalid; (2) Subjects alone cannot determine unity/diversity; (3) Predicates are unknown, but the author's own argument undermines this.
The passage focuses on the first and second objections.
3
Analyze the Hanafi responses
Examine how the author dismisses each objection: (1) The division of haythiyya is already answered; (2) The unity of subject is primary, as shown by the practice of scholars; (3) The argument from unknown predicates is deflected by conceding that distinction by subjects is more appropriate.
The author argues predicates are always subordinate to the subject.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Recognize how this dispute affects the classification of sciences, e.g., whether Ilm al-Hay'a and Ilm al-Sama' are distinct sciences or one.
The Hanafi view leads to a more unified classification based on subject.

And here a consideration: from here, Sharh Taftazani begins a series of objections, presenting three objections. As for the first, the formulation of the first objection is that your third discussion — that a single thing can be the subject of multiple sciences — depends on there being two types of aspect (haythiyya): one that is part of the subject and another that is explanatory. But the commentator (Taftazani) says that we have already objected that this division of aspect is invalid, and in both cases the aspect is a condition for the subject. So the commentator says: 'And you have known what is in it.' But we say: 'And also you have known its answer, so reflect.' And the second: the summary of the second objection is that the author's statement — that just as the unity and diversity of sciences occurs due to the unity and diversity of the subject, so too the unity and diversity of sciences occurs due to the predicates (mahmulat) — this is false. Rather, the unity and diversity of sciences occurs only due to the diversity and unity of the subject. The verifying scholars (muhaqqiqun) of the people of wisdom, when they intended to know the states of external existents, they determined the species and genera of the realities of those external existents. Then, making those genera and species the subject, to the extent of human capacity, all their essential accidents were mentioned in that science and discussed, i.e., making them predicates and proving them for the subject. So they obtained shar'i problems, and all these problems refer to the subject and are states of the subject. So in terms of the subject, these problems are unified, even though in terms of the predicate they are different. Therefore, due to that unity of subject, that science was considered one and was separately compiled, and a distinct name was assigned to it, and permission was given to everyone that whatever states of that subject become known, they should add them to that science. Because in every science, it is considered that all the essential accidents of the subject should be discussed to the extent that human capacity can encompass. So there is no meaning... The result of the previous discussion is that the purpose of a single science is only to take one thing or several proportionate things as subject, discuss all its essential accidents, and seek other accidents and add them to it. And this is the meaning of the distinction and difference of sciences: in one science, the states of one thing (subject) are discussed, and in another science, the states and accidents of another thing. And these two things (subjects) either differ in reality (mutaghayyir bil-dhat) or have an conventional difference (taghayyur i'tibari). Then there are several forms of conventional difference: one form is that one thing is made the subject of a science absolutely, then the same thing is made the subject of another science but restricted by some condition; another form is that one thing is made the subject for one science restricted by one condition, then made the subject for another science restricted by a different condition, so that the subjects of the two sciences have conventional difference. In any case, the summary of this entire discourse is that the unity and diversity of sciences is based on the unity and diversity of the subject. And 'those states are unknown' — from here the commentator (Taftazani) states that the unity and diversity of sciences cannot be due to the diversity of predicates and essential accidents, because these essential accidents are sought in the unknown, while the subject is known and manifest, and that which is capable of distinguishing sciences is known; the unknown cannot distinguish sciences. So the subject is the criterion for distinguishing sciences; the predicate itself is unknown and incapable of distinguishing sciences. Response: all this talk of Sharh Taftazani is futile because if the commentator intends that the distinction of sciences by subject matters is more appropriate and suitable, we accept that. But it does not imply that the... of the sciences [is] by predicates.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute in Hanafi usul between the author (Sadr al-Shariah) and the commentator (Taftazani) regarding the basis for distinguishing sciences. The issue is whether sciences are distinguished by their subject matters (mawdu'at) or by their predicates (mahmulat). Taftazani raises three objections to the author's view that subject matters are the basis. First, he attacks the division of aspect (haythiyya) into being part of the subject or explanatory, arguing it is invalid. The author dismisses this, stating the answer is known. Second, Taftazani argues that the author's statement that both subject and predicates can distinguish sciences is false; only subject matters distinguish. The author counters by explaining that in practice, scholars determine genera and species as subjects, then discuss all essential accidents as predicates. The unity of subject makes the science one, regardless of the diversity of predicates. Third, Taftazani argues that predicates cannot distinguish because they are unknown initially, whereas the subject is known. The author responds that even if Taftazani concedes that distinction by subjects is more appropriate, that does not mean distinction by predicates is impossible. The author maintains that subject matters are the primary basis. This dispute has implications for classifying sciences: it determines whether two sciences that share a subject but differ in predicates (e.g., astronomy and cosmology) are considered distinct sciences or not.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawdu'): The subject matter of a science that determines its identity and unifies its problems
محمول (mahmul): The predicate or accidental attribute discussed in a science; these are the problems (masa'il) of the science
تمایز العلوم (tamayyuz al-ulum): The process of distinguishing different scientific disciplines from one another

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented in this passage, what is the primary basis for distinguishing between different sciences?
A) The predicates (mahmulat) discussed in each science
B) The subject matters (mawdu'at) of each science
C) The methodology used in each science
D) The aspects (haythiyyat) under which subjects are considered

Key Takeaway
Sciences are distinguished by their subject matters because the subject is known and establishes unity, while predicates are unknown and subordinate.

Rational Proof for Diverse Essential Attributes of the Real One
Constructs rational proof for multiple essential attributes of Wahid Haqiqi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rational proof of three invalid scenarios to establish that Allah's attributes are essential and diverse.

Core Idea
If attributes are not essential, they must inhere through a part, a separate thing, or another attribute—all impossible.

This passage presents a rational proof (dalil aqli) from classical Hanafi usul al-fiqh (and kalam) establishing that the Real One (Allah) possesses multiple diverse essential attributes (a'radh dhatiyya mutanawwi'a). The commentator defends the author of al-Tawdih against an objection by al-Taftazani, showing the proof's completeness and logical rigor. Understanding this proof strengthens one's grasp of divine attributes and the methodology of rational inference in Hanafi theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Thesis
Recognize that the author asserts multiple essential attributes (real, relational, negative) are proven for the Real One.
This is the conclusion to be proved.
2
Understand the Three Invalid Scenarios
If attributes are not essential, their inherence must be through (1) a part (impossible due to Allah's simplicity), (2) a separate thing (causing need, impossible), or (3) another attribute (leading to infinite regress, impossible).
These are the three possibilities eliminated by proof.
3
Analyze the Rebuttal to Taftazani
Taftazani objected that the author omitted the second scenario (separate thing) in the text. The commentator shows it was included via the phrase 'and if it is not by Him' (وان کان بغیره).
This illustrates how textual criticism is handled.
4
Follow the Second Part of the Proof
After establishing one essential attribute, the author argues: if the others are not essential, they fall into the same invalid possibilities, so all must be essential.
This completes the proof of diversity.
5
Extract the Logical Structure
The proof is a disjunctive syllogism: the attributes must inhere essentially or through one of three impossible ways; therefore they inhere essentially.
Recognize the structure for similar kalam arguments.

اتفتازانی صاحب کو مغالطہ ہوا ہے کیونکہ مصنف نے اس شق کو بھی ذکر کیا ہے کیونکہ وان کان بغیر ہ اس سے مراد امر مباین اور صفات دونوں ہیں یہی تو وجہ ہے کہ مصنف نے اس کو باطل کرنے کیلئے ولا نہ پلیزم الاستمال بالغیر کہا ہے کیونکہ یہ امر مباین ہی کے بطلان کی دلیل ہے نہ کہ صفات کے بطلان کی نیز یہ جواب بھی دیا گیا ہے کہ امر مبائین کے ذکر کرنے کی اور اس کو باطل کرنے کی ضرورت نہیں ہے کیونکہ عرض ذاتی سے وہ عرض ذاتی مراد ہے جو محمول ہو سکے چونکہ امر مباین خود محمول نہیں ہوسکتا تو یہ دوسرے عرض ذاتی کے حمل کیلئے واسطہ بھی نہیں بن سکے گا (۳) تیسری صورت یہ ہے کہ ان صفات میں سے بعض کا ثبوت و عروض للواحد احقیقی دوسری بعض صفات کے واسطہ سے ہو ہر صفت کا عروض اور لحوق دوسری صفت کے واسطہ سے ہو تو یہ بھی باطل ہے کیونکہ اس صورت میں تسلسل فی المبادی لازم آئے گا مبادی سے صفات مراد ہیں تو ہر صفت دوسرے کیلئے میدا اور موقوف علیہ بن جائے گی تو تسلسل فی المبادی لازم آئے گا اور یہ باطل ہے اور اس کے بطلان کی دلیل عقائد و علم الکلام میں مذکور ہے لہذا تسلیم کرنا ہو گا کہ بعض صفات کا عروض و ثبوت لذاتہ ہے اس سے ایک عرض ذاتی ثابت ہو گیا ہم کہتے ہیں کہ اب دوسری صفات کا ذاتی ہونا بھی تسلیم کر لو اگر تسلیم نہیں کرتے تو ہم دلیل بیان کرتے ہیں وہ یہ ہے کہ اگر دوسری صفات کا عروض لذاتہ ہے تو ہمارامدگی ثابت ہو گیا یعنی اعراض ذاتیہ کا تنوع اگر لذاتہ نہیں ہے تو پھر لجزہ ہوگا یہ بھی باطل ہے یا لغیرہ ہوگا اگر غیر سے امر منفصل مراد ہے وہ بھی باطل ہے فقد مروجہ بطلانہ اگر غیر سے مراد صفة من صفاتہ ہے کہ ہر صفت کا لحوق بواسطہ صفت آخر ہو تو تسلسل فی المبادی لازم آئے گا لہذا آپ کو تسلیم کرنا پڑے گا کہ کہیں جا کر کسی صفت کا عروض والحوق لذاتہ ہو گا تو اس سے ہمارا مطلوب ثابت ہوگا یعنی اعراض ذاتیہ متنوعہ ثابت ہو جائیں گے۔
His statement: 'And we only said...' — the purpose of the commentator of al-Tawdih is that the author presented this inference regarding diverse essential attributes: that the Real One, whose essence cannot have multiplicity and plurality from any aspect or cause, is described with multiple attributes. Some of these are real attributes like power, some are relational like creation, some are negative like devoid of matter. When it is established that the Real One is described with multiple attributes, we can say that diverse essential attributes are established for the Real One because these multiple attributes are essential attributes; there is no difference among them. That they are of different types was apparent because real attributes are a separate type, relational attributes are a separate type. However, their being essential was subject to consideration. To prove that, he presented a proof: if these attributes are not essentially established and concurrent for the Real One, then there are three scenarios: (1) Their establishment and occurrence is due to a part—this is invalid because the Real One has no part; He is simple mentally and externally. (2) Their occurrence and connection is through a separate thing—this is also invalid because in that case the Real One would be in need of another thing with respect to attributes, which entails deficiency. Allah is free from deficiency. There was an objection by al-Taftazani that the author did not mention this option in the text. The response: al-Taftazani misunderstood because the author did mention it—'And if it is not by Him' refers to both a distinct thing and attributes. That is why the author said 'and it does not necessitate isti'mal bil-ghayr' to invalidate it, because that is proof of invalidity of the distinct thing, not attributes. Also it is answered that it was not necessary to mention and invalidate the distinct thing because the essential attribute meant is that which can be predicated; the distinct thing cannot be predicated itself, so it cannot serve as a medium for another essential attribute. (3) The third scenario: Some of these attributes are established for the Real One by means of others, each attribute occurring through another—this is also invalid because it would lead to a regress in principles. 'Principles' refers to attributes; each attribute would become a principle and dependent, leading to regress. Its invalidity is established in creedal works. Hence it must be accepted that some attributes have occurrence and establishment essentially. Thus one essential attribute is proven. We say: Now accept that the other attributes are also essential. If you do not accept, we present proof: if the occurrence of other attributes is essentially, our goal is achieved; if not, then through a part—invalid; or through something else. If by 'something else' is meant a separate thing, that is also invalid as its invalidity has passed. If by 'something else' is meant one of His attributes, that each attribute occurs through another, then regress follows. Therefore you must accept that eventually some attribute occurs essentially, and then our desired result is established, i.e., diverse essential attributes are proven.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof (dalil aqli) for the existence of multiple diverse essential attributes (a'radh dhatiyya mutanawwi'a) for the Real One (al-Wahid al-Haqiqi), i.e., Allah. The author, commenting on al-Tawdih, argues that since Allah has multiple attributes (power, creation, etc.), these must be essential; otherwise, their inherence would be through a part (impossible), through a separate thing (causing need, impossible), or through another attribute (leading to infinite regress, impossible). Therefore, at least one attribute is essential. Then, using a similar argument, he proves that all attributes must be essential because if any were non-essential, they would again fall into the same invalid possibilities. The commentator defends the author against Taftazani's criticism by showing that the author did cover the case of a separate thing. The proof hinges on the impossibility of infinite regress (tasalsul) in principles (mabadi).

Key Terms
الواحد الحقیقی (al-Wahid al-Haqiqi): The Real One, whose essence has no multiplicity or composition, i.e., Allah
عرض ذاتی (arad dhati): An essential accident that inheres in a subject without a separate cause
تسلسل (tasalsul): Infinite regress of causes or principles, considered logically impossible

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the third scenario (inherence through another attribute) invalid?
A) It implies the Real One has parts
B) It leads to an infinite regress of principles (attributes)
C) It makes the Real One dependent on a separate thing
D) It contradicts the unity of the divine essence

Key Takeaway
The rational proof eliminates all impossible mediums for divine attributes, proving they must be essential and diverse.

Objection and Response on Diversity of Essential Attributes
Responds to objection: only one essential attribute proven, not diversity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the objector's challenge and apply the two Hanafi rules to derive both multiplicity and diversity of essential attributes.

Core Idea
Multiple attributes in one subject are necessarily diverse in type.

This passage presents a dialectical exchange: the objector concedes one essential attribute but denies that additional attributes become essential, arguing only multiplicity (not diversity) is proven. The Hanafi response deploys two rules: (1) an attribute inhering through an essential attribute itself becomes essential, and (2) multiple attributes subsisting in one subject are diverse in type. Together these rules establish both multiplicity and typological diversity of essential attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
The objector accepts one essential attribute but claims all other attributes inhere through it without becoming essential themselves. This avoids regress but denies diversity.
The objector is hypothetical, representing a kalam disputant.
2
Locate the First Rule
The responder states: 'an attribute that occurs through an essential attribute becomes essential.' This rule converts medium attributes into essential ones, establishing multiplicity.
Rule applied: جو صفت عرض ذاتی کے واسطہ سے عارض ہو وہ بھی عرض ذاتی بن جاتی ہے
3
Locate the Second Rule
The responder then invokes a second rule: 'multiple attributes subsisting in one subject are diverse in type.' This rule transforms numerical multiplicity into typological diversity (tanawwu').
Rule applied: متعدد صفات جو ایک محل میں قائم ہوں وہ متنوعہ ہوتی ہیں
4
Synthesize the Conclusion
By applying both rules, the responder proves that the essential attributes are multiple and of different types (real, relational, negative). Thus the claim of diversity is proven.
The thamarah al-khilaf: affirming multiple diverse divine attributes in theology.

میں اختلاف نوعی ہوا کرتا ہے وہ مختلف انواع ہوتی ہیں یہاں چونکہ تمام صفات متعددہ ایک ہی محل ( واحد حقیقی کے ساتھ قائم ہیں لہذا ان کا تعدد نوعی ہو گا مثلا صفات حقیقیہ ایک نوع ہوگی صفات اضافیہ دوسری نوع ہوں گی صفات اینجا یہ تیسری نوع ہونگی تو اعراض ذاتی کا تنوع ثابت ہو گیا یہ اختلاف اور تعد شخص نہیں ہے
Then it is said: 'It is possible that it ends...' — the purpose of the commentator al-Taftazani is answering a question. The question: The manner in which you established diverse essential attributes we do not accept. Rather we only accept proof of one essential attribute, no more. That is: When you initially said that one scenario is that some attributes are established through other attributes, and in that case regress follows, so somewhere we must accept that an attribute's occurrence is essential, thus one essential attribute is proven and we accept that. But then your saying about other attributes that they too must be accepted as essential, otherwise regress follows—we do not accept that. We say: We do not accept the other attributes as essential, and the regress objection does not necessarily arise. Because once one essential attribute is established, we say that all remaining attributes occur through that essential attribute. Hence regress does not occur because ultimately their occurrence ends at that one essential attribute. Thus your claim that the Real One has diverse attributes (different types) is not proven; only one type is proven. And if it is granted that attributes are multiple, some essential, some through medium, still the claim is not proven because the claim was about diversity (tanawwu'), and only multiplicity (ta'addud) is proven. We respond (qulna): Our claim is fully proven; multiplicity and diversity are both proven. When you say all remaining attributes occur through that essential attribute, we say those remaining attributes become essential themselves because the rule is: an attribute that occurs through an essential attribute becomes essential. Thus multiplicity is established. Also diversity is established because when multiplicity of attributes is proven, diversity necessarily follows because the rule is: multiple attributes subsisting in one subject are diverse in type. For example, real attributes are one type, relational attributes another, negative attributes another. So diversity is proven; this is not personal difference.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and response regarding the proof of diverse essential attributes. The objector (likely Taftazani or a hypothetical opponent) concedes that one essential attribute is proven but argues that the remaining attributes can inhere through that essential attribute without becoming essential themselves, thus only one type of attribute is established, not diversity. The response uses two rules: (1) An attribute that inheres through an essential attribute itself becomes essential, establishing multiplicity. (2) Multiple attributes in a single subject necessarily differ in type (diversity), as shown by the examples of real, relational, and negative attributes. Therefore, both multiplicity and diversity of essential attributes are proven. The commentator emphasizes that the diversity is not merely numerical but typological (ikhtilaf naw'i).

Key Terms
تنوع (tanawwu'): Diversity of type (naw'i) among attributes, not mere numerical multiplicity
ضابطہ (dabita): A rule or principle used to derive the conclusion that multiple attributes in one subject are diverse

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the responder, what rule makes remaining attributes essential?
A) An attribute that inheres through an essential attribute becomes essential
B) All attributes are essential by nature
C) The Real One cannot have accidental attributes
D) Regress proves essentiality

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position on divine attributes uses two rules to establish both multiplicity and diversity: medium attributes become essential, and multiple attributes in one subject are diverse.

Rational Proof that Divine Attributes are Inherent
Rational proof for inherent divine attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rational argument (dalil aqli) to demonstrate that divine attributes are inherent (bil-dhat) and not derived from any external cause.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are multiple individuals of one type, inherently subsisting in the essence — external dependence would entail deficiency or infinite regression.

This lesson explores a rational proof used in Hanafi usul al-fiqh to establish that the divine attributes (sifat) are inherent in the essence of Allah (bil-dhat), not derived from any external source. The argument uses the analogy of whiteness in different loci to show that attributes can be multiple individuals of a single type. It then refutes the possibility of external derivation (isti'mal bil-ghayr) by demonstrating that it leads to either infinite regression (tasalsul) or deficiency in the divine essence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the type-individual relationship
Distinguish between a type (naw') and its individuals (afrad). Multiplicity in individuals of one type arises from differences in the locus (mahall). For example, whiteness in cloth and whiteness in a wall are different individuals of the type 'whiteness'.
This builds the foundation for treating attributes as individuals of a single type.
2
Apply the analogy to divine attributes
The divine attributes (e.g., knowledge, power) are like individual instances of a single type, with the divine essence as their locus. Their multiplicity does not imply composition (tarkib) in the essence.
3
Analyze the impossibility of external derivation (isti'mal bil-ghayr)
If attributes derived from something external (ghayr), two scenarios arise: (a) the 'ghayr' is itself another attribute → leads to infinite regression (tasalsul); (b) the 'ghayr' is a separate, non-attribute entity → implies deficiency in the essence (since it depends on something else). Both are invalid for the True One (al-wahid al-haqiqi).
This is the core rational proof.
4
Address the objection regarding inter-attribute dependence
An objector argues: some attributes (e.g., creation) depend on others (power, knowledge) without causing deficiency. The response clarifies that this dependence is intrinsic, not external derivation. The term 'ghayr' in the proof specifically means a separate entity (amr munfasil), not another attribute. When 'ghayr' refers to another attribute, the argument shifts to tasalsul.
This resolves the apparent counterexample.
5
Conclude the inherent nature of attributes
Since external derivation is impossible and intrinsic coherence avoids regression, the attributes must subsist inherently (bil-dhat) in the divine essence. This upholds both multiplicity and unity.

بالطیر سے شق ثانی کی تردید ہے اور اس کا عطف شق اول پر ہو رہا ہے حاصل عبارت اس طرح ہو جائے گا۔ وان کان لغیرہ فھو باطل لانہ يستلزم التسلسل ولانه يعلوم الاستكمال من غیرہ اس کے بعد شارح نے متن کی وضاحت کی ہے کہ واحد حقیقی کیلئے استعمال بالغیر محال ہے کیونکہ اس میں ذات باری تعالی میں نقص لازم آتا ہے اور اس کے کمالات میں احتیاج الی الخیر لازم آتا ہے جو کہ باطل ہے وفیہ نظر سے اشکال و جواب ہے، اشکال یہ ہوتا ہے کہ آپ نے یہ کہا کہ واحد حقیقی میں استعمال من الغیر محال ہے اس غیر سے آپ کی کیا مراد ہے اگر غیر سے امر منفصل مراد ہو تو یہ لازم نہیں ہے کیونکہ ضروری تو نہیں ہے کہ وہ غیر امر منفصل ہی ہو بلکہ ہر دو صورت میں استعمال بالخیر لازم آتا ہے تو پھر ہم یہ عرض کرتے ہیں کہ اس صورت میں یہ کہنا کہ استعمال بالخیر موجب نقصان فی ذاتہ تعالی ہے درست نہیں ہے کیونکہ بعض صفات کا احتیاج الی البعض ہوتا رہتا ہے اور اس کی وجہ سے ذات باری تعالی میں کوئی نقص لازم نہیں آتا مثلا خلق صفت باری تعالی یحتاج اور موقوف ہے قدرت پر علم پر ارادہ پر اس کے باوجود صفت کا ملہ ہے اس میں کسی قسم کا نقص نہیں ہے ويمكن سے جواب دے
